E3. Does becoming self-aware mean becoming a stranger to oneself? 

E3.1. The Ecstasy of "Unselfing": Dissolving the Self in Beauty and the Sublime.
Awareness, particularly through art or nature makes us a stranger to our "brooding self with its hurt vanity" by dissolving it into a greater whole.

E3.1.1. The Burden of the "I": Escaping the Prison of Narcissism through Beauty.
The problem of the "brooding self" and  how vulnerability, art, and beauty act as the necessary mechanisms to shatter the armor of the ego.

E3.1.1.a. The Fortress of the Self vs. The Citizenship of the Universe.
The "brooding self" as a form of confinement (narcissism) and the  necessity of expanding one’s interest to the "Not-Self" to achieve true freedom and justice.

E3.1.1.a_REF1_PH P_Picture on the wall of Freud´s office in his house in London

"Beauty is the form under which the intellect prefers to study the world. All privilege is that of beauty; for there are many beauties; as, of general nature, of the human face and form, of manners, of brain, or method, moral beauty, or beauty of the soul. […]

The question of Beauty takes us out of surfaces, to thinking of the foundations of things. Goethe said, “The beautiful is a manifestation of secret laws of Nature, which, but for this appearance, had been forever concealed from us.” And the working of this deep instinct makes all the excitement — much of it superficial and absurd enough — about works of art, which leads armies of vain travelers every year to Italy, Greece, and Egypt. Every man values every acquisition he makes in the science of beauty, above his possessions. The most useful man in the most useful world, so long as only commodity was served, would remain unsatisfied. But, as fast as he sees beauty, life acquires a very high value. [...]

We ascribe beauty to that which is simple; which has no superfluous parts; which exactly answers its end; which stands related to all things; which is the mean of many extremes. It is the most enduring quality, and the most ascending quality. [...]

Things are pretty, graceful, rich, elegant, handsome, but, until they speak to the imagination, not yet beautiful. This is the reason why beauty is still escaping out of all analysis. It is not yet possessed, it cannot be handled… It is properly not in the form, but in the mind. It instantly deserts possession, and flies to an object in the horizon. If I could put my hand on the north star, would it be as beautiful? The sea is lovely, but when we bathe in it, the beauty forsakes all the near water. For the imagination and senses cannot be gratified at the same time. [...]

The new virtue which constitutes a thing beautiful, is a certain cosmical quality, or, a power to suggest relation to the whole world, and so lift the object out of a pitiful individuality. Every natural feature — sea, sky, rainbow, flowers, musical tone — has in it somewhat which is not private, but universal, speaks of that central benefit which is the soul of Nature, and thereby is beautiful. [...]

All high beauty has a moral element in it… Gross and obscure natures, however decorated, seem impure shambles; but character gives splendor to youth, and awe to wrinkled skin and gray hairs. An adorer of truth we cannot choose but obey, and the woman who has shared with us the moral sentiment — her locks must appear to us sublime. Thus there is a climbing scale of culture, from the first agreeable sensation which a sparkling gem or a scarlet stain affords the eye, up through fair outlines and details of the landscape, features of the human face and form, signs and tokens of thought and character in manners, up to the ineffable mysteries of the intellect."
(Ralph Waldo Emerson, Essays and Lectures, Beauty)

Read it in the Marginalian: https://www.themarginalian.org/2015/06/26/emerson-beauty/?mc_cid=0dbdbd1e47&mc_eid=0b6483decb

"Beauty is the convenient and traditional name of something which art and nature share, and which gives a fairly clear sense to the idea of quality of experience and change of consciousness. I am looking out of my window in an anxious and resentful state of mind, oblivious of my surroundings, brooding perhaps on some damage done to my prestige. Then suddenly I observe a hovering kestrel. In a moment everything is altered. The brooding self with its hurt vanity has disappeared. There is nothing now but kestrel. And when I return to thinking of the other matter it seems less important. And of course this is something which we may also do deliberately: give attention to nature in order to clear our minds of selfish care."
(Murdoch, Sovereignty of good, 1970)

E1.6.C.1.7.b_REF1_LIT.wE3.1.3.

E3.1.1.a_REF2_PH BERTRAND RUSSELL ARGUES THAT THE CHIEF VALUE OF PHILOSOPHY LIES IN LIBERATING THE MIND FROM THE "PRISON" OF NARROW PERSONAL INTERESTS THROUGH THE IMPARTIAL CONTEMPLATION OF THE VAST "NOT-SELF," to achieve a "citizenship of the universe" and a supreme union with the infinite.
"Apart from its utility in showing unsuspected possibilities, philosophy has a value—perhaps its chief value—through the greatness of the objects which it contemplates, and the freedom from narrow and personal aims resulting from this contemplation. The life of the instinctive man is shut up within the circle of his private interests : family and friends may be included, but the outer world is not regarded except as it may help or hinder what comes within the circle of instinctive wishes. In such a life there is something feverish and confined, in comparison with which the philosophic life is calm and free. The private world of instinctive interests is a small one, set in the midst of a great and powerful world which must sooner or later, lay our private world in ruins. Unless we can so enlarge our interests as to include the whole outer world, we remain like a garrison in a beleaguered fortress, knowing that the enemy prevents escape and that ultimate surrender is inevitable. In such a life there is no peace, but a constant strife between the insistence of desire and the powerlessness of will. In one way or another, if our life is to be great and free, we must escape this prison and this strife.

         One way of escape is by philosophic contemplation. Philosophic contemplation does not, in its widest survey, divide the universe into two hostile camps— friends and foes, helpful and hostile, good and bad— it views the whole impartially. Philosophic contemplation, when it is unalloyed, does not aim at proving that the rest of the universe is akin to man. All acquisition of knowledge is an enlargement of the Self, but this enlargement is best attained when it is not directly sought. It is obtained when the desire for knowledge is alone operative, by a study which does not wish in advance that its objects should have this or that character, but adapts the Self to the characters which it finds in its objects. This enlargement of Self is not obtained when, taking the Self as it is, we try to show that the world is so similar to this Self that knowledge of it is possible without any admission of what seems alien. The desire to prove this is a form of self-assertion and, like all self-assertion, it is an obstacle to the growth of Self which it desires, and of which the Self knows that it is capable. Self-assertion, in philosophic speculation as elsewhere, views the world as a means to its own ends ; thus it makes the world of less account than Self, and the Self sets bounds to the greatness of its goods. In contemplation, on the contrary, we start from the not-Self, and through its greatness the boundaries of Self are enlarged ; through the infinity of the universe the mind which contemplates it achieves some share in infinity. [...]

         The true philosophic contemplation, [...], finds its satisfaction in every enlargement of the not-Self, in everything that magnifies the objects contemplated, and thereby the subject contemplating. Everything, in contemplation that is personal or private, everything that depends upon habit, self-interest, or desire, distorts the object, and hence impairs the union which the intellect seeks. By thus making barrier between subject and object, such personal and private things become a prison to the intellect. The free intellect will see as God might see, without a here and now, without hopes and fears, without the trammels of customary beliefs and traditional prejudices, calmly, dispassionately, in the sole and exclusive desire of knowledge—knowledge as impersonal, as purely contemplative, as it is possible for man to attain. Hence also the free intellect will value more the abstract and universal knowledge into which the accidents of private history do not enter, than the knowledge brought by the senses, and dependent, as such knowledge must be, upon an exclusive and personal point of view and a body whose sense-organs distort as much as they reveal.

         The mind which has become accustomed to the freedom and impartiality of philosophic contemplation will preserve something of the same freedom and impartiality in the world of action and emotion. It will view its purposes and desires as parts of the whole, with the absence of insistence that results from seeing them as infinitesimal fragments in a world of which all the rest is unaffected by any one man’s deeds. The impartiality which, in contemplation, is the unalloyed desire for truth, is the very same quality of mind which, in action, is justice, and in emotion is that universal love which can be given to all, and not only to those who are judged useful or admirable. Thus contemplation enlarges not only the objects of our thoughts, but also it makes us citizens of the universe, not only of one walled city at war with all the rest. In this citizenship of the universe consists man’s true freedom, and his liberation from the thraldom of narrow hopes and fears.

         Thus, to sum up our discussion of the value of philosophy ; Philosophy is to be studied, not for the sake of any definite answers to its questions, since no definite answers can, as a rule, be known to be true, but rather for the sake of the questions themselves ; because these questions enlarge our conception of what is possible, enrich our intellectual imagination and diminish the dogmatic assurance which closes the mind against speculation ; but above all because, through the greatness of the universe which philosophy contemplates, the mind also is rendered great, and becomes capable of that union with the universe which constitutes its highest good."
(Bertrand Russell, The Problems of Philosophy, 1912)

E3.1.1.a_REF3_PH THE GOAL OF WELL-BEING IS "BREAKING THROUGH ONE’S NARCISSISM."
"If “well-being” — [defined as] functioning well as a person, not as an instrument — is the supreme goal of one’s efforts, two specific ways stand out that lead to the attainment of this goal: Breaking through one’s narcissism and breaking through the property structure of one’s existence [...]
Narcissism is an orientation in which all one’s interest and passion are directed to one’s own person: one’s body, mind, feelings, interests… For the narcissistic person, only he and what concerns him are fully real; what is outside, what concerns others, is real only in a superficial sense of perception; that is to say, it is real for one’s senses and for one’s intellect. But it is not real in a deeper sense, for our feeling or understanding. He is, in fact, aware only of what is outside, inasmuch as it affects him. Hence, he has no love, no compassion, no rational, objective judgment. The narcissistic person has built an invisible wall around himself. He is everything, the world is nothing. Or rather: He is the world.."
(Erich Fromm, The Art of Being)

E3.1.1.a_REF3.1_PH DISSOLVING THE "BROODING SELF"
"Narcissism is an orientation in which all one’s interest and passion are directed to one’s own person: one’s body, mind, feelings, interests… For the narcissistic person, only he and what concerns him are fully real; what is outside, what concerns others, is real only in a superficial sense of perception; that is to say, it is real for one’s senses and for one’s intellect. But it is not real in a deeper sense, for our feeling or understanding. He is, in fact, aware only of what is outside, inasmuch as it affects him. Hence, he has no love, no compassion, no rational, objective judgment. The narcissistic person has built an invisible wall around himself. He is everything, the world is nothing. Or rather: He is the world.Fromm writes:
"A person living in this mode is not necessarily very narcissistic. He may have broken through the shell of his narcissism, have an adequate appreciation of reality outside himself, not necessarily be “in love with himself”; he knows who he is and who the others are, and can well distinguish between subjective experience and reality. Nevertheless, he wants everything for himself; has no pleasure in giving, in sharing, in solidarity, in cooperation, in love. He is a closed fortress, suspicious of others, eager to take and most reluctant to give."
(Maria Popova´s review of Erich Fromm's The Art of Being in The Marginalian)

"If a person has the will and the determination to loosen the bars of his prison of narcissism and selfishness, when he has the courage to tolerate the intermittent anxiety, he experiences the first glimpses of joy and strength that he sometimes attains. And only then a decisive new factor enters into the dynamics of the process. This new experience becomes the decisive motivation for going ahead and following the path he has charted… [An] experience of well-being — fleeting and small as it may be — … becomes the most powerful motivation for further progress…
Awareness, will, practice, tolerance of fear and of new experience, they are all necessary if transformation of the individual is to succeed. At a certain point the energy and direction of inner forces have changed to the point where an individual’s sense of identity has changed, too. In the property mode of existence the motto is: “I am what I have.” After the breakthrough it is “I am what I do” (in the sense of unalienated activity); or simply, “I am what I am.”"
(Erich Fromm, The Art of Being)

Read it in The Marginalian: https://www.themarginalian.org/2016/03/23/erich-fromm-the-art-of-living/?mc_cid=af79031373&mc_eid=0b6483decb

E3.1.1.a_REF4_PH NICK CAVE REFRAMES "SELF-DOUBT" NOT AS A WEAKNESS, BUT AS A "HUMANISING FORCE" THAT KEEPS US "HUMBLE, VULNERABLE, OPEN-MINDED AND CONNECTED TO THE WORLD."
"Do you ever suffer from self-doubt?

A girl was falling in love with me, and while she courted me, she shared a part of herself which was you. I had never heard your music before, and it struck me that you were like me - you and I were born within a 100km radius of one another. The young girl who loved me shared with me friendship, forgiveness, and a figure of alien greatness who at one time came from some place around here.

I now have two young sons Max and Joey. They enjoy listening to your music, they dance around the living room. Joey is an emotional lightning rod who feels the world more intensely than anyone I have ever met. Little Max cannot speak yet, but he sings, and when he smiles you can see clear through into his soul. One day when they are old enough to really understand, I will tell them that you came from here. I will tell them that they can aspire to be more than a person from a place, and maybe they will not end up like their father.
THOMAS, MELBOURNE, AUSTRALIA

Dear Thomas,

You sent this letter some time ago and I keep coming back to it, responding to its wonderfully precise and tender heart. I’ve read it to several people, including film director and friend, Andrew Dominik, who basically cried, as did my normally steely assistant, Rachel. It reads like a Raymond Carver story — lean and lovely and sad — a world in every line. I have received and read around forty thousand letters on The Red Hand Files and yours is perhaps my favourite. I am very happy that this letter was written by a guy from my hometown of Melbourne — that makes me proud.

Do I ever feel self-doubt? The answer is yes, very much so. My self-doubt tends to manifest itself in sudden, hot rushes of embarrassment, and usually emerges alongside a loss of playfulness or humour — or rather, when I start to take myself, and indeed life, too seriously. It seems to arise as a countering response to my own sense of self-importance. Self-doubt is common to us all, I suspect, and uncomfortable and unpleasant as it is, a certain amount can be the truest humanising force, keeping us humble, vulnerable, open-minded and connected to the world. But, of course, we need to remain vigilant and not allow our self-doubt to become compulsive — to imprison us, shut us down and make us incapable of forward movement. At these times, it is worth remembering that the world can be funny — as can we — and to not take ourselves too seriously.

But, Thomas, the main reason I am writing to you is to just say thank you for your letter, it is a superb thing, really, and something I will treasure. When I read it to Susie this morning she said, ‘What a lovely man.’ ‘Yeah, I know,’ I said ‘He’s from Melbourne.’

Love, Nick"
(Nick Cave, The Red Hand Files, ISSUE #143 / APRIL 2021)

E3.1.1.a_REF5_PH A_Alexandra Bircken_Valet Stand_2017 /Alexandra Bircken/ ; I_Snoopy_2014

UNSELFING IS THE ACT OF DROPPING THE ARMOR TO RECOGNIZE THE "WOUNDED INNER WORLD" SHARED BY ALL.
"[...] What is virtually impossible to see within a mirror is that the very essence of our humanness, our vulnerability and fragility, is the most beautiful thing we possess.
Yet, when we are young that vulnerability can appear to us as shame or weakness, as we attempt to brace ourselves against what we may see as a brutal, unforgiving and judgemental world. But those who have no awareness of their own fragility, who present themselves as overconfident, armoured-up and invulnerable, sacrifice the essence of what makes them both human and beautiful.
Vulnerability is the very thing that permits us to connect with each other, to recognise in others the same discomfort they have with themselves and with their place in the world. Vulnerability is the engine of compassion, and can be a superpower, a special vision that allows us to see the quivering, wounded inner world that most of us possess. [...]
With love, Nick"
(Nick Cave, The Red Hand Files, ISSUE #65 / OCTOBER 2019)

E3.1.1.a_REF6_PH  EGOTISM IS HEALTHY, ON THE CONDITION OF GETTING OUT OF IT.
“EGOTISM 
is not the cult of the self. For Stendhal, who popularized the word, it is rather its benevolent and lucid study, its analysis, its deepening, its perfection—not its cult, but its culture. A necessary and healthy moment, provided one gets out of it.”
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical dictionary, 2001)

E3.1.1.b_REF1_LIT  P_Alen Fayden ; P_Jamie Hall ; P_Marwell wildlife

"One thing is just the way in which beauty … does lead people I think to be concerned with justice. Beauty brings about what Iris Murdoch called “a nonselfing.” She said that when you suddenly see something beautiful — her example was suddenly seeing a bird lift off — it brings about a nonselfing. You can see beauty pressing us towards justice. There are certain attributes that beautiful things have. Some people would say symmetry. Any definition of justice always involves at its heart some idea of balance or symmetry. Even if you look back over lots of philosophers who are talking about forms of justice, they always have this idea, say, equal pay for equal work, that’s a symmetry. [...]
But sometimes people will say to me, well, first of all that they believe that it’s right, that the whole unselfing part is right, but they don’t believe in symmetry, and I really do believe in it because — and I think part of the reason why in this country we don’t like to talk anymore about symmetry in art or in justice is because we’re so asymmetrical, with so much money and so many weapons and, you know … if we had to start saying the heart of beauty is symmetry everybody would have to say, « gee, you know, we’ve got a big problem ».”
(Anna Deavere Smith and Sarah Lewis on beauty)

Read it in The Marginalian: https://www.themarginalian.org/2018/07/17/oliver-sacks-beauty-deep-time/?mc_cid=0dbdbd1e47&mc_eid=0b6483decb

E3.1.1.b_REF2_PH A_Wenshu Manjusri Goddess ; A_Jesus with sword coming out of his mouth, from an altar in Hildesheim, Germany_circa1430

“For James Joyce, an epiphany is an aesthetic experience too great for the beholder to bear. It exceeds the boundaries of the normal—it has no practical application. It is an experience of the sublime that opens and expands the boundaries of your ego. These experiences can be of beauty but also of awe. Epiphanies go beyond ethical judgments—the sublime can be terrible. The terror is only felt if you are clinging to your ego. When you open up and embrace and affirm reality, there is no more good and bad or beauty and horror. The passing of time conceals an eternal principle of truth, which is reality in the present. The epiphany breaks the spell of time and gives us a realization of eternity. "We feel and experience that we are eternal" (Spinoza). Religions use the imagery of a god coming at you with a sword to break the ego and shake you into the realization of eternity (Jesus with a sword coming out of his mouth, the Wenshu Manjusri Goddess waving a sword over his head)—a means to open up our ego. The experience of eternity can't be expressed in language, only in poetry. Poetry, by its rhythm and choice of words, has the power to provoke epiphanies.”
(Interview with Joseph Campbell, The Power of Myth, Episode 6—Masks of Eternity—34'–43')

E3.1.1.b_REF3_PO “THE ART OF POETRY
To gaze at a river made of time and water
and remember Time is another river.
To know we stray like a river
and our faces vanish like water.
To feel that waking is another dream
that dreams of not dreaming and that the death
we fear in our bones is the death
that every night we call a dream.
To see in every day and year a symbol
of all the days of man and his years,
and convert the outrage of the years
into a music, a sound, and a symbol.
To see in death a dream, in the sunset
a golden sadness such is poetry,
humble and immortal, poetry,
returning, like dawn and the sunset.
Sometimes at evening there's a face
that sees us from the deeps of a mirror.
Art must be that sort of mirror,
disclosing to each of us his face.
They say Ulysses, wearied of wonders,
wept with love on seeing Ithaca,
humble and green. Art is that Ithaca,
a green eternity, not wonders.
Art is endless like a river flowing,
passing, yet remaining, a mirror to the same
inconstant Heraclitus, who is the same
and yet another, like the river flowing.”
(Jorge Luis Borges)

“ARTE POÉTICA
Mirar el río hecho de tiempo y agua
y recordar que el tiempo es otro río,
saber que nos perdemos como el río
y que los rostros pasan como el agua.
Sentir que la vigilia es otro sueño
que sueña no soñar y que la muerte
que teme nuestra carne es esa muerte
de cada noche, que se llama sueño.
Ver en el día o en el año un símbolo
de los días del hombre y de sus años,
convertir el ultraje de los años
en una música, un rumor y un símbolo,
ver en la muerte el sueño, en el ocaso
un triste oro, tal es la poesía
que es inmortal y pobre. La poesía
vuelve como la aurora y el ocaso.
A veces en las tardes una cara
nos mira desde el fondo de un espejo;
el arte debe ser como ese espejo
que nos revela nuestra propia cara.
Cuentan que Ulises, harto de prodigios,
lloró de amor al divisar su Itaca
verde y humilde. El arte es esa Itaca
de verde eternidad, no de prodigios.
También es como el río interminable
que pasa y queda y es cristal de un mismo
Heráclito inconstante, que es el mismo
y es otro, como el río interminable.”
(Jorge Luis Borges)

E3.1.1.b_REF4_PO "MORNING BIRDS

I wake up the car
with its windshield sprinkled with frost.
I put on my sunglasses.
The birdsong grows dim. 

While another man buys a newspaper 
at the station kiosk 
not far from a large freight car 
entirely reddened by rust 
and glistening in the sun. 

No empty spaces anywhere here. 

Through the spring warmth, an icy corridor
Where someone comes striding 
to tell us that we are defaming him
even in the highest places.
 
Through a hidden door in the landscape
the magpie arrives
black and white. Bird of Hel.
And the blackbird flits here and there
until it blackens the entire drawing,
except for those white clothes on a clothesline:
a Palestrina choir.

No empty spaces anywhere here. 

How wonderful to feel my poem growing
as I shrink.
It grows, it takes my place.
It ousts me.
It throws me out of the nest.
The poem is finished."
(Tomas Tranströmer, Accords and Traces - Klanger och spår, 1966)

E3.1.1.b_REF5_PO A_Dani Calundy, Epic teen angst-ridden futuro grungy painting

"Self-knowledge might be the most difficult of life’s rewards — the hardest to earn and the hardest to bear. To know yourself is to know that you are not an unassailable fixity amid the entropic storm of the universe but a set of fragilities in constant flux. To know yourself is to know that you are not invulnerable.
The honest encounter with that vulnerability is the wellspring of art: Every artist’s art is their coping mechanism for the extreme sensitivity to aliveness that we call beauty — the transcendent and terrifying capacity to be moved by the world, to let something outside us stir deeply something within us. All great art — and only honest art can be great — is therefore the work of vulnerability and all integrity the function of fidelity to one’s fragilities."
(Maria Popova, Brain Pickings)
"You must be vulnerable to be sensitive to reality. And to me being vulnerable is just another way of saying that one has nothing more to lose. I don’t have anything but darkness to lose. I’m way beyond that."
(Bob Dylan)

There are no more hours: there is life open as an expense.

E3.1.1.b_REF7_PH A_Michelangelo Pistoletto_On the other side of the mirror, Performance_2018_La Biennale di Alessandria_(1) ; A_Michelangelo Pistoletto_On the other side of the mirror, Performance_2018_La Biennale di Alessandria_(2) ; A_Michelangelo Pistoletto_On the other side of the mirror, Performance_2018_La Biennale di Alessandria_(3) /Michelangelo Pistoletto/

"IN THE WEST, A MIRROR IS AN ESSENTIALLY NARCISSISTIC OBJECT : MAN THINKS OF A MIRROR ONLY TO LOOK AT HIMSELF ; but in the East, it seems, the mirror is empty ; it is a symbol of the very emptiness of symbols (« The mind of the perfect man, says a Tao master, is like a mirror. It grasps nothing but repels nothing. It receives, but does not preserve. »): the mirror only captures other mirrors, and this infinite reflection is emptiness itself (which, as we know, is the form). Thus the haiku makes us remember what never happened to us; in it we recognize a repetition without origin, an event without cause, a memory without a subject, a speech without moorings."
(Roland Barthes, The Empire of Signs, 1970)

E3.1.2.a_REF1_LIT A_Phil Greenwood_Buttercup Ridge ; A_Phil Greenwood_Blossom_2003 ; A_Phil Greenwood_Autumn Vines_2007 /Phil Greenwood/

OH! NEVER TO BE JUST SOMEWHERE! NEVER TO BE JUST SOMEONE...
"The image of spring is formed by the superimposition of many memories. [...]
Yes, it is from all these juxtaposed memories that I construct the abstract image of spring.
 And that is also what worries me when the first beautiful days arrive: I would like to be able to contemplate renewal everywhere at once, which means that I am never completely at ease anywhere, even in the most beautiful garden in the world, or even in my little garden in Cuverville when I knew every flower personally.
As soon as the air is warm enough and the sky is blue, I want to evaporate into the whole of nature, carried away by the passing breeze, floating without a fixed place, here and there. Oh! Never to be just somewhere! Never to be just someone...
Spring is a becoming; it is the season of preparation, of hope."
(André Gide, Spring, The Garland of Years, 1942)

E3.1.2.a_REF2_LIT "TO THE GREEKS, ECSTASY MEANT TO STAND OUTSIDE ONESELF. How is that possible? Through existential engineering. “Give me a place to stand,” Archimedes proclaimed in the third century B.C., “and I will move the earth.” Levered by ecstasy, one springs out of one’s mind. Thrown free of one’s normal self, a person stands in another place, on the limits of body, society, and reason, watching the known world dwindle in the distance (a spot standing far away)."
(Diane Akerman, Deep play)

E3.1.2.a_REF3_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN DESCRIBES ROUSSEAU'S BRIEF STAY ON SAINT-PIERRE ISLAND AS A RARE MOMENT OF TOTAL "COINCIDENCE" WITH NATURE AND THE SELF that granted access to a godlike, self-sufficient feeling of existence, which Jullien interprets as an "ecstasy" that expansively opens the subject to infinity.

"[Rousseau's stay] on Saint-Pierre Island was a brief coincidence following the stoning of Môtiers. He didn't unpack his trunks or settle in: It was as if he were staying in an inn from which he would have to leave the next day. Coincidence with the landscape, made possible by the 'circumscription' of an island; coincidence with society, made possible by a small group of people who entertain but do not monopolize; coincidence with nature, made possible by the study of botany; coincidence with oneself: "In this isolated stay, I embraced myself." This coincidence in all respects, undisturbed by any intrusion, provides access to a pure feeling of existence. As long as this state lasts, one is self-sufficient, like God.
But what does "exist" mean? Rousseau promotes it from within "feeling" itself, revealing its existential meaning for the first time by detaching it from "being" and connecting it to living. In this moment of coincidence, we can finally access it, enjoying "nothing outside ourselves" ("nothing but ourselves and our own existence"). Far from withdrawing into adequacy with himself, the subject experiences "ecstasy," says Rousseau ("my sweet ecstasies"). Ecstasy means "standing outside" in Greek, as expressed by ex-istere in Latin. In this sense, "ecstasy" is not mystical but rather expresses an ethical overflowing and stepping outside of oneself. Such a re-coincidence neither closes in on itself nor stabilizes. At the edge of the lake, in ephemeral harmony with the landscape, Rousseau expansively surrenders to the vagueness of what he calls "reverie." The subject finds itself by opening up to infinity. Otherwise, coincidence would be a forcing that may satisfy the mind but denies life."
(François Jullien, Dé-coïncidence, Where Art and Existence Come From, 2017).

E1.4.C.3.3_REF2_PH.wE3.1.2.a.

BACHELARD URGES US TO INVEST OURSELVES ACTIVELY IN DAYDREAMING, WHICH IS AN EXPERIENCE OF THE IMAGE, AND TO PLUNGE INTO A CONTEMPLATIVE STATE THAT DISSOLVES THE SUBJECT-OBJECT DISTINCTION. “For Bachelard, imagination is as vital as reason. He criticizes psychoanalysis for reducing dreams to mere obscure desires and passions. Instead, he grants dreams an autonomous reality, arguing that they possess their own logic—one that reason may not comprehend. Our attraction to certain images isn't arbitrary; these images are integral to the human spirit. Reason and daydreaming, in Bachelard's view, represent the pinnacle of human mental achievement, contrasting sharply with the mundane aspects of everyday life.
Bachelard distinguishes between nocturnal dreams—where the subject is suspended and obliterated—and daydreams, where the subject is present and engaged. "A poor daydream is one that invites you to take a nap,": true daydreaming is an active, image-rich experience that blurs the line between subject and object.
Consider the contemplation of still water. Bachelard ponders: when a man gazes at his reflection, is it truly man observing himself, or the world observing man? This illustrates how the dreamer becomes one with the world. For Bachelard, this non-separation of man and world in reverie is fundamental. The psychic division between subject and world is a later development, achieved through education and rational thinking. This explains why children excel at reverie.
Poetic imagery has the power to immerse us in reverie. Bachelard writes, "I need the reveries of others to remember that I too was a dreamer under candlelight" (Fire and Dreams). Daydreaming, then, becomes a means of reconnecting with others—a "communion of men." It's trans-subjective, transcending individual boundaries.
Daydreaming is a form of meditation centered on imagination rather than present-moment awareness. It suspends time and frees us from social and professional constraints. Bachelard argues passionately for its importance in work: "If you take away dreams, you'll knock out the worker. Neglect the dream-inspiring powers of work, and you diminish and annihilate the worker... The dream-inspiring capability of work is the very condition of the worker's mental integrity." He envisions a future where every trade has its dreamer, its dreaming guide, where factories have poetic offices. For Bachelard, will without the capacity to dream is mere brutality.
While projects represent the rationalized dimension of work, following predefined methods, true joy in work comes from the ability to dream—to visualize one's actions against resistant materials. This visualization stage, familiar to craftsmen, is crucial. Yet in today's professional culture, the idea of "dreaming well to work well" is seldom heard or valued.”
(Julien Lamy, in the program Les Chemins de la Philosophie, broadcast on France Culture, on 05/21/2019)

E1.6.C.1.1.b_REF4.1_PH.wE3.1.2.a.

IN FRONT OF THE WATERS, NARCISSUS HAS THE REVELATION OF HIS IDENTITY AND HIS DUALITY, the revelation of his double masculine and feminine powers, the revelation especially of his reality and his ideality.
Near the fountain takes thus birth an idealizing narcissism [...]. The sublimation is not always the negation of a desire; it is not always presented as a sublimation against instincts. It can be a sublimation of an ideal. Then Narcissus does not say any more: « I like myself such as I am », he says: « I am such as I like myself ». I am with effervescence because I love myself with fervor. I want to appear, so I must increase my adornment. Thus life illustrates itself, life covers itself with images. Life grows; it transforms the being; life takes on whiteness; life blossoms; the imagination opens to the most distant metaphors; it participates in the life of all flowers. With this floral dynamic, real life takes on a new blassoming. Real life is better if given its fair leeway of unreality. [...]
  The least sigh 
  That I would exhale 
  Would come to confiscate 
  What I adored 
  On the blue and blond water 
  And skies and forests 
  And rose of the stream. 
   (« Narcissus », Paul Valéry, Combinations) 
[...] Narcissus at the fountain is not only deep into the contemplation of himself. His own image is the center of a world. With Narcissus, for Narcissus, it is all the forest which is reflected, all the sky which comes to take conscience of its grandiose image. [...] A cosmic narcissism [...] thus gives continuum quite naturally to the egoistic one. « I am handsome because nature is beautiful, nature is beautiful because I am handsome». Such is the endless dialogue of creative imagination and its natural models."
(Gaston Bachelard, Water and Dreams, 1942)


E3.1.2.a_REF4_LIT THIS IS THE CONTACT WITH THE "SUPREME UNIVERSAL STRANGENESS" AND THE "ESSENTIAL" REALITY BEHIND APPEARANCES.
"It felt as if everything was draining away. I didn't feel it as a void that had eaten away at my being and trapped me inside it. I was now nothing more than a frame around the void until I was completely consumed by it. It wasn't the emptiness we usually feel. That heavy emptiness, if I can put it that way. This time, it was a liberation. Things were losing their weight around me. I was separating myself from things that were losing their arbitrary and conventional meanings. This enormous quantity of contradictory and intertwined meanings had caught me or had been a multitude of confused paths—a labyrinth in which I was lost. Everything was now permeated by a dazzling light. I realized with boundless joy that everything is. I could think of nothing else but that everything is—that all things are—and realizing that they were, all things were, but differently, in a light of grace—delicate and fragile.
To me, fragility seemed to be one of the qualities of appearance or manifestation. However, this fragility was not disturbing. Quite the contrary. A great luminous energy seemed to be constantly on the verge of disarticulating and dissolving things. This light and force seemed hidden under the mask of things, but now it burst forth and exploded. It was a unique light, and things were the particular events of this light—the event-objects in which it was particularized. Truthfully, it had only been on the verge of exploding and dissolving everything, but the fire did not spread. Things had become luminous and translucent, but their contours were merely on the verge of fading and disappearing. There was no ecstasy or union. It was as if I had arrived at a frontier, on the edge of history, yet still here. Why did this invasion of light stop? Why wasn't I engulfed by the universal fire? Who or what within me was able to stop it? What accident or mistake prevented it? It was as if the luminous energy had collided with an invisible obstacle—lighter than a cloud—like smoke that covered and blackened everything. In an instant, things became heavy, opaque, and dark again. From that moment on, I understood that things are beautiful insofar as they retain a little of that light. I felt myself becoming heavy, thick, and leaden again—a leaden thing that the void can gnaw at, turning you into something to eat.
It seems to me that I was at the frontier of existence, very close to the place where things lose their name and definition, where time stops and history ends.”
(Eugène Ionesco, Diary in Pieces)

E3.1.2.a_REF4.1_LIT THIS IS THE CONTACT WITH THE "SUPREME UNIVERSAL STRANGENESS" AND THE "ESSENTIAL" REALITY BEHIND APPEARANCE (Continued)
"I had the experience. I knew what it was like to be outside of history. It is possible to achieve this state. This initial state of astonishment and amazement is unique to the human condition. It can enlighten anyone, regardless of their social status, historical era, or economic circumstances. None of these factors play a role in this case; none contribute to the emergence or disappearance of this experience. It happened, and so it happens in this kind of no man's land on the margins of the absolute or nothingness. Anyone—in the 20th century, the 15th century, or any other century; a biblical farmer or a modern petty bourgeois—can experience this once or twice. They can be filled with this luminous, irresistible astonishment and have the ahistorical, fundamentally asocial feeling of supreme, universal strangeness. I wonder if this unusual feeling—this astonishment without answer and almost without question—is a reaction of my deepest consciousness. Truthfully, I am not asking. I know it is this amazement that constitutes my most authentic consciousness. I know full well that the answer is the very impossibility of giving one. The non-answer is the best answer. [...]
In the past, when I was a teenager and for a little while after, astonishment gave rise to euphoria. Once again, I try to describe this state of mind, this event. I was in a small provincial town. I must have been about eighteen years old. It was a bright day, just before noon. It was early June. I was walking in front of the small town's low, white houses. What happened next was completely unexpected. The town underwent a sudden transformation. Everything became deeply real and deeply unreal. Reality and unreality were closely and indissolubly intertwined. The houses became whiter and cleaner. They appeared completely new and virginal in the light—an unknown world that I felt I had known forever. It was a world that the light dissolved and then reconstituted. A warm, luminous, overflowing joy welled up from deep within me. It was an absolute presence. I told myself that this was "truth," though I didn't know how to define it. Without a doubt, if I had tried to define it, it would have vanished. I told myself that since I had experienced this event and knew everything, I would never be unhappy again. I had learned that we do not die; I only had to remember these moments to overcome any future worries and anxieties. I had a revelation about what was essential; everything else was inessential.”
(Eugène Ionesco, Diary in Pieces)

E3.1.2.a_REF5_PH "OCEANIC FEELING
An expression borrowed by Freud from his "revered friend" Romain Rolland to describe certain spiritual or mystical states. It is 'a feeling of indissoluble union with the great Whole, and of belonging to the universal,' much like a wave or a drop of water are united with the ocean that carries them, or like falling out of the world. It is also a sense of unity, as Freud described in his reading: 'a sense of eternity, of something boundless, without limits,' a mixture of serenity and falling out of the world, and being one with the Whole. This experience is well-documented in all civilizations and often independently of religion (see Michel Hulin, La mystique sauvage, Puf, 1993; reprinted in 2008). Freud does not dispute its reality but refuses to see it as the origin of religions (Civilization and Its Discontents, I). He is undoubtedly right. Religions presuppose separation (between the sacred and the profane, and between God and the world) rather than unity. This is why they are generally wary of mystics and the oceanic feeling in particular: "When I am one with everything, what need do I have for a church?" "When I am one with everything, what need do I have for a church?"
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical dictionary, 2001)

E3.1.2.a_REF6_PH “OPEN 
[...] For Rilke, [...] it is the 'indescribable openness' of nature. This occurs when we cease to treat nature as an external, opaque object and instead inhabit its internal, shared, open-ended space. 'Through all beings passes the single space, the inner space of the world. Silently, the birds fly through us. [...]  All eyes of all living things look toward the Open." This is another way of describing the present, but it is thought of as space rather than duration ("the pure and simple space in which flowers never cease to bloom"), as coexistence rather than succession. Thus, humans and animals are open in the Open, passing through the passage and sharing the same fleeting eternity at the heart of being, infinity, and mystery. Animals live it without knowing it, and, as Rilke observes, most humans know it without living it or hasten to forget it."
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2001)

E3.1.2.b. The Paradox of Presence: "I am the size of what I see."
True unselfing is not just disappearing, but achieving a higher reality through objective attention, conatus (perseverance), and recognizing the "strangeness" of the other.

E3.1.2.b_REF1_PH JACQUES DARRIULAT CONTRASTS ROUSSEAU'S ROMANTIC ECSTASY, FOUND IN THE INTOXICATED DISSOLUTION OF THE SELF INTO THE VASTNESS OF NATURE, with Spinoza's concept of conatus, which defines existence not as a loss of self but as the active, divine power to stubbornly maintain one's individual identity and cohesion against the forces of dissolution.
"By communing with the soul of the world, the individual loses himself ecstatically in immensity. This is the feeling of the sublime that Kant described as the feeling of the absolutely great and the intuition of the infinite on which Schleiermacher based his new theology. However, according to Rousseau, such an experience is inspired by reverie, not by the experience of eternity, as Spinoza would have it. The ecstasy of the solitary walker arises from the dissolution of the self, which loses itself in the Whole. The more sensitive the soul of the contemplator, the more he surrenders to the ecstasy aroused in him by this harmony, the 'harmony of the three kingdoms' of nature. A sweet and profound reverie then takes hold of his senses, and he loses himself in delicious intoxication in the immensity of this beautiful system with which he identifies. All particular objects escape him; he sees and feels nothing but the whole. I never meditate or dream more deliciously than when I forget myself. I feel ecstasy and inexpressible rapture at melting into the system of beings and identifying myself with the whole of nature" (Seventh Promenade). There is nothing like this in Spinoza. The conatus, or the desire to assert one's own existence, is the essence of being—that is, divine power expressed by the vital force that animates each individual. "Everything, insofar as it is in itself, strives to persevere in its being" (Eth. III, 6). This is not a matter of losing one's individuality through communion with the soul of the greater whole. Rather, it is a matter of affirming the power to act that gives the individual its cohesion, coherence, and identity. Every particular existence thus affirms the unity that maintains its existence and has no desire to dissolve into an undifferentiated immensity. "For what we have shown so far applies equally to humans and other individuals, all of whom are animated, albeit to varying degrees" (Scholia II, 13). Everything, including stones, owes its cohesion to a divine force that maintains its unity and affirms its existence against the various pressures exerted on it by surrounding things. These pressures cause it to suffer a thousand passions in a full world where all bodies interact with one another. The stone is unaware of this, but man can grasp the principle and law of its cohesion and understand its necessity. Like stone, man is only a part of nature. Every part is a temporary aggregation of corpuscles that form a system for the duration assigned to this necessarily fragile organism. It is perpetually attacked by external bodies and regenerated by the renewal of its cells—the micro-formations that infinitely compose it. The density of the Absolute is realized in both matter and thought. Without conatus—the power to persevere in existence—the individual would immediately lose his identity and the organization that gives him substance would dissolve into disorganized, undifferentiated matter. Conatus is therefore an immanent cause of the identity of the individual, destined to assert and renew itself during the duration of its existence according to the resistance it is able to oppose to the forces that are contrary to it, in extension through the existence of the body and in thought through the chain of adequate ideas."
(Jacques Darriulat, Spinoza and the Fullness of This World: A Society of Reason, 2017)


E1.7.C.2.3_REF1_PH.wE3.1.2.b.

"FICHTE: "FORGETTING ONESELF IS THE CRITERION OF REALITY."
How do we avoid a formidable pitfall that threatens philosophy: to forget that man is first of all a being in relation, that is to say in relation with the world, things, other men? It is with this in mind that Fichte writes this dialogue with himself: "as you read this book, when you contemplate this object, when you speak with your friend, do you think about what you are doing, what you are reading, what you contemplate, what you hear or see? Not at all! I do not think of myself, I completely forget myself in the book, in the object, in the conversation - the formulation is correct when we say that we are absorbed in what we do or we are immersed in it". Fichte's remarks are decisive: the criterion for checking that you are in relation to reality is that you have gone to the background.
Thsi explains my astonishment with the school of meditation we call in France "full consciousness". Mindful walking, mindful eating - in a strict sense, is torture and a confinement. I am all the more surprised that the experience of meditation that I discovered about thirty years ago precisely taught me to free myself from full consciousness; my consciousness goes to the background: it is the world, it is things, beings which suddenly appear more fully and it is this movement which makes one happy - to forget oneself. We've all experienced it, there are times when you don't really know why or how, things just happen on their own, you just feel like you're serving the situation"
(Fabrice Midal, 3 minutes of philosophy to become human again, broadcast on France Culture on 08/19/2019)

Listen to the podcast in French: Écouter le podcast: https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/3-minutes-de-philosophie-pour-redevenir-humain/fichte-loubli-de-soi-est-le-critere-du-reel

E1.7.B.2.4.a_REF1_PO.wE3.1.2.b.

E3.1.2.b_REF2_LIT FOR SEGALEN, TRUE AWARENESS (UNSELFING) ARISES ONLY WHEN MAN CONCEIVES NATURE AS "DIFFERENT FROM HIM. He defines the sublime experience of nature not as super-human, but as "ex-human"—blind, eternal, and giant - the "greater whole" that exists completely independently of human ego or morality.
"The Exoticism of Nature"

This is our first experience with exoticism. The outside world immediately sets us apart. We will flee from old disputes about the reality of things. Oh, what does it matter if they move us? Nature only became a concept when humans learned to conceive of it as separate from themselves.

For a long time, he animated it with his own breath. He lent it his passions and gestures. Can we say that the Vedas felt nature? No! They animated it according to their desires. We know how much the Greeks ignored nature, and it is said that savages largely do the same. The sense of nature that is not anthropomorphized—of nature that is blind, eternal, gigantic, and not superhuman, but ex-human—and from which springs the strange—all of humanity—this exotic sense of nature only appeared with the knowledge of its forces and laws. These forces and laws were so distant from human ones that distraught man ran to the other end of the world and recognized two worlds: the physical and the moral. [...]

Regarding my plan of the universe being absorbed by a seed idea that swells... The wise man... discerns that he almost took a particular taste for a principle of certainty, and that he considered his will to be the center of the world for a moment. He lucidly perceives the passionate origin of the theory that upset him. In this way, he knows its relativity. He knows the precise place where it breaks the chain of causality to take hold of the will... "

(For me, it is an aptitude of my sensitivity—the ability to perceive diversity—which I consider the aesthetic foundation of my understanding of the world. I know where it comes from: myself. I know it's no more true than anything else, but also that it's no less true. I believe that I was the one who had to bring it to light, and that I will have fulfilled my role by doing so. "See the world, and then express your vision of it." I saw the world in all its diversity. I wanted to convey the flavor of this diversity.)"
(Victor Segalen, Essay on Exoticism, an Aesthetic of Diversity, 1908)

E3.1.2.b_REF2.1_LIT SEGALEN ARGUES THAT WE ONLY TRULY "TASTE" NATURE WHEN WE ARE "SEPARATED, DIFFERENTIATED" FROM IT. This challenges the "oceanic feeling" of Freud or the "fusion" of Rousseau (whom he explicitly criticizes). He redefines "Unselfing" not as becoming the object, but as recognizing the object as Other. 

"En somme, c'est l'éternel essai sur « le sentiment de la Nature » que je reprends ainsi. Sa banalité même va être la pierre de touche où se raiera, ou non, où se marquera, ou non, la pureté, la nouveauté de ma vision. Si elle éclaire ces fouillis fades où se répandirent les Rousseau, les George Sand, et d'autres, c'est qu'elle a vraiment quelque vertu singulière. Il ne me déplaît pas de l'exercer sur d'aussi piètres sujets.
Mais si de tout cela il ne sort rien d'imprévu... qu'on ferme le livre. C'est que le sens du Divers ne me possède pas... ou bien qu'on n'aura pas su l'accueillir.

Il existe une curieuse opposition entre le sentiment de la Nature et la Vie dans la Nature. On ne voit, on ne sent, on ne déguste la nature avec une grande joie esthétique, que lorsqu'on s'en est un peu séparé, différencié. Ceci prend une valeur singulière si je remplace, comme le veut le jeu de ma pensée, le mot sentiment par le mot Exotisme: Exotisme de la Nature : ne peut exister que si on la sent différente de soi. [...]

Maurice de Guérin est un bel exemple, pour l'Exotisme de la Nature, de ce que j'ai dessein de faire pour l'Exotisme des Races et des Moeurs m'en imbiber d'abord, puis m'en extraire, afin de les laisser dans toute leur saveur objective (comme les mêmes mots se retrouvent obstinément avec leur même force! Je dois aboutir à l'exotisme essentiel : celui de l'Objet pour le sujet !). Ainsi Maurice de Guérin s'est d'abord pénétré de la Nature, puis il s'en est totalement retiré. Cependant que ses contemporains romantiques, « n'ont fait de la nature que le corollaire de leur moi, et n'en ont rendu que des aspects particuliers ». [...]
(Je suis obligé de copier ce qui suit! En somme, cet essai de Clouard me vole et me comble à la fois : en me donnant un admirable exemple d'Exotisme de la Nature, et en le traitant d'une sorte telle qu'il me faudra tout faire pour la surpasser. En tout cas la reprendre fortement du point de vue exotique.)
« Voilà son (Maurice de Guérin) grand trait original : l'objectivité. Or cette objectivité accroît toute émotion de ce qu'elle y met de ferme, de solide, d'éternel... Maurice de Guérin a seulement allié (je disais le compromis de la Vie, avec Jules de Gaultier et je pensais que ma formule d'esthétique n'était ni plus vraie, ni moins vraie qu'une autre) le sentiment profond de l'Unité universelle au sentiment des différences des choses. [...] »
Il se peut qu'un des caractères de l'Exote soit la liberté, soit d'être libre vis-à-vis de l'objet qu'il décrit ou ressent, du moins dans cette phase finale, quand il s'en est retiré. Au contraire, les Loti sont mystiquement ivres et inconscients de leur objet, qu'ils mélangent à eux, et auquel ils se mélangent éperdument, « ivre de dieu! ». Or, Clouard dit : « La seule force de ces beautés montrera, je pense, qu'elles n'eussent pu naître sans une entière liberté de l'auteur à l'égard de son objet ni sans une incomparable fermeté de l'esprit : il faut sentir ce qu'une telle originalité a de réfléchi et de voulu jusque dans le plus fort de l'exaltation. » [...]
En somme, c'est l'éternel essai sur « le sentiment de la Nature » que je reprends ainsi. Sa banalité même va être la pierre de touche où se raiera, ou non, où se marquera, ou non, la pureté, la nouveauté de ma vision. Si elle éclaire ces fouillis fades où se répandirent les Rousseau, les George Sand, et d'autres, c'est qu'elle a vraiment quelque vertu singulière. Il ne me déplaît pas de l'exercer sur d'aussi piètres sujets.
Mais si de tout cela il ne sort rien d'imprévu... qu'on ferme le livre. C'est que le sens du Divers ne me possède pas... ou bien qu'on n'aura pas su l'accueillir.

Il existe une curieuse opposition entre le sentiment de la Nature et la Vie dans la Nature. On ne voit, on ne sent, on ne déguste la nature avec une grande joie esthétique, que lorsqu'on s'en est un peu séparé, différencié. Ceci prend une valeur singulière si je remplace, comme le veut le jeu de ma pensée, le mot sentiment par le mot Exotisme: Exotisme de la Nature : ne peut exister que si on la sent différente de soi. [...]

Maurice de Guérin est un bel exemple, pour l'Exotisme de la Nature, de ce que j'ai dessein de faire pour l'Exotisme des Races et des Moeurs m'en imbiber d'abord, puis m'en extraire, afin de les laisser dans toute leur saveur objective (comme les mêmes mots se retrouvent obstinément avec leur même force! Je dois aboutir à l'exotisme essentiel : celui de l'Objet pour le sujet !). Ainsi Maurice de Guérin s'est d'abord pénétré de la Nature, puis il s'en est totalement retiré. Cependant que ses contemporains romantiques, « n'ont fait de la nature que le corollaire de leur moi, et n'en ont rendu que des aspects particuliers ». [...]
(Je suis obligé de copier ce qui suit! En somme, cet essai de Clouard me vole et me comble à la fois : en me donnant un admirable exemple d'Exotisme de la Nature, et en le traitant d'une sorte telle qu'il me faudra tout faire pour la surpasser. En tout cas la reprendre fortement du point de vue exotique.)
« Voilà son (Maurice de Guérin) grand trait original : l'objectivité. Or cette objectivité accroît toute émotion de ce qu'elle y met de ferme, de solide, d'éternel... Maurice de Guérin a seulement allié (je disais le compromis de la Vie, avec Jules de Gaultier et je pensais que ma formule d'esthétique n'était ni plus vraie, ni moins vraie qu'une autre) le sentiment profond de l'Unité universelle au sentiment des différences des choses. [...] »
Il se peut qu'un des caractères de l'Exote soit la liberté, soit d'être libre vis-à-vis de l'objet qu'il décrit ou ressent, du moins dans cette phase finale, quand il s'en est retiré. Au contraire, les Loti sont mystiquement ivres et inconscients de leur objet, qu'ils mélangent à eux, et auquel ils se mélangent éperdument, « ivre de dieu! ». Or, Clouard dit : « La seule force de ces beautés montrera, je pense, qu'elles n'eussent pu naître sans une entière liberté de l'auteur à l'égard de son objet ni sans une incomparable fermeté de l'esprit : il faut sentir ce qu'une telle originalité a de réfléchi et de voulu jusque dans le plus fort de l'exaltation. » [...]"
(Victor Segalen, Essay on Exoticism, an Aesthetic of Diversity, 1908)


E3.1.2.b_REF3_LIT."THERE ARE MOMENTS WHEN EVERY DETAIL OF THE ORDINARY INTERESTS ME IN ITS OWN RIGHT, and I have an affection for everything because I can read everything clearly. Then I see—as Vieira said Sousa described—the common with singularity, and I am a poet with that soul with which Greek criticism formed the intellectual age of poetry. But there are also moments, and this is one that oppresses me now, when I feel more myself than external things, and everything turns into a night of rain and mud, lost in the solitude of a sidings, between two third-class trains.
Yes, my intimate virtue of often being objective, and thus straying from thinking about myself, suffers, like all virtues, and even like all vices, from a decline in affirmation."
(Fernando Pessoa, The Book of Disquiet, 1914)

E3.1.2.b_REF4_PH “We must try to penetrate the secret order of things and take nature as our model. It is a model of grandeur, of emptiness, and a generator of the absolute. It is only by following its path that we can approach being. As an eighth-century painter said: “Outside, I took nature as my teacher and found the nature of my heart.” 
(Words of Master Huang, reported by Fabienne Verdier, A Passenger of Silence)

E3.1.2.b_REF5_LIT I AM THE SIZE F WHAT I SEE.
"I reread passively, receiving inspiration and deliverance from those simple phrases by Caeiro about the small size of his village. He says that because the village is small, you can see more of the world than from the city, and that is why the village is bigger than the city.
 

'Because I am the size of what I see, and not the size of my height.'" 

Phrases like these seem to grow without my intention, cleansing me of the metaphysics I add to life spontaneously. After reading them, I go to my window overlooking the narrow street. I look at the big sky and the many stars, and I feel free, as if I have wings, and this feeling shakes my whole body. 
"I am the size of what I see!" Each time I contemplate this phrase, it seems more destined to reconstruct the universe into a constellation. "I am the size of what I see!" What great mental possession travels from the depths of emotion to the stars reflected in it! Thus, in a way, the stars are there. 
Now, knowing how to see, I look at the vast metaphysics of the heavens with certainty, wanting to die singing. "I am the size of what I see!" The vague moonlight, entirely mine, begins to spoil the midnight blue horizon. 
I want to raise my arms and shout savage things, speak to lofty mysteries, and affirm a broad new personality to the vast empty spaces.
 
But I collect myself and slow down. "I am the size of what I see!" The phrase encompasses my entire soul. I lean on it, and all my emotions fall upon me, inside, as upon the city outside. The hard moonlight, which begins at dusk, falls upon us all, indecipherable."
(Fernando Pessoa, The Book of Disquiet, 1914)

E3.1.2.b_REF7_LIT "KNOWING ANOTHER IS ENDLESS. AND I HAVE DISCOVERED THAT MAN’S EXPERIENCE OF THEM ENLARGES ROCK, FLOWER AND BIRD. The thing to be known grows with the knowing. E1.7.C.3.1.a_REF1_PH.wE3.1.2.b.

"VI. THE MARVELOUS MOMENT

YOU ARE LISTENING TO A SONG. SUDDENLY I ASK, "At this moment, who are you?" How will you answer this question immediately and spontaneously, without stopping to find words? If the question does not shock you out of listening, you will answer by humming the song. If the question surprised you, you will answer, "At this moment, who are you?" But if you stop to think, you will try to tell me, not about this moment, but about the past. I shall get information about your name and address, your business and personal history. But I asked who you are, not who you were. [...]

In times of happiness and pleasure, we are usually ready enough to be aware of the moment, and to the experience be all. In such moments we "forget ourselves," and the mind makes no attempt to divide itself from itself, to be separate from experience. But with the arrival of pain, whether physical or emotional, whether actual or anticipated, the split begins and the circle goes round and round.

As soon as it becomes clear that "I" cannot possibly escape from the reality of the present, since "I" is nothing other than what I know now, this inner turmoil must stop. No possibility remains but to be aware of pain, fear, boredom, or grief in the same complete way that one is aware of pleasure. The human organism has the most wonderful powers of adaptation to both physical and psychological pain. But these can only come into full play when the pain is not being constantly restimulated by this inner effort to get away from it, to separate the "I" from the feeling. The effort creates a state of tension in which the pain thrives. But when the tension ceases, mind and body begin to absorb the pain as water reacts to a blow or cut. [...]

We cannot compare joy with sorrow. Comparison is possible only by the very rapid alternation of two states of mind, and you cannot switch back and forth between the genuine feelings of joy and sorrow as you can shift your eyes between a cat and a dog. Sorrow can only be compared with the memory of joy, which is not at all the same thing as joy itself. [...]"
(Alan Watts, The wisdom of insecurity, 1951)

E3.2.1. The Illusion of Mastery and the Terror of the "Stranger" Within. The conscious mind's realization that it is being steered by unknown forces, the anxiety of not knowing the source of one's emotions, and the philosophical view that the true source of tragedy and repression is internal, not external.
