E3.2.1_REF4_PH A_León Ferrari_Sem título [public and private self]_1985-1986

CLÉMENT ROSSET ARGUES THAT THE "ANTI-TRAGIC" AND PARANOID TENDENCY TO ATTRIBUTE MISFORTUNE TO EXTERNAL FORCES OBSCURES THE TRUE NATURE OF TRAGEDY, which locates the ultimate source of terror and the unknown not in an alien "elsewhere," but within the disturbing interiority of the self.
"In general, the concept of exteriority is arguably the anti-tragic theme par excellence because it is the core theme of paranoia ("they" have condemned me to perdition).  [...]. Its exteriority ensures that a being can only be accidentally affected by tragedy. It is either a scattered halo around the sphere of the rational world (the idea of irrationality) or a fatal power that disrupts human determinism. Without this external interference, determinism would be healthy, normal, and harmonious (the idea of destiny). In any case, it is something that is not primary but intervenes and disrupts: otherness, the enemy. This elementary fantasy, from Rousseau to the present day, has sustained everything conceived as mediocre or crazy in philosophy. Paranoid figures, such as blaming others or God, are just variations on the original theme of attributing the tragic nature of existence to an "elsewhere." "Elsewhere" sums up the misunderstanding of tragedy and the origin of the idea of "misfortune." The two themes, misfortune and tragedy, are inextricably linked by exclusivity: if there is tragedy, there is no misfortune. [...]
While the concept of exteriority refers to the non-tragic, the concept of interiority refers to the specific realm of tragedy, as well as the connections between Greek tragedy and modern psychoanalytic perspectives. Both Sophocles and Freud reject the idea of an external force oppressing man and instead locate the source of terror within oneself. They both discover a force within man sufficient to describe the totality of his misfortunes, at least his "psychological" misfortunes. Nothing is more tragic or terrifying for humans than that which comes from within themselves. Nothing is stranger or more unknown. Here, in this primal terror of the self, lies the source of what Freud described as "repression." The idea that the closest is also the farthest away, the best known is the most unknown, and the most familiar is the most strange is a theme that fuels Greek tragedy, detective stories, and psychoanalytic thought. What is the unknown X sought by the tragic hero, the police inspector, and the psychoanalyst? Tragedy says it is yourself; the detective novel says it is the number one innocent, described from the outset as a character too familiar to be suspected; and psychoanalysis says it is the unknown force within you that represses you. (Clément Rosset, The Logic of the Worse, 1971).

E3.2.1_REF4.1_LIT A_Salvador Dali_Sketches of Freud_1938 

CLÉMENT ROSSET DRAWS ON FREUD’S CONCEPT OF THE UNHEIMLICHE AND THE FIGURE OF OEDIPUS TO ARGUE THAT THE ULTIMATE SOURCE OF ANXIETY IS NOT THE SPECIFIC CONTENT OF WHAT IS REPRESSED, BUT THE TERRIFYING DISCOVERY THAT THE MECHANISM OF REPRESSION ITSELF IS AN UNCONTROLLABLE, alien force operating within our deepest intimacy. 
"In his 1919 study, Das Unheimliche, Freud equated the strange with the familiar, an idea expressed by the ambiguous notion of heimlich. This concept sums up the mechanism of terror. Seeing the present, the close, and the familiar as absent, distant, and strange is the tragic experience par excellence—but only after it is too late. Yet, of everything close to humans, nothing is closer than themselves and the psychological forces at work within them. Psychological powers are so familiar yet so distant from any real knowledge that Freud called them the unconscious. The possibility of relegation to the unconscious takes place in silence and is both the most familiar and the most unknown, thus defining one of the most characteristic "points" of anxiety. This idea is further explained in Freud's essay "Inhibition, Symptom, and Anxiety," which poses a fundamental question: Does the anxiety-provoking content of certain themes lead people to repress them, or does the mechanism of repression itself cause anxiety? If we opt for the second hypothesis, anxiety is not defined by a particular object but by how that object was excluded from consciousness. This is distressing because it is man's most intimate work and yet it escapes his control. What is most "his" is also most foreign to him. Psychoanalysis asks the neurotic, "What are you afraid of?" In other words, everyone. No, perhaps not of what is terrible about what you have forgotten, but rather of the fact that you forgot it without realizing it. You are afraid of yourself, of this person who is unknown to you and who controls the mechanism within you that allows you to admit or exclude this or that representation from your consciousness—whichever it may be, ultimately. If you wake up in anguish, trying in vain to remember the frightening dream, it's not the fear of reliving it that frightens you. Rather, it's the fear of coming face-to-face with the unknown force within you that causes you to forget your dream at that very moment. What represses you is much more frightening than what you repress. This is what Freud taught and what Greek tragedy taught as well, notably in Oedipus Rex. Oedipus is both a psychoanalytic and tragic hero not because he is incestuous and a parricide, but because he questions something exterior to the subject that concerns only interiority."
(Clément Rosset, Logic of the Worse, 1971)

E3.2.2. The Constitutive Other: How Passion, Instinct, and the "Self" Create the Subject. The "Other" is not just a saboteur, but the very foundation of the subject. 
How the body, passions, and the "Self" construct the "I," and how one can actively engage with this otherness for healing or creativity.

E3.2.2_REF1_PH CARL GUSTAV JUNG POSITED THAT THE PSYCHE POSSESSES AN INHERENT SPIRITUAL HEALING PROCESS WHERE THE TRANSCENDENT "SELF" GUIDES THE "EGO" TOWARD UNITY.
"Carl Gustav Jung was a mystical dissident from Freud.
In 1913, Jung, a disciple of Freud, broke with his mentor. Jung believed that dreams were an essential vehicle of psychoanalysis with healing properties, whereas Freud believed that dreams merely expressed neuroses. Jung and Freud had opposing approaches to dreams. For Freud, dreams must always be reduced to reveal the symptoms of a neurosis. For Jung, however, dreams contain the cure for neurosis. From dream to dream, there is a healing process that comes from within the psyche itself. For Jung, analysis is essentially the interpretation of dreams and this process"
(Pierre Trigano).

According to Jung, dreams do not belong to the dreamer. Rather, they are the expression of an otherness that comes from deep within. We do not create dreams; we receive them from the "Self" when the "Ego" is asleep. The Self is the transcendent center of the psyche. For Jung, psychoanalysis is always a spiritual experience, unlike for Freud. The Self flows through all of us; it is a secular, mystical vision. The Self guides the ego toward unity. Problems cannot be solved, but they can be overcome by integrating opposites and allowing things to happen (influenced by Taoism). This does not mean the cancellation of the Ego because we need to be differentiated beings. The ego is essential for experiencing the outside world and the inner process of the Self.
The Self can reunite opposites and reduce intrapsychic conflicts imposed by the ego, which defends a one-sided point of view steeped in the unconscious.

Freud believed that dreams were merely a symptom of neurosis, so he retained only certain dream symbols, while Jung studied dreams in their entirety as a process and took the time to interpret their symbolic meanings.
"If there was one faculty neglected by philosophers, it was imagination. However, one need only consider humanity to sense the singular dynamism of the human imagination. Images that superficial psychology considers fleeting and contingent appear to be deeply rooted in the human psyche. Images have a hierarchy, and Jung endeavored to elucidate this hierarchy to find the fundamental images—those that are alive in all centuries and in all people of different races and cultures. These, he calls, as is well known, the archetypes of the imagination" (Gaston Bachelard).

Through practice, Jung believed it was possible to establish a relationship between the ego and the self. He called this relationship "active imagination," which allows the ego to question the self and find answers in dreams.

Jung supported Nazism for a time between the two world wars. This aberration has been analyzed and explained by the incest he suffered at the hands of an uncle as a child. Like some people who were abused in early childhood, he was fascinated by the male omnipotence he found embodied in Nazism."
(Pierre Trigano on the radio program Les Chemins de la Philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on September 24, 2019).

Listen to the podcast in French: https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/faut-il-tuer-freud-24-carl-gustav-jung-le-dissident-mystique

E3.2.2_REF2_PH DELEUZE ARGUES THAT THE "MECHANISM OF THE BODY" AND ITS "SPONTANEITY" ARE WHAT ALLOW THE MIND TO BECOME A SUBJECT. The "I" is not a ghost in the machine; it is the result of the machine's "dispositions."
"We just saw how time changes when the subject is formed in the mind. Now, we can move on to the second point: What becomes of the organism? Previously, it was presented only as a mechanism of distinct perceptions. Now, saying that the subject is constituted in the mind means that, under the influence of principles, the organism takes on a double spontaneity.
 

First, there is a spontaneity of relation. "When an idea is conceived, the animal spirits rush into all the neighboring traces and awaken related ideas." In order for animal minds to find, in neighboring traces, ideas linked to the initial idea, the mind must first associate these ideas; the mechanism of distinct perceptions must intersect with a physical spontaneity of relationships in the body itself, a spontaneity that depends on the same principles as subjectivity. The body was previously considered only as the mind—the collection of ideas and impressions in the mechanism of their distinct production. Now, the body is considered as the subject itself, in the spontaneity of the relationships it establishes between ideas under the influence of principles.

On the other hand, there is a spontaneity of disposition. As we have seen, it was important for Hume to distinguish between two kinds of impressions: impressions of sensation and impressions of reflection. Our entire problem depends on this distinction because impressions of sensation only form the mind and give it an origin. Impressions of reflection, on the other hand, constitute the subject in the mind and qualify the mind in various ways. Hume presents these impressions of reflection as part of the collection, but they must first be formed. In their formation, they depend on a particular process and on principles that are the principles of subjectivity.

The mind cannot extract a new, original idea by contemplating all its sensations a thousand times unless nature has fashioned its faculties to feel a new, original impression arising from such contemplation.

The problem, then, is to understand the new dimension that the principles of subjectivity confer on the body when constituting impressions of reflection in the mind. Impressions of sensation are defined by a mechanism and refer to the body as the process of that mechanism. Impressions of reflection, however, are defined by spontaneity and a disposition and refer to the body as the biological source of that spontaneity. Hume analyzes this new dimension of the body by studying the passions. The organism is disposed to produce passion and has a particular and specific disposition to the passion in question as "a primitive internal movement." This is the case with hunger, thirst, and sexual desire. However, one might object that not all passions are like this. There are passions such as pride, humility, love, hate, and love between the sexes, as well as joy and sorrow, to which no particular bodily disposition corresponds. This is because nature does not produce these passions "immediately of itself," but "must be seconded by the operation of other causes." These causes are natural but not original. In other words, in this case, an external object assumes the role of bodily disposition and produces passion in determinable natural circumstances. Therefore, even in this case, the phenomenon of passion can only be understood based on bodily disposition. Just as nature has given the body certain appetites and inclinations, so too has it acted with regard to the mind. What is the general meaning of disposition? Through passion, it spontaneously provokes the appearance of an idea of the object that responds to the passion."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.2.2_REF2.1_PH "ASSOCIATION OF IDEAS EXPLAINS ONLY THE SUPERFICIAL IN US... ONLY AFFECTIVITY CAN JUSTIFY THE SINGULAR CONTENT, THE DEEP." This deep, singular force (passion/interest) acts as the "Other Within" that drives the conscious mind's associations.
"The particular path of the mind must be studied. There is a whole casuistry to be explored: Why, at a particular moment in a particular consciousness, will this perception evoke this idea rather than another?" The association of ideas does not explain why one idea is evoked rather than another. Therefore, from this point of view, the relationship must be defined as 'that particular circumstance in which we deem it appropriate to compare two ideas, even when they are arbitrarily linked in the imagination.'" While association is necessary to make any relationship possible in general, it does not explain each particular relationship. What gives a relationship its sufficient reason is circumstance.

This notion of circumstance appears constantly in Hume's philosophy. It is at the center of history and makes a science of the particular and differential psychology possible. When Freud and Bergson demonstrate that the association of ideas only explains the superficial aspects of us—the formalism of consciousness—they essentially mean that only affectivity can justify the singular, the profound, and the particular. Undoubtedly, they are right. But Hume never said anything else. He simply believed that the superficial and formal also had to be explained and that this task was the most important. For the rest, he invokes circumstance. For him, this notion always refers to affectivity. We must take the idea that affectivity is a matter of circumstance literally.
These variables define our passions and interests. Understood in this way, a set of circumstances always singularizes a subject since it represents the state of their passions and needs, as well as the distribution of their interests and beliefs. Therefore, we can see that the principles of passion must be combined with the principles of association for the subject to be constituted in the mind. While the latter explains how ideas are associated, only the former can explain why a particular idea is associated at a given moment rather than another. [...]
In all cases, we see that the subject presents itself in the mind under the influence of two kinds of combined principles. It is as if the principles of association give the subject its necessary form while the principles of passion give it its unique content. The latter function as a principle of the subject's individuation. However, this duality does not imply an opposition between the singular and the universal. The principles of passion are no less universal and constant than the others. They define laws in which circumstances play the role of variables. They concern the individual in the sense that a science of the individual can be developed. [...] We can anticipate how this unity will manifest in the subject. If the relationship cannot be separated from circumstances and the subject cannot be separated from singular content that is essential to it, then subjectivity is essentially practical. Its definitive unity will be revealed in the relationships between motive and action, means and end. This unity is the unity of the relationships themselves and of the circumstances. Indeed, these means-end and motive-action relationships are relationships, but they are also something else. The fundamental proposition of empiricism is that there is no theoretical subjectivity and that there cannot be any. On closer inspection, this is just another way of saying that the subject is constituted in the given. If the subject is constituted in the given, then there is no subject other than the practical one."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.2.2_REF3_PO. A_René Magritte_Not to be reproduced, 1937_A picture of a portrait looking at a portrait of himself framed as a mirror

"I IS ANOTHER."
“I want to be a poet, and I am working to become a seer: you will not understand at all, and I can hardly explain it to you. It is a matter of reaching the unknown by disrupting all the senses. The suffering is enormous, but one must be strong, be born a poet, and I have recognized myself as a poet. It is not my fault at all. It is wrong to say: I think: one should say: I am thought. - Pardon the pun. - I is another.”
(Arthur Rimbaud. Letter to Georges Izambard - May 13, 1871)

"For I is another. If the copper awakens as a bugle, it is not its fault. It is obvious to me: I witness the blossoming of my thought: I watch it, I listen to it: I strike a bow: the symphony stirs in the depths, or leaps onto the stage. [...]
I say that one must be a seer, make oneself a seer.
The Poet makes himself a seer through a long, immense, and reasoned derangement of all the senses. All forms of love, suffering, madness; he seeks himself, he exhausts all poisons within himself, to retain only their quintessences. Ineffable torture, where he needs all his faith, all his superhuman strength, where he becomes among all the great sick man, the great criminal, the great accursed, and the supreme Scholar! For he arrives at the unknown! Since he has cultivated his soul, already rich, more than any other! He arrives at the unknown, and when, in a state of panic, he ends up losing the intelligence of his visions, he has seen them! Let him die in his leap through unheard-of and unnameable things: other horrible workers will come; they will begin with the horizons where the other has collapsed."
(Arthur Rimbaud, Letter to Paul Demeny - May 15, 1871)

E3.2.2_REF4_PH REASON IS A KIND OF FEELING" AND A "CALM DETERMINATION OF THE PASSIONS." This means the "rational self" we identify with is actually just a stabilized form of the "Other Within" (passion/instinct).

"Every idea derives from a corresponding impression; consequently, every impression is reproduced in an idea that represents it exactly. [...]
The characteristic feature of reflective impressions and the effects of principles is that they qualify the mind as a subject in various ways. Therefore, what is revealed from the affections is the idea of this subjectivity. The word 'idea' can no longer have the same meaning. The psychology of affections is the philosophy of a constituted subject.
This is the philosophy that rationalism has lost. Hume's philosophy is a sharp critique of representation. Hume does not critique relations, but rather representations because they cannot present relations. By making representation a criterion and placing the idea in reason, rationalism has placed in the idea what cannot be constituted in the first sense of experience. This includes what cannot be given without contradiction in an idea: the idea's own generality and the existence of the object. It also includes the meaning of the words "always," "universal," "necessary," and "true." Rationalism transferred the determination of the mind to external objects. This removed the meaning and understanding of practice and the subject from philosophy. In fact, reason is an affection of the mind, not the mind itself. In this sense, it will be called instinct, habit, or nature.

Reason is nothing more than a calm and general determination of the passions based on reflection or a distant view.

Reason is a kind of feeling. Thus, just as the philosophical method goes from an absence of ideas to a presence of impressions, the theory of reason goes from skepticism to positivism—from skepticism of reason to a feeling-based positivism that ultimately includes reason as a reflection of feeling in the qualified mind. [...]"
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3. The Self as Hoax: Deconstructing the "I" as a "Controlled Hallucination". We become a stranger to this fiction by realizing its illusory nature.

E3.3.1. The Phenomenological Void — The Search for a "Whole Self". When we look inward via introspection or logic, we never find a unified "I," but rather a chaotic collection of perceptions, memories, and sensations. The "self" is not a substance, but a "bundle" or an "aggregate."

E3.3.1_REF1_PH I NEVER CAN CATCH MYSELF AT ANY TIME WITHOUT A PERCEPTION, AND NEVER CAN OBSERVE ANY THING BUT THE PERCEPTION.
"For my part, when I enter most intimately into what I call myself, I always stumble on some particular perception
or other, of heat or cold, light or shade, love or hatred, pain or pleasure. I never can catch myself at any time without a perception, and never can observe any thing but the perception. When my perceptions are removed for any time, as by sound sleep; so long am I insensible of myself, and may truly be said not to exist. And were all my perceptions removed by death, and could I neither think, nor feel, nor see, nor love, nor hate after the dissolution of my body, I should be entirely annihilated, nor do I conceive what is farther requisite to make me a perfect non-entity. If any one, upon serious and unprejudiced reflection thinks he has a different notion of himself, I must confess I call reason no longer with him. All I can allow him is, that he may be in the right as well as I, and that we are essentially different in this particular. He may, perhaps, perceive something simple and continued,
which he calls himself; though I am certain there is no such principle in me."
(David Hume,A Treatise of Human Nature, Book I, Part 4, section VI)

E3.3.1_REF2_LIT A_Chen Kong Fang_Penteadeira com espelho_1981

"THERE IS NO WHOLE SELF. HE WHO DEFINES PERSONAL IDENTITY AS THE PRIVATE POSSESSION OF SOME DEPOSITORY OF MEMORIES IS MISTAKEN.Whoever affirms such a thing is abusing the symbol that solidifies memory in the form of an enduring and tangible granary or warehouse, when memory is no more than the noun by which we imply that among the innumerable possible states of consciousness, many occur again in an imprecise way. Moreover, if I root personality in remembrance, what claim of ownership can be made on the elapsed instants that, because they were quotidian or stale, did not stamp us with a lasting mark? Heaped up over years, they lie buried, inaccessible to our avid longing. And that much-vaunted memory to whose ruling you made appeal, does it ever manifest all its past plenitude? Does it truly live? The sensualists and their ilk, who conceive of your personality as the sum of your successive states of mind, are similarly deceiving them­ selves. On closer scrutiny, their formula is no more than an ignominious circumlocution that undermines the very foundation it constructs, an acid that eats away at itself, a prattling fraud and a belabored contradiction. [...]
I do not deny this consciousness of being, nor the immediate security of here I am that it breathes into us. What I do deny is that all our other convictions must be adjusted to the customary antithesis between the self and the non-self, and that this antithesis is constant. The sensation of cold, of spacious and pleasurable suppleness, that is in me as I open the front door and go out along the half-darkness of the street is neither a supplement to a pre-existing self nor an event that comes coupled to the other event of a continuing and rigorous self. […]
There is no whole self. It suffices to walk any distance along the inexorable rigidity that the mirrors of the past open to us in order to feel like outsiders, naively flustered by our own bygone days. There is no community of intention in them, nor are they propelled by the same breeze. [...]
I, for example, am not the visual reality that my eyes encompass, for if I were, darkness would kill me and nothing would remain in me to desire the spectacle of the world, or even to forget it. Nor am I the audible world that I hear, for in that case silence would erase me and I would pass from sound to sound without memory of the previous one. Subsequent identical lines of argument can be directed toward the senses of smell, taste, and touch, proving not only that I am not the world of appearances — a thing generally known and undisputed — but that the apperceptions that indicate that world are not my self either. That is, I am not my own activity of seeing, hearing, smelling, tasting, touching. Nor am I my body, which is a phenomenon among others. Up to this point the argument is banal; its distinction lies in its application to spiritual matters. Are desire, thought, happiness, and distress my true self? The answer, in accordance with the precept, is clearly in the negative, since those conditions expire without annulling me with them. Consciousness — the final hideout where we might track down the self­ — also proves unqualified. Once the emotions, the extraneous perceptions, and even ever-shifting thought are dismissed, consciousness is a barren thing, without any appearance reflected in it to make it exist. [...]
The self [is] a mere logical imperative, without qualities of its own or distinctions from individual to individual."
(Jorge Luis Borges , The Nothingness of Personality, 1922 - found in the posthumous collection Selected Non-Fictions)

E3.3.1_REF3_PH "SELF 
The subject, considered in its objectivity. This contradiction makes it elusive, if not impossible. Buddhism teaches that there is no self, either in me (no atman) or in everything (no Brahman). Only aggregates and processes exist, and they are all conditioned and impermanent. This means that the subject is not a substance but a story. It is not an essence, but rather an accident or several accidents. Not a principle, but a result—always ephemeral. Such is also the spirit of our human sciences."
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2001)

E3.3.2. The Architecture of the Hoax — Fiction, Association, and Prediction. The brain/mind operates on "delirium" and "prediction," using principles of association to synthesize a "subject" out of chaos. The "I" is an effect of these processes, not the cause of them.

E3.3.2_REF1_LIT DANIEL DENNETT' CALLS THE "GREAT DENIAL," THE PHILOSOPHICAL POSITION THAT SUBJECTIVE INTERIOR EXPERIENCE IS AN ILLUSORY "CARTESIAN THEATER" PRODUCED BY THE BRAIN'S UNCONSCIOUS COMPUTATIONS, rendering introspection a fabricated story with no real access to or control over thought.
“Metaphors are typically abandoned once they are proven to be insufficient. But in some cases, they become only more entrenched: the limits of the comparison come to redefine the concepts themselves. This latter tactic has been taken up by the eliminativists, philosophers who claim that consciousness simply does not exist. Just as computers can operate convincingly without any internal life, so can we. According to these thinkers, there is no “hard problem” because that which the problem is trying to explain—interior experience—is not real. The philosopher Galen Strawson has dubbed this theory “the Great Denial,” arguing that it is the most absurd conclusion ever to have entered into philosophical thought—though it is one that many prominent thinkers espouse. Chief among the deniers is Daniel Dennett, who has often insisted that the mind is illusory. Dennett refers to the belief in interior experience derisively as the “Cartesian theater,” invoking the popular delusion—again, Descartes’s fault—that there exists in the brain some miniature perceiving entity, a homunculus that is watching the brain’s representations of the external world projected onto a movie screen and making decisions about future actions. One can see the problem with this analogy without any appeals to neurobiology: if there is a homunculus in my brain, then it must itself (if it is able to perceive) contain a still smaller homunculus in its head, and so on, in infinite regress.
Dennett argues that the mind is just the brain and the brain is nothing but computation, unconscious all the way down. What we experience as introspection is merely an illusion, a made-up story that causes us to think we have “privileged access” to our thinking processes. But this illusion has no real connection to the mechanics of thought, and no ability to direct or control it. Some proponents of this view are so intent on avoiding the sloppy language of folk psychology that any reference to human emotions and intentions is routinely put in scare quotes. We can speak of brains as “thinking,” “perceiving,” or “understanding” so long as it’s clear that these are metaphors for the mechanical processes. “The idea that, in addition to all of those, there is this extra special something—subjectivity—that distinguishes us from the zombie,” Dennett writes, “that’s an illusion.”
Most people, like Strawson, find this logically absurd, though it’s hard to object without sounding defensive, full of wounded pride. I want to insist that this is unfair, that finding a conclusion logically unsatisfying is not the same as finding it merely unflattering. But then I wonder whether I am capable of really knowing the difference. If most of my thinking is in fact unconscious—if I have no “privileged access” to the workings of my brain—then how can I claim to be an authority on my own motives? Perhaps some deep limbic instinct is impelling me to deny the theory, which is then expressed through my brain’s speech center in terms of rational principles. The more I read about theories of mind, the more I’ve come to see my interior life as a hall of mirrors, capable of all kinds of tricks and sleights of hand. Perhaps it’s true that consciousness does not really exist—that, as Brooks put it, we “overanthropomorphize humans.” If I am capable of attributing life to all kinds of inanimate objects, then can’t I do the same to myself? In light of these theories, what does it mean to speak of one’s “self” at all?”
(Meghan OGieblyn, God Human Animal Machine, 2021)

E1.7.C.2.5_REF1_SCI.wE3.3.2.
"Strogatz (32:37): Well, alright, so as we move toward the end of our discussion here, I think we should start talking about the self. I’m  reminded of expressions that you have been using earlier in this conversation where you say things like, “the brain’s best guess,” or “the brain is trying to make sense of…” It almost makes me picture what people used to call the omunculus. Like, there’s a little person inside your head who’s looking out through your eyes, trying to make sense of the world. It’s sort of: the self, what is it? I mean, who’s in  there? Who is it that’s trying to make sense?

Seth (33:09): Yeah, I mean, so this is why when I say the brain believes this, or the brain predicts this, I tried to be careful to say the brain rather than you. Because I think the experience of being you, the experience of self, is another kind of controlled hallucination. It’s another kind of brain-based best guess, OK? And this is another challenge to this sort of naive how-things-seem view of perception.

(33:32) Now, just as it seems as though there’s a real world out there that just pours itself into our minds, it might also seem to us that the self is the thing that does the perceiving. This quiddity, this essence of you or me, maybe the soul that resides somewhere homunculus-like inside the skull, doing all the perceiving and then deciding what to do and then pulling various meat strings in the body to make actions happen.

(34:00) And I think this is fundamentally mistaken. And again, this is not a new thing to say. Here, the philosopher David Hume, Scottish philosopher, had what he called “the bundle theory of self,” which is the idea that the nature of self is just an ongoing collection of perceptions. It’s not the thing that does the perceiving. It’s another kind of perception, but this time based at least in part on the body. So just as the brain, in trying to make sense of what’s out there in the world, is making predictions about the causes of signals that come into
the eyes and the ears and so on, it’s also trying to figure out what’s happening inside the body and trying to also control the body too. And this also happens, in my view anyway, through a process of the brain creating predictions and using them to update sensory signals, but in this case from the body. Or when making actions to use predictions to actually, you know, overwhelm sensory data so they become selffulfilling predictions, so that if I move my arm to pick up the cup again, now that’s a kind of self-fulfilling prediction about where my arm will be.

(35:10) But the key point is that there’s a common principle here underlying experiences of the world, and experiences of the self. They’re both forms of perceptual prediction. Neither of them reflect things as they really are. And both can be subject to illusions — they can both go awry in various ways.

(35:32) So lots of the book is kind of taking this idea that we build up about how our perception of the world works, and then turning the lens inward to understand how the same core principle can explain experiences of selfhood. And these include not just the sort of highlevel aspects of self that “I’m Anil Seth, and I live in Brighton” and so on and so on, and “I remember this funny story about getting a ticket in San Diego.” But much more basic elements of self too, like the fact that this object that I call my body is part of myself — emotions and moods are part of self, experiences of free will. Now, they are not the consequences of some almost supernatural causal power that sweeps in and changes the course of physical events in the world. No, that’s another kind of perception of what my body is doing.

(36:23) I’ve come to think this is a very satisfying view of self, that all these diferent elements of what it is to be me or to be you can be understood as forms of perception that play some role in guiding the behavior of the organism. And it can also help us understand what happens when these processes go wrong. There’s so many conditions in psychiatry and neurology, and that we can induce in the lab using virtual reality, where we can tease apart these elements of self so that some remain and some are altered. The more you do this, the less sense it makes to presume that there is such a thing as a unified, stable, unchanging essence of anyone."
(From the transcript of the podcast: What is the nature of consciousness, The joy of why, Quanta Magazine, May31, 2023)

E3.3.2_REF3_PH  A_Daniel Senise_Legenda_2008_(1) ; A_Daniel Senise_Legenda_2008_(2) ; A_Daniel Senise_Legenda_2008_(3) ; A_Daniel Senise_Legenda_2008_(4) /Daniel Senise/

FOR HUME,SUBJECTIVITY IS NOT AS A GIVEN ENTITY BUT AS A PRACTICAL SYNTHESIS CONSTITUTED WITHIN THE MIND THROUGH THE TRANSFORMATIVE PRINCIPLES OF ASSOCIATION AND PASSION, a process where imagination (or fiction) paradoxically becomes a foundational principle of human nature itself, creating the illusion of continuity and identity necessary for a coherent system of knowledge and action. 
“"Fiction becomes a principle of human nature." That is the most important point. Indeed, the meaning of the principles of human nature is to transform a collection of ideas into a system of knowledge and objects. However, for a system to exist, ideas must not only be associated in the mind, but perceptions must also be grasped as separate from the mind. Impressions must, in a sense, be torn from the senses. The object of the idea must have an existence independent of the senses. The objects of knowledge must truly be objects. The principles of association, the vividness of the impression, and simple belief are not enough to achieve this. The system is complete when "an interruption in the appearance to the senses" is overcome by the fiction of a continuous being that fills these intervals and preserves a perfect and complete identity in our perceptions.

Belief in the existence of bodies breaks down into several moments. First is the principle of identity, a product of the fiction by which we apply the idea of time to an unchanging, continuous object. Next is the confusion by which we attribute previous identities to similar impressions because the easy transition—an effect of resemblance—resembles the effect produced by considering the identical object. Finally, there is a new fiction: continuous existence. This fiction is used to overcome the contradiction that arises between the discontinuity of impressions and the identities we have attributed to them. And that's not all. It may be surprising that, a few pages later, Hume first presents the reconciliation achieved by the fiction of continuous existence as satisfactory and then as false, requiring other fictions and reconciliations. On the one hand, continuous existence aligns well with the discontinuity of appearances. Therefore, it can legitimately unite discontinuous images and the perfect identity we attribute to them. However, it is important to note that this attribution of identity is false, our perceptions are interrupted, and the assertion of continuous existence makes an illegitimate use of the principles of human nature. Moreover, this use itself is a principle. The opposition is internal, at the heart of the imagination. The difference between imagination and reason has become a contradiction.

Imagination tells us that our similar perceptions have continuous, uninterrupted existence, and their absence does not destroy them. However, reflection tells us that our perceptions are discontinuous and differ from one another.


According to Hume, the contradiction lies between extension and reflection, imagination and reason, and the senses and understanding. However, these terms are not ideal, as they also apply to general rules. Elsewhere, Hume expresses this better: "between the principles of imagination and the principles of reason." In previous chapters, we continually demonstrated the opposition between reason and imagination and between human nature and fantasy. First, we saw how the principles of human nature fix the imagination. Then, we saw how the imagination recovers beyond this fixation. Finally, we saw how reason corrects this recovery. Now, however, at the last moment, the opposition has truly become a contradiction, and the imagination asserts itself on a specific point. This final moment is also a beginning. For the first time, the imagination opposes itself as a principle—the principle of the world—to the principles that fix it and the operations that correct it. Since fiction is among the principles, along with the world, the principles of association encounter fiction and oppose it without being able to destroy it. The most internal opposition asserts itself between constituted and constituent imaginations, between the principles of association and the fiction that has become a principle of nature.

Fiction, or extension, can no longer be understood, corrected, or destroyed by reflection precisely because it has become a principle.”
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3.2_REF3.1_PH THE SUBJECT'S CORE FUNCTION IS TO "BELIEVE" AND "INFER"—TO ADD SOMETHING TO THE "GIVEN" THAT ISN'T ACTUALLY THERE. The "I" is a synthetic system we impose on the chaos of experience.
"We believed that we had discovered the core of empiricism within the specific issue of subjectivity. First, however, we must ask how subjectivity is defined. The subject is defined as a movement that develops itself. The subject develops itself. The only meaning that can be attributed to the concept of subjectivity is mediation and transcendence. However, we should note that the process of developing oneself or becoming other is twofold: the subject both surpasses and reflects itself. Hume recognized these two dimensions and presented them as the fundamental characteristics of human nature: inference and invention, belief and artifice. [...]


Believing and inventing are what the subject does.

Believing is inferring one part of nature from another part that is not given. To invent is to distinguish powers and constitute functional totalities that are not given in nature either. This subject, which invents and believes, constitutes itself within the given in such a way that it transforms the given into a synthesis or system. This must be explained. In this problem, we discover the absolute essence of empiricism. Philosophy in general has always sought a plane of analysis from which to examine the structures of consciousness—that is, criticism—and justify experience as a whole. Therefore, critical philosophies are initially opposed by a difference in plane. We engage in transcendental criticism when we ask: How can anything be given to a subject? How can the subject give itself anything? How can there be anything given? To do so, we must place ourselves on a methodically reduced plane that gives us an essential certainty, a certainty of essence. [...]

Hume's merit lies in identifying this empirical problem in its pure state and keeping it separate from the transcendental and the psychological."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3.2_REF3.2_PH A_Antonio Dias_The place, map, mind_(1) ; A_Antonio Dias_The place, map, mind_(2) ; A_Antonio Dias_The place, map, mind_(3) /Antonio Dias/

DELEUZE DEFINES THE TRANSITION FROM "MIND" TO "SUBJECT" AS A MOVEMENT OF BELIEF AND ARTIFICE. THE SELF IS NOT A THING FOUND; IT IS A SYSTEM CREATED.
"We will say that, at the very least, the given gives itself to the senses and presupposes organs, or even a brain. Undoubtedly, but what must always be avoided is attributing an organization to the organism that will only come when the subject comes to mind. In other words, an organization that depends on the same principles as the subject. In an essential text, Hume considers a physiological explanation of association and subjectivity.

When we conceive an idea, animal spirits rush into neighboring traces, awakening related ideas.

Hume presents this explanation as 'probable and plausible'; however, he deliberately neglects it. When he invokes it, he does so not to explain association but rather the errors arising from it. Such a cerebral organization gives us a valid physiological model of the associative process but presupposes the principles on which it depends. Therefore, it cannot account for them. In short, organisms and the senses do not inherently possess the characteristics of human nature or subjectivity; they must receive them from elsewhere. The body's mechanism cannot explain the subject's spontaneity by itself. An organ is only a collection of impressions considered in the mechanism of their appearance.

External objects are seen and touched; then, they become present to the mind. In other words, they acquire a relationship instead of a cluster of joint perceptions.

In short, we always come back to the same conclusion: The mind—a collection of perceptions—cannot claim to be anything other than itself.

But what does it claim to be when it claims to be itself? The collection remains arbitrary; every idea and impression can disappear or separate from the mind without contradiction. How can we talk about the given or the mind in general? What is the consistency of the mind? [...]

"Five notes played on a flute give us the impression and idea of time, even though time is not a sixth impression that presents itself to the ear or any other sense." Similarly, the idea of space is merely the concept of visible or tangible points arranged in a specific order. Space is discovered in the arrangement of visible and tangible objects, just as time is discovered in the perceptible succession of changing objects.

Thus, the given is not in space; space is in the given. Space and time exist in the mind. [...]

We observe a continual succession of perceptions in the mind, so the idea of time is always present.

Perception is composed of parts. These parts are arranged in such a way as to give us the notions of distance and contiguity, length, width, and thickness.

Now, we must ask the following question: When we talk about the subject, what do we mean? We mean that imagination, from a simple collection, becomes a faculty; the distributed collection becomes a system. The given is taken up in a movement that goes beyond the given, and the mind becomes human nature. The subject invents and believes. It is a synthesis of the mind. We will pose three questions: First, what are the characteristics of the subject in terms of belief and invention? Second, by what principles does the subject constitute itself in this way? What factors have transformed the mind? Finally, what are the different stages of this mental process? What are the moments of the system?"
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3.2_REF3.3_PH "PRINCIPLES OF ASSOCIATION" (RESEMBLANCE, CONTIGUITY, CAUSALITY) BUILD THE FICTION OF THE “I”. THESE PRINCIPLES "NATURALIZE" THE MIND, GIVING IT A FALSE SENSE OF CONSTANCY AND UNITY.
“What factor will transform the mind? We have seen that Hume's answer is simple. What transforms the mind into a subject and constitutes a subject in the mind are the principles of human nature. These principles are of two kinds: the principles of association and the principles of passion. The latter can be presented in the general form of a principle of utility. A subject is an entity that, under the influence of a principle of utility, pursues a goal or intention, organizes means to an end, and establishes relationships between ideas under the influence of principles of association. Thus, the collection becomes a system. A collection of perceptions becomes a system when they are organized and connected. [...]

Relationships are external to ideas. If they are external, then the problem of the subject, as posed by empiricism, arises: What other causes depend on them? In other words, how is the subject constituted in the collection of ideas? Relationships are external to their terms.

Relationships are an effect of the principles of association. These principles give constancy to the mind and naturalize it. Each principle seems to address a particular aspect of the mind: contiguity to the senses, causality to time, and resemblance to the imagination. They have one thing in common: they designate a quality that naturally leads the mind from one idea to another. We understand the meaning of the word "quality"; the fact that one idea naturally introduces another is not a quality of the idea itself, but rather a quality of human nature. Only human nature is qualitative. Indeed, the collection of ideas will never explain how simple ideas regularly group together to form complex ideas. The ideas "most suited to unite in a complex idea" must be designated to each person. These ideas are not designated in the mind without the mind becoming a subject to which these ideas are assigned—a subject that speaks. Ideas are designated in the mind at the same time that the mind itself becomes a subject."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

"I have no heroes, no values to acknowledge—there is no identity, there is no time, there is only the great sensuality of memory and bodies. [...]
Definition
Time does not exist, it is a pond.
It is the self deployed in space that creates its own mobile matter, which we call time.
For convenience rather than silence."
(Christophe Béguin, War of Beauty, Impeccables, 2011)

E3.3.2_REF5_PH "THE BACKGROUND OF THE MIND IS DELIRIUM"
"Hume consistently asserts the unity of the mind, imagination, and ideas. The mind is not natural; it has no inherent nature. Rather, it is identical to the idea in the mind. Ideas are given as they are; they are experience. The mind is also given. It is merely a collection of ideas, not a system. The previous question can be restated as follows: How does a collection become a system? This collection of ideas is called imagination insofar as it designates not a faculty but the whole of things in the vaguest sense, which appear as collections without albums, plays without theaters, or flows of perceptions. The comparison to theater should not mislead us. We have no knowledge of the place where these scenes are performed or the materials it is made of. The place is no different from what happens there, and the representation is not in a subject." The question remains: How does the mind become a subject? How does the imagination become a faculty?" [...]
No doubt, Hume constantly repeats that the idea is in the imagination. However, the preposition does not indicate the inherent nature of any subject. On the contrary, it is used metaphorically to exclude any activity from the mind that is distinct from the movement of the idea. This ensures the identity of the mind and the idea in the mind. This means that the imagination is not a factor, an agent, or a determining force. Rather, it is a location that must be identified, that is, fixed and determinable. Nothing is done by the imagination; everything is done in the imagination. It is not even a faculty for forming ideas; the production of an idea by the imagination is merely the reproduction of an impression in the imagination. It certainly has its activity, but it is without constancy or uniformity. It is fanciful and delirious, the movement of ideas and the sum of their actions and reactions. As the realm of ideas, fantasy is a collection of individuals. As the link between them, fantasy is the movement that runs through the universe, engendering fire-breathing dragons, winged horses, and monstrous giants. The essence of the mind is delirium—or, which amounts to the same thing from other points of view, chance and indifference. Imagination itself is not a nature, but a fantasy. Constancy and uniformity are not found in my ideas. Nor are they found in the way ideas are linked by imagination; this connection is random. The generality of an idea is not a characteristic of an idea; it does not belong to the imagination. Rather, it is a role that any idea can play under the influence of other principles—not the nature of a species of ideas.
What are these other principles? How does imagination become human nature? Constancy and uniformity only exist in the way ideas are associated in the imagination. Association, in its three principles (contiguity, resemblance, and causality), transcends imagination; it is not imagination. Association affects imagination. Association finds its end and object in the imagination, not its origin. Association is a quality that unites ideas, not a quality of the ideas themselves."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3.2_REF5.1_PH "SUBJECTIVITY IS DETERMINED AS AN EFFECT... THE MIND BECOMES SUBJECT, BY BEING AFFECTED BY PRINCIPLES."
"Through belief and causality, we will see that the subject transcends the given. In other words, it transcends what the mind provides: I believe in what I have neither seen nor touched. However, if the subject can transcend the given, it is because it is the effect of principles that transcend the mind and affect it. Before belief can exist, the three principles of association organize the given as a system. This imposes constancy on the imagination, which it does not possess on its own. Without this constancy, the imagination would never be human nature. It attributes links and principles of union to ideas that are the original qualities of this nature. These qualities are not characteristics of the idea itself. Causality's privilege is that it alone can make us affirm existence and believe because it gives the idea of the object solidity and objectivity, which it would lack if it were only associated by contiguity or resemblance to the present impression. However, the other two principles share a role with causality. They fix and naturalize the mind, preparing and accompanying belief. This is the unique basis of empiricism: human nature transcends the mind in its principles, so nothing in the mind transcends human nature—nothing is transcendental. Association is a rule of the imagination, not a product or manifestation of its free exercise. Association guides the imagination, making it uniform and constraining it. In this sense, ideas are linked in the mind, not by the mind. Human nature is imagination, but other principles have made it constant and fixed. [...]
The effect of association manifests itself in three forms. The idea either takes on a role and becomes capable of representing all the associated ideas in the imagination by resemblance, forming a general idea, or the union of ideas by the mind acquires a regularity that it did not have before. Second, the union of ideas by the mind acquires a regularity that it did not have before. This is "nature in a way designating to each the simple ideas that are most suitable for uniting into a complex idea": substance and mode. Or, one idea introduces another, forming a relation. In all three cases, the effect of association is the easy passage of the mind from one idea to another. The essence of relation is easy transition. Having become nature, the mind has a tendency. [...]

When Hume speaks of an act of the mind or a tendency, he does not mean that the mind is active but rather that it is activated and has become a subject. The coherent paradox of Hume's philosophy is presenting a subjectivity that transcends itself yet remains passive. Subjectivity is defined as an effect, an impression of reflection. The mind becomes a subject by being affected by principles.
Nature can only be studied scientifically through its effects on the mind. However, the only true science of the mind must have nature as its object.

"Human nature is the only science of man."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3.2_REF5.2_PH "THE MIND IS NOT SUBJECT, IT IS SUBJECTED."

"Sensory impressions are merely the origin of the mind, while reflective impressions are its qualification, the effect of principles within it. The origin point of view, which states that every idea derives from and represents a pre-existing impression, is not as important as it has been made out to be. This point of view merely gives the mind a simple origin and prevents ideas from representing things, which would make it difficult to understand the resemblance of ideas. The real importance lies in reflective impressions because they qualify the mind as a subject. The essence and destiny of empiricism are not linked to the atom, but to association. Empiricism does not fundamentally pose the problem of the origin of the mind, but rather, the problem of the constitution of the subject." [...]

The mind is not a subject; it is a subject. When the subject is constituted in the mind under the influence of principles, the mind simultaneously grasps itself as an "I" because it is qualified. However, if the subject is constituted only in the collection of ideas, how can the collection of ideas grasp itself as an ego? How can it say "I" under the effect of the same principles? It is unclear how one can transition from tendencies to the ego or from the subject to the ego. How can the subject and the mind ultimately become one in the ego? The self must be a simultaneous collection of ideas and tendencies, as well as a mind and a subject. It is a synthesis—but an incomprehensible one—bringing together, without reconciling them, origin and qualification in its concept.

There are two irreconcilable principles: all our distinct perceptions are distinct existences and the mind never perceives any real connection between them.

Hume adds: Perhaps a solution is possible. We will see later what meaning can be given to this hope."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3.2_REF5.3_PH THE "I" EXISTS ONLY IN THE RELATIONS, WHICH DO NOT EXIST IN THE THINGS THEMSELVES. THE COHERENT SELF IS THUS AN ADDITION TO REALITY, NOT A PART OF IT.
"THE PRINCIPLES OF HUMAN NATURE

[...] Experience has two meanings, both of which are rigorously defined by Hume. In neither of these meanings is experience constitutive. According to the first meaning, if we define experience as the collection of distinct perceptions, we must acknowledge that relations do not stem from experience. Rather, they are the result of the principles of association—the principles of human nature that constitute a subject capable of transcending experience. If we use the word in its second sense to designate the various conjunctions of objects in the past, we must again recognize that the principles do not come from experience, since experience must be understood as a principle." [...]

The given has two meanings: experience is a collection of ideas that is given, but the subject that transcends experience and the relationships that do not depend on ideas are also given. Thus, empiricism can only be truly defined in terms of dualism. Empirical duality exists between terms and relations or, more precisely, between the causes of perceptions and the causes of relations and between the hidden powers of nature and the principles of human nature. Only this dualism, considered in all its possible forms, can define empiricism and pose this fundamental question: "How is the subject constituted in the given?" The given is the product of the powers of nature, and the subject is the product of the principles of human nature. [...]

We will call any theory that, in one way or another, asserts that relations derive from the nature of things non-empirical. [...]

The subject is the mind activated by principles. This notion of activation surpasses the alternative. As the principles take effect in the depths of the mind, the subject—which is the effect itself—becomes increasingly active and increasingly less passive. Initially passive, it becomes active by the end. This confirms our idea that subjectivity is a process and that we must consider the various stages of this process. In Bergson's words, the subject is initially an imprint, an impression left by principles. However, it gradually converts into a machine capable of using this impression."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3.2_REF5.4_PH A_Nevin Aladag_Sessions, still from video_2013_(1) ; A_Nevin Aladag_Sessions, still from video_2013_(2) ; A_Nevin Aladag_Traces, still from video_2015_(1) ; A_Nevin Aladag_Traces, still from video_2015_(2) /Nevin Aladag/

"THE SUBJECT IS THE PRODUCT OF PRINCIPLES IN THE MIND... BY ITSELF, THE MIND IS NOT A SUBJECT." 
 "The subject is the product of principles in the mind, but it is also the mind which exceeds itself. The mind becomes subject by the effect of principles, so that the subject is at the same time constituted by principles and founded on fantasy. How does this happen? By itself, the mind is not a subject: it is a given collection of separate impressions and ideas. An impression is defined by its vivacity; an idea, as a reproduction of the impression. Thus the spirit by itself has two fundamental characteristics: resonance and vivacity. One remembers the metaphor which pictures the mind as a percussion instrument. When does it become a subject? It is when it mobilizes its vivacity in such a way that part of its character (impression) communicates it to the other part (idea), and on the other hand, when all the parts taken together resonate by producing something new. These are the two modes of excelling: belief and invention."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.3.3. The Stranger in the Mirror . The psychological and linguistic reasons we cling to this hoax  are fear, grammar and a desire for security. There is a potential liberation found in realizing the "I" is a fiction.

E3.3.3_REF1_PH "ON BEING AWARE
It must be obvious, from the start, that there is a contradiction in wanting to be perfectly secure in a universe whose very nature is momentariness and fluidity. But the contradiction lies a little deeper than the mere conflict between the desire for security and the fact of change. If I want to be secure, that is, protected from the flux of life, I am wanting to be separate from life. Yet it is this very sense of separateness which makes me feel insecure. To be secure means to isolate and fortify the "I," but it is just the feeling of being an isolated "I" which makes me feel lonely and afraid. In other words, the more security I can get, the more I shall want.

To put it still more plainly: the desire for security and the feeling of insecurity are the same thing. To hold your breath is to lose your breath. A society based on the quest for security is nothing but a breath-retention contest in which everyone is as taut as a drum and as purple as a beet.

We look for this security by fortifying and enclosing ourselves in innumerable ways. We want the protection of being "exclusive" and "special," seeking to belong to the safest church, the best nation, the highest class, the right set, and the "nice" people. These defenses lead to divisions between us, and so to more insecurity demanding more defenses. Of course it is all done in the sincere belief that we are trying to do the right things and live in the best way; but this, too, is a contradiction.

I can only think seriously of trying to live up to an ideal, to improve myself, if I am split in two pieces. There must be a good "I" who is going to improve the bad "me." "I," who has the best intentions, will go to work on wayward "me," and the tussle between the two will very much stress the difference between them. Consequently "I" will feel more separate than ever, and so merely increase the lonely and cut-off feelings which make "me" behave so badly.

We can hardly begin to consider this problem unless it is clear that the craving for security is itself a pain and a contradiction, and that the more we pursue it, the more painful it becomes. This is true in whatever form security may be conceived. [...] 

To understand that there is no security is far more than to agree with the theory that all things change, more even than to observe the transitoriness of life. The notion of security is based on the feeling that there is something within us which is permanent, something which endures through all the days and changes of life. We are struggling to make sure of the permanence, continuity, and safety of this enduring core, this center and soul of our being which we call "I." For this we think to be the real man - the thinker of our thoughts, the feeler of our feelings, and the knower of our knowledge. We do not actually understand that there is no security until we realize that this "I" does not exist."
(Alan Watts, The wisdom of insecurity, 1951)

[EN] Alexandra Bircken, Semi-detached house, 2021

[FR] Alexandra Bircken, Maison jumelée, 2021
"Il doit être évident, dès le départ, qu'il y a une contradiction à vouloir être parfaitement en sécurité dans un univers dont la nature même est l'instantanéité et la fluidité. Mais la contradiction est un peu plus profonde que le simple conflit entre le désir de sécurité et la nature du changement. Si je veux être en sécurité, c'est-à-dire protégé du flux de la vie, je veux être séparé de la vie. Pourtant, c'est ce sentiment même de séparation qui me donne le sentiment d'insécurité. Être en sécurité signifie isoler et fortifier le  « moi », mais c'est justement le sentiment d'être un « moi » isolé qui me fait me sentir seul et avoir peur. En d'autres termes, plus je peux obtenir de sécurité, plus j'en veux.

Pour le dire encore plus clairement : le désir de sécurité et le sentiment d'insécurité sont la même chose. Retenir son souffle, c'est perdre son souffle. Une société fondée sur la recherche de la sécurité n'est rien d'autre qu'un concours de rétention du souffle dans lequel chacun est tendu comme un tambour et violet comme une betterave. [...]

Nous pouvons à peine commencer à considérer ce problème s'il n'est pas clair que la soif de sécurité est elle-même une douleur et une contradiction, et que plus nous la poursuivons, plus elle devient douloureuse. [...] 

Comprendre qu'il n'y a pas de sécurité, c'est bien plus qu'être d'accord avec la théorie selon laquelle tout change, plus même que de constater le caractère éphémère de la vie. La notion de sécurité repose sur le sentiment qu'il existe en nous quelque chose de permanent, quelque chose qui perdure à travers tous les changements de la vie. Nous nous efforçons de nous assurer de la permanence, de la continuité et de la sécurité de ce noyau durable, ce centre et cette âme de notre être que nous appelons « moi ». C'est pour cela que nous pensons être l'être véritable - celui qui pense nos pensées, celui qui ressent nos sentiments et celui qui possède nos connaissances. En fait, nous ne comprenons pas qu'il n'y a pas de sécurité tant que nous ne réalisons pas que ce « je » n'existe pas."
(Alan Watts, La sagesse de l'insécurité, 1951)
