E3.6.4_REF4_LIT A_Achille & Patrocle

YOU COULD PLAY OUT YOUR PART BEFORE YOUR OWN AWARENESS, AND BE TO THE END BOTH PERFORMER AND AUDIENCE!
“I think that down at the bottom of my being has been concealed from pretty early days a deep distrust and a shrewd suspicion of that solidity of the objective world that most people take for granted. I was always superstitious—at one time I even believed in Hell—but I am pretty sure that the more I became true to my subtler instincts the more I regarded the whole astronomical universe with a certain detachment, regarded it in fact as the mere material stage for playing whatever romantic, picturesque, or fantastical rôle a person’s life-illusion might arbitrarily select. It has always appealed to me — that legendary last word of Cesar Augustus: 

“Have I not played the Farce well? Put out the lights: ring down the curtain! Plaudite et vale! “ 

Deep, deep, deep must have sunken into my soul that grandiose acting in the presence of the gods and of Fate, of those classic Greeks and Romans! My mania for the sceptical Shakespeare, who like his own more sceptical master Montaigne, had a passion for these consummate actors, encouraged this predilection in me. Oh, how I revelled in every line of his Julius Cesar; and how startled I was when, in reading Dante, I found Brutus and Cassius thrust down to the bottom of Hell! 

At a very early age somebody read to me Church’s Stories  from Homer, and I found out that those more primeval and more elemental heroes were just as addicted to this vision of themselves as acting a spectacular part before gods and men, whether in exultation or desperation, as were the Greeks and Romans of the historical times. Thus I learned at a very early age what a consolation in life it is to enjoy things and to endure things as an actor; that is to say, not hugger-mugger, and in brute unconsciousness, but as though in default of any god or even of any fellow mortal, you could play out your part before your own awareness, and be to the end both performer and audience! “
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934, Faber and Faber, p. 121-122)

E3.6.4_REF5_LIT. A_Daniel Senise_Biógrafo XLVII_2018

"ONE OF THE THINGS THE STORY TEACHES US IS THIS: READ YOURSELF AS A FICTION AS WELL AS A FACT.

When I was growing up poor in a poor place with a pair of Pentecostal parents who were waiting for Jesus to return and roll up time and space like a scroll, I never thought my life was narrow or my chances bleak. I thought I was Heathcliff, Huck Finn, Hotspur, Aladdin, the Big Bad Wolf. The Fish with a Golden Ring.

And later, when I had left home at sixteen and was living in a Mini, I had my favourite books stashed in the boot and whenever I could be in the library, I was there. This wasn’t a fantasy world or escapism — though it was an escape; it was the hidden door in the blank wall. Open it.

I opened the book and went through.

The escape into another story reminds us that we too are another story. Not caught, not confined, not predestined, not only one gender or passion. Learning to read yourself as a fiction as well as a fact is liberating — it is the difference between energy and mass. Mass is the beloved object — the world we can touch and feel — but mass is also the dead weight in ourselves and others.

Shifting the dead weight takes energy but at its atomic core the dead weight is energy. Transforming mass into energy, energy into mass is what creative work is about. An idea becomes embodied. A tragedy is released."
(Jeanette Winterson, Oranges Are Not the Only Fruit, 1985)

E3.6.4_REF6_TH A_René Magritte_Faraway looks_1927 ; A_René Magritte_Everyday_1966 /René Magritte/

TO BE OURSELVES WE MUST HAVE OURSELVES — POSSESS, IF NEED BE RE-POSSESS, OUR LIFE-STORIES.
"In the twelfth chapter, titled “A Matter of Identity,” Dr. Sacks recounts the case of a patient with a memory disorder that rendered him unable to recognize not only others but himself — unable, that is, to retain the autobiographical facts which a person constellates into a selfhood. To compensate for this amnesiac anomaly, the man unconsciously invented countless phantasmagorical narratives about who he was and what he had done in his life, crowding the void of his identity with imagined selves and experiences he fully believed were real, were his own, far surpassing what any one person could compress into a single lifetime. [...]
But just as depression can be seen as melancholy in the complex clinical extreme and bipolar disorder as moodiness in the complex clinical extreme, every pathological malady of the mind is a complex clinical extreme of a core human tendency that inheres in each of our minds in tamer degrees. By magnifying basic tendencies to such extraordinary extremes, clinical cases offer a singular lens on how the ordinary mind works — and that, of course, is the great gift of Oliver Sacks, who wrests from his particular patient case studies uncommon insight into the universals of human nature.
“Such a patient,” Sacks writes of the inventive amnesiac man, “must literally make himself (and his world) up every moment.” And yet that is precisely what we are all doing in a certain sense, to a certain degree, as we continually make ourselves and our world up through the stories we tell ourselves and others.
Sacks examines the building blocks of that self-conception and how narrative becomes the pillar of our identity:

"We have, each of us, a life-story, an inner narrative — whose continuity, whose sense, is our lives. It might be said that each of us constructs and lives, a “narrative,” and that this narrative is us, our identities.
If we wish to know about a man, we ask “what is his story — his real, inmost story?” — for each of us is a biography, a story. Each of us is a singular narrative, which is constructed, continually, unconsciously, by, through, and in us — through our perceptions, our feelings, our thoughts, our actions; and, not least, our discourse, our spoken narrations. Biologically, physiologically, we are not so different from each other; historically, as narratives — we are each of us unique. [....]
To be ourselves we must have ourselves — possess, if need be re-possess, our life-stories. We must “recollect” ourselves, recollect the inner drama, the narrative, of ourselves. A man needs such a narrative, a continuous inner narrative, to maintain his identity, his self."
(Maria Popova, Brain Pickings, on Oliver Sacks' The man who mistook his wife for a hat, 1985)

Read it in The Marginalian: https://www.themarginalian.org/2018/01/15/oliver-sacks-identity-self-narrative/

E3.6.4_REF8_SCI A_Rui Chafes_The weight of paradise_2004_(1) ; A_Rui Chafes_The weight of paradise_2004_(2) /Rui Chafes/

THE HUMAN SPECIES CONTEMPLATING ITSELF.
"As we hurtle toward a cold and barren cosmos, we must accept that there is no grand design. Particles are not endowed with purpose. There is no final answer hovering in the depths of space awaiting discovery. Instead, certain special collections of particles can think and feel and reflect, and within these subjective worlds they can create purpose. And so, in our quest to fathom the human condition, the only direction to look is inward. That is the noble direction where to look. It is a direction that forgoes ready-made answers and turns to the highly personal journey of constructing our own meaning. It is a direction that leads to the very heart of creative expression and the source of our most resonant narratives. Science is a powerful, exquisite tool for grasping an external reality. But within that rubric, within that understanding, everything else is the human species contemplating itself, grasping what it needs to carry on, and telling a story that reverberates into the darkness, a story carved of sound and etched into silence, a story that, at its best, stirs the soul."
(Brian Greene, Until the End of Time: Mind, Matter, and Our Search for Meaning in an Evolving Universe)

E3.6.4_REF9_CUR In Ang Lee's film Life of Pi (2012), the tiger on the boat does not exist: it is Pi murdering the cook (the hyena), Pi despairing after seeing the cook kill his mother (the orangutan) and the zebra.
 
The tiger was “sleeping” under the boat's tarpaulin and only woke up after the unbearable murder of Pi's mother.
At first, he is terrified of the “tiger,” but little by little he “tames” it.
Once back in the human world, the “tiger” that is Pi returns to hide in the forest without even looking back: Pi must forget what happened on the boat in order to continue living sanely.
Pi constructed the story after recounting it to the investigators who questioned him upon his return to the coast.

Watch the key scene on YouTube.
We understand that the animals were projections of the humans on the boat.: https://youtu.be/7_jJ1rECP7E

Pi “releases” the art of the tigre in himself so that he can live among humans again: https://youtu.be/NKSQtevzuqo

E3.6.4_REF12_LIT I_Paloma Valdivia_Illustration from Pablo Neruda’s Book of Questions_(2)

BY ADVOCATING FOR "NOT DECIDING" SO THAT "FURTHER INFORMATION TO KEEP COMING IN," SAUNDERS CHAMPIONS A SELF-NARRATIVE THAT IS FLUID AND REVISING, RATHER THAN DOGMATIC.
"In a world full of people who seem to know everything, passionately, based on little (often slanted) information, where certainty is often mistaken for power, what a relief it is to be in the company of someone confident enough to stay unsure (that is, perpetually curious). [...]

It’s hard to be alive. The anxiety of living makes us want to judge, be sure, have a stance, definitively decide. Having a fixed, rigid system of belief can be a great relief. […]

As long as we don’t decide, we allow further information to keep coming in. Reading a story like “Gooseberries” [from Chekhov] might be seen as a way of practicing this. It reminds us that any question in the form “Is X right or wrong?” could benefit from another round of clarifying questions. Question: “Is X good or bad?” Story: “For whom? On what day, under what conditions? Might there be some unintended consequences associated with X? Some good hidden in the bad that is X? Some bad hidden in the good that is X? Tell me more.”"
(George Saunders, A Swim in a Pond in the Rain: In Which Four Russians Give a Master Class on Writing, Reading, and Life, 2021)

E3.6.4_REF12.1_LIT WE MUST "DENY OURSELVES THE COMFORT OF ALWAYS BEING THE SAME PERSON."

"Openness to more — to truth beyond story, to beauty beyond certainty — is precisely what teaches us how to love the world more."
(Maria Popova, The Marginalian)


"This feeling of fondness for the world takes the form, in his [Chekhov's] stories, of a constant state of reexamination. (“Am I sure? Is it really so? Is my preexisting opinion causing me to omit anything?”) He has a gift for reconsideration. Reconsideration is hard; it takes courage. We have to deny ourselves the comfort of always being the same person, one who arrived at an answer some time ago and has never had any reason to doubt it. In other words, we have to stay open (easy to say, in that confident, New Age way, but so hard to actually do, in the face of actual, grinding, terrifying life). As we watch Chekhov continually, ritually doubt all conclusions, we’re comforted. It’s all right to reconsider. It’s noble — holy, even. It can be done. We can do it. We know this because of the example he leaves in his stories, which are, we might say, splendid, brief reconsideration machines."
(George Saunders, A Swim in a Pond in the Rain: In Which Four Russians Give a Master Class on Writing, Reading, and Life, 2021)

E3.7. The Self as Habit: "Your Identity is Literally Your 'Repeated Beingness'". Becoming self-aware means actively choosing new habits to build a new self, intentionally becoming a stranger to the person we used to be.

E3.7.1. The Sedimentation of Circumstance — Deconstructing the Myth of a Fixed Essence. What we call "Identity" is actually just an accumulation of past moments, accidents, and repetitions.

E3.7.1_REF1_PH P_Blur

"TWO MAIN REASONS CAN BE CITED FOR THE APPALLING NATURE OF THE IDEA OF CHANCE, OR THE MATERIALISM OF CHANCE: 1) The idea of chance dissolves the idea of nature and calls into question the notion of being; 2) It precisely matches the definition of terror proposed by psychoanalysis following Freud: the loss of familiarity or, more accurately, the discovery that the familiar is, unexpectedly, an unknown realm par excellence, the high place of strangeness. […]
The condition required for the recognition of such natures is that the power of chance—or of habit, custom, learning, in short, of everything that can be considered an adjacent “circumstance”—stops at the boundaries of “something” that exists prior to the possibility of such interventions. Thus, human, plant, and mineral natures require, in order to exist, that something transcending all circumstances be contained within them. However, the thinker of chance asserts that “what exists” is exclusively made up of circumstances; that the relatively stable entities that bear, for example, the names of man, stone, or plant, represent certain sedimentations of circumstances that, by chance, by a happy (or unhappy) coincidence, have led to the organization of hazardous and shifting generalities (as random and shifting as each of the singularities of which they are composed); sedimentations that only the brevity—in every sense of the word—of a human perspective allows us to envisage as generalities, entities, natures. […]"
(Clément Rosset, Logic of the worse, 1971)

E3.7.1_REF2_PH  V_Jean Dupuy_Self-portrait_1974_(1) ; V_Jean Dupuy_Self-portrait_1974_(2) ; V_Jean Dupuy_Self-portrait_1974_(3) ; V_Jean Dupuy_Self-portrait_1974_(4) /Jean Dupuy/

"HABIT IS THE CONSTITUTIVE ROOT OF THE SUBJECT."
“First, in relation to time: The mind, as conceived in the manner of its perceived appearances, is essentially a succession of time. Speaking of the subject now means speaking of duration, custom, habit, and expectation. Expectation is habit; habit is expectation. These two aspects, the push of the past and the momentum toward the future, are the two fundamental dynamics at the heart of Hume's philosophy. There is no need to strain the texts to find most of the characteristics of Bergsonian memory in habit-expectation. Habit is the constitutive root of the subject. What the subject is at its root is the synthesis of time: the present and past in relation to the future. [...]

Expectation, the future, is the synthesis of time that the subject creates in the mind.

Habituation not only familiarizes us with things we have long enjoyed, but also engenders a preference for them over more estimable, yet unfamiliar, objects.

The same is true of belief. We know that belief is merely a vivid idea linked by a causal relationship to a present impression. Belief is a feeling—a particular way of feeling an idea. It is the idea "felt rather than conceived," the vivid idea. Therefore, to analyze this feeling, we must examine the causal relationship, as this is what gives the idea its vividness. In this analysis, the feeling reveals its source as the product of the synthesis of time. [...]

Habit is the great guide of human life. Without habit, we would never learn to adapt means to ends or use our natural abilities to achieve results. It would mean the end of action and almost all speculation.

In short, synthesis consists of establishing the past as the rule for the future. In both belief and property, we encounter the same transformation. Time was the structure of the mind, but now, the subject presents itself as the synthesis of time. To understand this transformation's meaning, note that the mind included memory in Hume's sense of the word. In the collection of perceptions, we distinguished the impressions of the senses, the ideas of memory, and the ideas of the imagination according to degrees of vividness. Memory is the reappearance of an impression in the form of a vivid idea. However, it did not operate any synthesis of time itself. It did not transcend structure; its essential role was to reproduce the given structures. Habit, on the other hand, presents itself as a synthesis and returns to the subject. Memory was the old present; it was not the past. The past is not simply what has been, but what determines, acts, drives, and weighs in a certain way. In this sense, habit is to memory as the subject is to the mind. Even more so, habit easily dispenses with the mind's dimension of memory; habit does not need memory. It usually does without it in one way or another. Sometimes, there is no evocation of memories; sometimes, there is no particular memory to evoke. In short, the past is not simply what has been; it is constituted by a synthesis that gives the subject its true origin and source."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.7.1_REF2.1_PH A_Liliana Porter_1973_(1) ; A_Liliana Porter_1973_(2) ; A_Liliana Porter_1973_(3) /Liliana Porter/

"The past and the present are constituted in time, under the influence of certain principles, and the synthesis of time is itself only this constitution, this organization, this double affection. The problem is thus: how, in time, do a present and a past constitute themselves? From this point of view, the analysis of the causal relation in its essential dualism makes sense. On the one hand, Hume presents us with experience as a principle that manifests a multiplicity, a repetition of similar cases; literally, this principle affects the time of the past. On the other hand, he sees in habits another principle that determines us to focus now from one object to the one that accompanied it, that is to say, that organizes time as a perpetual present to which we must and can adapt ourselves. And if we refer to the distinctions that Hume makes when he analyzes « the inference from impression to idea, » we can give the following definitions: understanding is the mind itself, but which, under the influence of the principle of experience, reflects time as a past subject to its observation; and imagination, under the influence of the principle of habits, is still the mind, but which reflects time as a determinate future filled by its expectations. Belief is the relationship between these two constituted dimensions."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.7.1_REF3_PH  GASTON BACHELARD ARGUES THAT THE CONTINUITY OF THE SELF ACROSS THE "VOID" OF TIME IS MAINTAINED ONLY BY THE "DENSITY" OF HABIT.
"First, the individual corresponds to a simultaneity of instantaneous actions insofar as he is complex. He finds himself only in proportion to the extent to which these simultaneous actions begin again. We can express this by saying that an individual, in the sum of his qualities and becoming, corresponds to a harmony of temporal rhythms. Indeed, rhythm is the best way to understand the continuity of the discontinuous, which we must establish to connect the peaks of being and outline its unity. Rhythm bridges silence just as being bridges the temporal void separating moments. Being continues through habit, just as time lasts through the regular density of moments without duration. At least, this is how we interpret Roupen's thesis: 'The individual is the expression, not of a constant cause, but of a juxtaposition of incessant memories fixed by matter, whose ligature is itself only a habit overlapping all others.'" Being is nothing more than a strange place of memories, and one could say that its perceived permanence is merely an expression of its habitual nature." 

Ultimately, the coherence of being is not made up of matter's inherent qualities and becoming; it is entirely harmonic and ethereal. It is fragile and free, like a symphony. A particular habit is a sustained rhythm in which actions are repeated fairly evenly, balancing their novelty value without ever losing their character as something new. The dilution of the new can be such that the habit sometimes passes for unconscious. It seems that the intense consciousness of the first attempt has been lost by being divided among all the repetitions. [...]

At whatever level we grasp the individual—in matter, life, or thought—he is a fairly variable sum of unrecorded habits. Since the habits that characterize a being do not simultaneously take advantage of all the moments that could actualize them, a being's unity always seems affected by contingency. Fundamentally, the individual is a sum of accidents, and this sum is also accidental. At the same time, a being's identity is never fully realized. It suffers from a lack of sufficient attention to the richness of habits. Global identity is made up of more or less exact repetitions and more or less detailed reflections. Undoubtedly, the individual strives to copy today from yesterday, aided by the dynamics of rhythms. However, these rhythms are not all at the same point in their evolution. Thus, even the most solid spiritual permanence of a desired identity affirmed in character degrades into resemblance. Life carries our image from mirror to mirror, making us reflections of reflections. Our courage is made up of the memory of our decisions. However firm we may be, we never remain whole because we have never been aware of our entire being. [...]"
(Gaston Bachelard, The Intuition of the Moment, 1931)

E3.7.2. The Architecture of Becoming — Active Repetition (the neuroscience of prediction), the Courage to Be a Stranger and the practical method of casting "votes" for a new identity.

E3.7.2_REF1_LIT A_Andy Denzler_Study for the portrait of Ella_2021_(1) ; A_Andy Denzler_Study for the portrait of Ella_2021_(2) /Andy Denzler/

"No two human beings ever experience two sensations, experiences, feelings, or thoughts identically. Everything changes, everything is always different. All of these variables merging, interacting, destroying each other, building new forms, ideas, “realities,” mean that the human experience is one of constant change and, as we label it, “growth” [and yet] most living human beings build their lives around the belief that these differences, changes, don’t exist. They choose to ignore these things and attempt to program or control their own existence. They make schedules, long-term commitments, set up a system of time and become controlled by their system of controls. [...] 

People don’t want to know that they change.
Unless they feel it is an improvement, and then they are all for “change,” and will go to great lengths to “make changes” or contrive situations or force a change that is unnatural… Some attitudes I see all around me are:
Change is acceptable as long as it is controllable.
Change can be predicted.
Changes can be contrived and/or altered and/or planned. [...]

Most simply, people know to some extent that they feel different at different times or look different to themselves different days, but few people really try to experience this or question it or really investigate its reasons or its implications. [...]

To be a victim of your own knowledge is not understanding what your knowledge is and what its result is.
To be a victim of change is to ignore its existence.
To be a victim of “living by what you think” is to ignore the possibilities of “another way to live” or the possibility of “being wrong about the way it is” or ignoring the possibility of “not knowing what you think.”
Thinking you know the answer is as dangerous as not thinking about the possibility of no answers."
(Keith Haring, Keith Haring Journals)

E3.7.2_REF2_LIT JAMES CLEAR ARGUES THAT YOUR IDENTITY IS NOT FIXED BUT IS THE EMBODIMENT OF YOUR HABITS, AS EACH REPEATED ACTION SERVES AS A "VOTE" PROVIDING EVIDENCE FOR WHO YOU BELIEVE YOU ARE, MEANING THAT THE PROCESS OF BECOMING A BETTER VERSION OF YOURSELF IS THE PROCESS OF BUILDING NEW HABITS.
“Your identity emerges out of your habits. You are not born with preset beliefs.
Every belief, including those about yourself, is learned and conditioned through
experience.

More precisely, your habits are how you embody your identity. When you
make your bed each day, you embody the identity of an organized person. When
you write each day, you embody the identity of a creative person. When you
train each day, you embody the identity of an athletic person.
The more you repeat a behavior, the more you reinforce the identity
associated with that behavior. In fact, the word identity was originally derived
from the Latin words essentitas, which means being, and identidem, which
means repeatedly. Your identity is literally your “repeated beingness.”
Whatever your identity is right now, you only believe it because you have
proof of it. If you go to church every Sunday for twenty years, you have
evidence that you are religious. If you study biology for one hour every night,
you have evidence that you are studious. If you go to the gym even when it’s
snowing, you have evidence that you are committed to fitness. The more
evidence you have for a belief, the more strongly you will believe it.
For most of my early life, I didn’t consider myself a writer. If you were to ask
any of my high school teachers or college professors, they would tell you I was
an average writer at best: certainly not a standout. When I began my writing
career, I published a new article every Monday and Thursday for the first few
years. As the evidence grew, so did my identity as a writer. I didn’t start out as a
writer. I became one through my habits.
Of course, your habits are not the only actions that influence your identity, but
by virtue of their frequency they are usually the most important ones. Each
experience in life modifies your self-image, but it’s unlikely you would consider
yourself a soccer player because you kicked a ball once or an artist because you
scribbled a picture. As you repeat these actions, however, the evidence
accumulates and your self-image begins to change. The effect of one-off
experiences tends to fade away while the effect of habits gets reinforced with
time, which means your habits contribute most of the evidence that shapes your
identity. In this way, the process of building habits is actually the process of
becoming yourself. […]

When working for you, identity change can be a powerful force for selfimprovement.
When working against you, though, identity change can be a curse. Once you
have adopted an identity, it can be easy to let your allegiance to it impact your
ability to change. Many people walk through life in a cognitive slumber, blindly
following the norms attached to their identity.

“I’m terrible with directions.”
“I’m not a morning person.”
“I’m bad at remembering people’s names.”
“I’m always late.”
“I’m not good with technology.”
“I’m horrible at math.”
. . . and a thousand other variations.
When you have repeated a story to yourself for years, it is easy to slide into
these mental grooves and accept them as a fact. In time, you begin to resist
certain actions because “that’s not who I am.” There is internal pressure to
maintain your self-image and behave in a way that is consistent with your
beliefs. You find whatever way you can to avoid contradicting yourself.
The more deeply a thought or action is tied to your identity, the more difficult
it is to change it. It can feel comfortable to believe what your culture believes
(group identity) or to do what upholds your self-image (personal identity), even
if it’s wrong. The biggest barrier to positive change at any level—individual,
team, society—is identity conflict. Good habits can make rational sense, but if
they conflict with your identity, you will fail to put them into action.
On any given day, you may struggle with your habits because you’re too busy
or too tired or too overwhelmed or hundreds of other reasons. Over the long run,
however, the real reason you fail to stick with habits is that your self-image gets
in the way. This is why you can’t get too attached to one version of your
identity. Progress requires unlearning. Becoming the best version of yourself
requires you to continuously edit your beliefs, and to upgrade and expand your
identity. […]

Each habit not only gets results but also teaches you something far more
important: to trust yourself. You start to believe you can actually accomplish
these things. When the votes mount up and the evidence begins to change, the
story you tell yourself begins to change as well.
Of course, it works the opposite way, too. Every time you choose to perform a
bad habit, it’s a vote for that identity. The good news is that you don’t need to be
perfect. In any election, there are going to be votes for both sides. You don’t
need a unanimous vote to win an election; you just need a majority. It doesn’t
matter if you cast a few votes for a bad behavior or an unproductive habit. Your
goal is simply to win the majority of the time.
New identities require new evidence. If you keep casting the same votes
you’ve always cast, you’re going to get the same results you’ve always had. If
nothing changes, nothing is going to change.
It is a simple two-step process:

1. Decide the type of person you want to be.
2. Prove it to yourself with small wins.”
(James Clear, Atomic habits, 2018)

E3.7.2_REF3_SCI BARRETT USES NEUROSCIENCE TO VALIDATE THE BUDDHIST VIEW OF THE SELF AS A CONSTRUCTED FICTION, showing how deconstructing this concept reduces suffering through emotional recategorization and meditation.
“Buddhism considers the self to be a fiction and the primary cause of human suffering. Whenever you crave material things like expensive cars and clothes, or desire compliments to enhance your reputation, or seek posi-tions of status and power to benefit your life, Buddhism says you are treat-ing your fictional self as real (reifying the self). These material concerns may bring immediate gratification and pleasure but they also entrap you, like golden handcuffs, and cause persistent suffering, which we would call pro-longed unpleasant affect. To a Buddhist, a self is worse than a passing physical illness. It is an enduring affliction.
My scientific definition of the self is inspired by the workings of the brain yet is sympathetic to the Buddhist view. The self is part of social reality. It’s not exactly a fiction, but neither is it objectively real in nature like a neutron. It depends on other people. In scientific terms, your predictions in the moment, and your actions that derive from them, depend to some extent on the way that others treat you. You can’t be a self by yourself. We can un-derstand why Tom Hanks’s character in the movie Cast Away, who was ma-rooned alone on a desert island for four years, needed to create a companion named Wilson out of a volleyball.
Certain behaviors and preferences are consistent with your self and some are not. There are foods you enjoy and others you’d prefer not to eat. You might call yourself a “dog person” or a “cat person.” These behaviors and preferences vary quite a bit: your favorite food might be French fries, but not at every meal. The most enthusiastic dog lovers know a couple of dogs that they can’t stand and are secretly fond of a few cats. Overall, your self is like a collection of dos and don’ts that summarizes your likes, dislikes, and habits in the moment.
We’ve seen something like this before. These dos and don’ts are like the features of a concept. So in my view, the self is a plain, ordinary concept just like “Tree,” “Things That Protect You from Stinging Insects,” and “Fear.” I am quite sure you don’t go around thinking of yourself as a concept, but just go with me for a bit on this.
If the self is a concept, then you construct instances of your self by simulation. Each instance fits your goals in the moment. Sometimes you cat-egorize yourself by your career. Sometimes you’re a parent, or a child, or a lover. Sometimes you’re just a body. Social psychologists say that we have multiple selves, but you can think of this repertoire as instances of a single, goal-based concept called “The Self ” in which the goal shifts based on context.
How does your brain keep track of all the varied instances of your “Self ” as an infant, a young child, an adolescent, a middle-aged adult, and an older adult? Because one part of you has remained constant: you’ve always had a body. Every concept you have ever learned includes the state of your body (as interoceptive predictions) at the time of learning. Some concepts involve a lot of interoception, such as “Sadness,” and others have less, such as “Plastic Wrap,” but they’re always in relation to the same body. So every categorization you construct  —  about objects in the world, other people, purely mental concepts like “Justice,” and so on  —  contains a little bit of you. This is the rudimentary mental basis of your sense of self.
The fiction of the self, paralleling the Buddhist idea, is that you have some enduring essence that makes you who you are. You do not. I speculate that your self is constructed anew in every moment by the same predictive, core systems that construct emotions, including our familiar pair of networks (interoceptive and control), among others, as they categorize the continuous stream of sensation from your body and the world. As a matter of fact, a portion of the interoceptive network, called the default mode net-work, has been called the “self system.” It consistently increases in activity during self-reflection. If you have atrophy in your default mode network, as happens in Alzheimer’s disease, you eventually lose your sense of self.
Deconstructing the self offers a new inspiration for how to become the master of your emotions. By tweaking your conceptual system and changing your predictions, you not only change your future experiences; you can actually change your “Self.” […]
When you are suffering from some ill or insult that has befallen you, ask yourself: Are you really in jeopardy here? Or is this so-called injury merely threatening the social reality of your self ? The answer will help you recategorize your pounding heartbeat, the knot in the pit of your stomach, and your sweaty brow as purely physical sensations, leaving your worry, anger, and dejection to dissolve like an antacid tablet in water.
I’m not saying this kind of recategorization is easy, but with practice it’s possible, and it’s also healthful. When you categorize something as “Not About Me,” it exits your affective niche and has less impact on your body budget. Similarly, when you are successful and feel proud, honored, or gratified, take a step back and remember that these pleasant emotions are en-tirely the result of social reality, reinforcing your fictional self. Celebrate your achievements but don’t let them become golden handcuffs. A little composure goes a long way.
If you are interested in taking this strategy further, try meditation. Mind-fulness meditation, just one type of many, teaches you to stay alert and pres-ent in the moment but to observe sensations as they come and go, non-judgmentally.* This state (which requires tremendous practice) reminds me of the quiet, alert state of newborn babies when they observe the world, their brains comfortably awash in prediction error, with no anxiety in sight. They experience sensations and release them. Meditation achieves some-thing similar. This state may take years of practice to achieve, so the next best thing is to recategorize your thoughts, feelings, and perceptions as physical sensations, which are easier to let go of. You can use meditation, at least at first, to prioritize categorizations that focus on the physical, and deprioritize those that add more psychological meaning about you or your place in the world.
Meditation has a potent effect on brain structure and function, though scientists have not sorted out the exact details yet. Key regions in the intero-ceptive and control networks are larger for meditators, and connections be-tween these regions are stronger. This matches what we might expect, since the interoceptive network is critical to constructing mental concepts and representing physical sensations from the body, and the control network is critical to regulating categorization. […]
Sometimes deconstructing the self is too challenging. You can achieve some of the same benefits more simply by cultivating and experiencing awe, the feeling of being in the presence of something vastly greater than yourself. It helps you get some distance from your self.

*From a Buddhist perspective, we might say that deconstructing the self helps to “suspend categorization.” From a neuroscience perspective, however, the brain never stops predicting so you can’t turn off concepts.”

(Lisa Feldman Barrett, How Emotions Are Made - The Secret Life of the Brain, 2017)

E3.8. The Social Self: A Stranger to Ourselves, a Character for Others. Our "I" is not even our own story—it is a social fiction.

E3.8.1. The "I" is a Ghost: Deconstructing the Myth of the Interior. What we call "I" is a phantom, a void, or a collection of borrowed qualities, rather than a solid, autonomous entity.

E3.8.1_REF1_PO P_Sylvia Plath

"What do I know of sorrow? No one I love has ever died or been tortured. I have never wanted for food to eat, or a place to sleep. I have been gifted with five senses and an attractive exterior. So I can philosophize from my snug little cushioned seat. So I am going to one of the most outstanding colleges in America; I am living with two thousand of the most outstanding girls in the United States. What have I to complain about? Nothing much. The main way I can add to my self-respect is by saying that I’m on scholarship, and if I hadn’t exercised my free will and studied through high school I never would be here. But when you come right down to it, how much of that was free will? How much was the capacity to think that I got from my parents, the home urge to study and do well academically, the necessity to find an alternative for the social world of boys and girls to which I was forbidden acceptance? And does not my desire to write come from a tendency toward introversion begun when I was small, brought up as I was in the fairy-tale world of Mary Poppins and Winnie-the-Pooh? Did not that set me apart from most of my school mates? — the fact that I got all A’s and was “different” from the rough-and-tumble Conways — how I am not quite sure, but “different” as the animal with the touch of human hands about him when he returns to the herd. All this may be a subtle way of egoistically separating myself from the common herd, but take it for what it’s worth. [...]

As for free will, there is such a narrow crack of it for man to move in, crushed as he is from birth by environment, heredity, time and event and local convention. If I had been born of Italian parents in one of the caves in the hills I would be a prostitute at the age of 12 or so because I had to live (why?) and that was the only way open. If I was born into a wealthy New York family with pseudo-cultural leanings, I would have had my coming-out party along with the rest of them, and be equipped with fur coats, social contacts, and a blasé pout. How do I know? I don’t; I can only guess. I wouldn’t be I. But I am I now; and so many other millions are so irretrievably their own special variety of “I” that I can hardly bear to think of it. I: how firm a letter; how reassuring the three strokes: one vertical, proud and assertive, and then the two short horizontal lines in quick, smug succession. The pen scratches on the paper … I … I … I … I … I … I."
(Sylvia Plath, The Unabridged Journals of Sylvia Plath, 1982)

E3.8.1_REF2_PH ACCEPTING ONE'S UNIQUE EXISTENCE IMPLIES THE HUMILIATING CONCESSION THAT ONE IS "ONLY THAT" AND NOTHING MORE.
“The taste for complication expresses, first and foremost, a need for duplication, necessary for the evasive assumption of a reality whose raw uniqueness is instinctively sensed as indigestible. Understood in this way, this rejection of simplicity allows us to understand why the “precious” make a fuss: less to shine in the world than to attenuate the brilliance of reality, whose radiance hurts them with its intolerable uniqueness. The thing is only tolerable when mediated, duplicated: there is nothing in this world that can be taken at face value. This is what Magdelon expresses very clearly in Molière when she declares to her father that she cannot “come straight to the point of marital union” and that she has “pain in her heart at the mere sight of it” (Les Précieuses ridicules, se. IV). We know the meaning, confirmed by etymology, of the French expression “de but en blanc”: to go straight for the target, to aim directly at the one without the aid of the double. Complication here is only a stopgap, a protective attitude against the inevitability of the one, to which fuss, whether precious or metaphysical in nature, and assuming that these two natures differ from each other, will never bring anything but a temporary, or at least illusory, obstacle. Temporary if it is only a passing fuss; illusory in any case, even if it is a stubborn and definitive fuss: because the rejection of the unique will never be accompanied by the grasping of a double, so that the search for the double to which the unique has been sacrificed is doomed to failure anyway, since it is the search for “nothingness,” which we foolishly imagine to be the “other” of reality. This fuss is thus related to a very deep anxiety, which can be summarily described as the worry that by accepting to be what we are, we are at the same time conceding that we are only that. Uniqueness implies both triumph and humiliation: triumph in being the only one in the world, humiliation in being only that one self, that is to say, almost nothing, and soon nothing at all. Preciousness would like triumph without humiliation, and in this it reflects not only a taste for complication, but more profoundly a distaste for oneself as unique."
(Clément Rosset, reality and its double, 1976)

E3.8.1_REF3_PH I_The Possibility of an island

NIETZSCHE ARGUES THAT PEOPLE DO NOT SERVE THEIR REAL EGO, BUT A "PHANTOM OF THIS EGO, WHICH HAS GROWN UP IN THE HEADS OF THEIR FRIENDS."
"Pseudo-egotism.— The great majority—whatever they may think and say about their “ selfishness ”—as long as they live, do nothing for their ego, but only for the phantom of this ego, which has grown up in the heads of their friends and been transmitted to them; consequently they all live in a mist of impersonal, half-personal opinions, and of arbitrary, so to speak poetic valuations, the one for ever in the head of somebody else, and this one again in other heads: an odd world of phantasms, which knows how to give itself a matter-of-fact appearance ! This mist of opinions and habits grows and lives on, almost independently from the people which it envelopes— its outcome is the extensive effect of the general judgments on “ man ” ; all these people, unknown to themselves, believe in the bloodless abstract idea “ man,” that is, in a fiction; and every change which, through the judgments of some powerful men (such as princes and philosophers) occurs in this abstract idea, has an extraordinary and irrational effect on the great majority, for the sole reason that not a single individual in this multitude is able to oppose a real ego, accessible to and sounded by himself, to the general pale fiction, and thereby to destroy it."
(Friedrich Nietzsche, The dawn of day, Aphorism 105, 1881)

E3.8.1_REF4_PH  THE "SUPREMACY OF THE SOCIAL SELF OVER THE PRIVATE SELF."
“The supremacy of the social self over the private self is evident in the fact that, since Saint Augustine, all philosophers have attributed the continuity of the person to the faculty of memory. Without memory, the unity of the self would disperse and disintegrate into isolated sensations, independent of one another. However, the social self is interested in the person's actions and words because psychological follow-up does not lend itself to detailed recollection. It follows that if the self can only recommend itself on the basis of its memory, then it can only be the memory of a social being. Consequently, there is no self other than the social self.
Perhaps we can now better understand the profound meaning of Pascal's remark: "Let us therefore no longer mock those who seek honor for their positions and offices, for we love no one except for borrowed qualities." Pensées, Fragment 323, Pascal." It is not only a matter of stating the deplorable but necessary necessity of honoring the great, their positions, and their offices. It is also a matter of stating the serious philosophical truth that apart from the signs and actions that emanate from me and make me who I am, nothing belongs to me."
(Clément Rosset, Far from Me, Study on Identity)

E3.8.1_REF4.1_PH "What is the Ego?

Suppose a man puts himself at a window to see those who pass by. If I
pass by, can I say that he placed himself there to see me? No; for he
does not think of me in particular. But does he who loves someone on
account of beauty really love that person? No; for the small-pox, which
will kill beauty without killing the person, will cause him to love her
no more.

And if one loves me for my judgment, memory, he does not love _me_, for
I can lose these qualities without losing myself. Where, then, is this
Ego, if it be neither in the body nor in the soul? And how love the body
or the soul, except for these qualities which do not constitute _me_,
since they are perishable? For it is impossible and would be unjust to
love the soul of a person in the abstract, and whatever qualities might
be therein. We never, then, love a person, but only qualities.

Let us, then, jeer no more at those who are honoured on account of rank
and office; for we love a person only on account of borrowed qualities."
(Pascal, Pensées, Fragment 323-688)

E3.8.1_REF5_PH  A_Jon Rafman_New Age Demanded_2013 /Jon Rafman/ ; A_Darcy Dias_[Magazine covers] /Darcy Dias/

“THERE IS NO OTHER IDENTITY OTHER THAN SOCIAL IDENTITY, THERE IS NO 'DEEP SELF'. You never know anyone because there is nobody to know. The theme developed in 'Loin de moi' was already present in Montaigne (« Our fact and our self are only pieces added  on »), Pascal and David Hume.
We do have distinctive signs, particular signs, qualities but that does not imply that they are organized around an ego. Pascal wrote « it's not for me, it's for my qualities that I am liked ».
On the other hand, isn't consciousness pure becoming? The quest for the ego is a ghost hunt - a hunt in the void, a haunting of the truth (in the sense of being the haunting of a thing - its ghost), condemned to an infinite fall in the abyss. At the end of the quest for identity there is no absolute, the regression is infinite - all identity is constructed or posited in a Platonic metaphysical tradition. « The less I know myself, the better I feel »: the inability to make sense of my ego is not a burden."
(Clément Rosset in the program 'À voice nue', broadcast on France Culture on 02/22/2006)

E3.8.1_REF6_PH  “AUTHENTICITY
The truth about oneself, or being what one appears to be (an 'authentic document'), or the truth about oneself to oneself (authentic behavior). In the latter sense, it is the opposite of bad faith. Is it a synonym for good faith? Rather, it is its name. The two concepts do not entirely overlap. To be sincere is to love the truth more than oneself. For many of our contemporaries, to be authentic is to love the truth of who one is or believes oneself to be. "Be yourself," they say across the Atlantic. Psychology has replaced morality, and personal development has replaced religion. Am I cowardly, selfish, or brutal? Undoubtedly, but at least give me credit for being authentically so! Authenticity is a comfortable virtue, which is why we doubt that it is one. It is narcissistic good faith, or good-faith narcissism. However, good faith does not excuse everything."
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary (2001)

E2.2.A.1.2.a.vii.wE3.8.1. "[Presence]
Presences [are] the return of the unchartable soul… They are a mode of attending, being present to [one’s] experiences, without dominating or controlling them. […]

Understanding other conceptions of persons puts one on the way of being them; but understanding presences — if indeed there is understanding of them to be had — does not put one any closer to being one. It cannot be achieved by imitation, willing, practice, or a good education. It is a mode of identity invented precisely to go beyond of achievement and willfulness."
(Amélie Rorty, The Identities of Persons, 1976)

E3.8.2. Manufactured by the Other: The "I" as a Social Invention. We are "cemented" into a whole by the gaze of others and political necessity.

E3.8.2_REF1_LIT  "Paradoxically, in our golden age of identity politics and trigger-ready outrage, this repression of our inner wildness and fracturing of our wholeness has taken on an inverted form, inclining toward a parody of itself. Where Walt Whitman once invited us to celebrate the glorious multitudes we each contain and to welcome the wonder that comes from discovering one another’s multitudes afresh, we now cling to our identity-fragments, using them as badges and badgering artillery in confronting the templated identity-fragments of others. (For instance, some of mine: woman, reader, immigrant, writer, queer, survivor of Communism.) Because no composite of fragments can contain, much less represent, all possible fragments, we end up drifting further and further from one another’s wholeness, abrading all sense of shared aspiration toward unbiased understanding. The censors of yore have been replaced by the “sensitivity readers” of today, fraying the fabric of freedom — of speech, even of thought — from opposite ends, but fraying it nonetheless. The safety of conformity to an old-guard mainstream has been supplanted by the safety of conformity to a new-order minority predicated on some fragment of identity, so that those within each new group (and sub-group, and sub-sub-group) are as harsh to judge and as fast to exclude “outsiders” (that is, those of unlike identity-fragments) from the conversation as the old mainstream once was in judging and excluding them. In our effort to liberate, we have ended up imprisoning — imprisoning ourselves in the fractal infinity of our ever-subdividing identities, imprisoning each other in our exponentially multiplying varieties of otherness."
(Maria Popova, Brain Pickings on John O’Donohue, Walking on the Pastures of Wonder: John O’Donohue in Conversation with John Quinn, 1997)

E3.8.2_REF2_PH "The unceasing propaganda in our time for “the individual” seems to me deeply suspect, as “individuality” itself becomes more and more a synonym for selfishness. A capitalist society comes to have a vested interest in praising “individuality” and “freedom” — which may mean little more than the right to the perpetual aggrandizement of the self, and the freedom to shop, to acquire, to use up, to consume, to render obsolete.

I don’t believe there is any inherent value in the cultivation of the self. And I think there is no culture (using the term normatively) without a standard of altruism, of regard for others. I do believe there is an inherent value in extending our sense of what a human life can be. If literature has engaged me as a project, first as a reader and then as a writer, it is as an extension of my sympathies to other selves, other domains, other dreams, other words, other territories of concern."
(Susan Sontag, “The Conscience of Words”, 2001 published in At the Same Time: Essays and Speeches)

E3.8.2_REF3_PH THE "I" AS A NARCISSISTIC EXPANSION
"NAMES:
Caritatsim:
A linguistic term referring to affectionate names given to everyday objects. Fashion magazines: "The little coat that keeps you warm." [...]

I believe the purpose of caritatisms is to elevate common nouns to the status of proper nouns, or at least suggest the process. Caritatisme individualizes the object through an affective projection. It makes the object an extension of the self and christens it as unique (the ideal status of a proper noun, as the term suggests). It denotes the ultimate difference as being irreducible; yet, the ultimate difference is me. See Corneille's Medea: In this extreme misfortune—she has slaughtered her children in retaliation against Jason, who abandoned her—what is left? —Me. In absolute seclusion, what do you have left? My pencil, my chicken, my strawberries, my chocolate macaron. The name is the name of what I love; it is my name. I only name what I love. I only name what is worth naming."
(Roland Barthes, How to Live Together: Lectures at the Collège de France, 1976–1977)

E2.2.B.3.4.d.wE3.8.2.

"THE DIALECTIC OF THE MASTER AND THE SLAVE: ACCORDING TO HEGEL, IN ORDER TO REALIZE ITS OWN SPIRITUALITY, CONSCIOUSNESS MUST BE RECOGNIZED BY ANOTHER CONSCIOUSNESS.

"About Hegel: The master is unfortunately recognized in his autonomy by a consciousness that he himself does not recognize as autonomous. Therefore, he cannot be satisfied, and his autonomy is only negative. Mastery is a dead end.
The slave, however, is not bound to his condition; he wants to change it. Unlike the master, he can educate himself. What we call history is nothing but the sequence of his long efforts to obtain real freedom."
(Albert Camus)

The dialectic of the master and the slave: This retrospective expression summarizes Chapter IV of The Phenomenology of Spirit and was popularized by Alexandre Kojève's lectures. In Hegelian vocabulary, dialectic starts from Kant's concept that it allows us to think about necessary contradictions that reason conceals when it tries to think about what is beyond its capabilities. Hegel builds on this concept, believing that it applies not only to thought, but also to reality. Reality is composed of finite things that transcend themselves. Dialectic is a process in which a thing is called upon to deny its own nature, transform itself, and become something else. In a sense, Hegel was a disciple of Heraclitus because becoming governs reality.
Hegel developed dialectics because he could not conceive of life using reason alone. For him, thinking about life was the goal of philosophy. Dialectics is an attempt to develop a conception of thought equal to life, rather than simply applying concepts to it.
In order to realize its own spirituality, consciousness must be recognized by another consciousness. It must deny its singularity to access others. Man is born twice: once as a natural being and once as a spiritual being. The dialectic of mastery and servitude is a conceptual parable describing a structure found in various contexts. This structure corresponds to a primitive scene in the prehistoric, pre-social normative state (ethical, political, etc.). In this scene, two individuals facing each other have no resources other than themselves with which to relate to each other. However, this parable should not be reduced to the experience of civilized man.
In previous chapters, Hegel describes how consciousness forgets itself in an attempt to find truth in objects in the world. Consciousness does not succeed, but it becomes aware that the world is not only given to it; the way it relates to the world also depends on itself. Consciousness becomes self-conscious, taking itself as an object in the quest for truth. However, it does not succeed because it lacks a reflection of itself in the things it desires in nature. Furthermore, the consumed object disappears. Consciousness understands that it can only grasp its own truth in a relationship of recognition—in the sense of granting value to someone—in the face of an alter ego. Recognition involves proving to the other—who knows nothing about me—that one is not a mere natural being (which disappears in consumption) by engaging in a fight to the death. "It is only by risking one's life that one preserves one's freedom." This struggle demonstrates that we are not merely animals. It is not a struggle for survival to save one's skin, as with animals, but rather a struggle to be recognized as consciousness. I am willing to risk my life to demonstrate that my value surpasses that of natural life.
To gain recognition for my actions, I need others to do the same. To show that I am superior to life, I must be recognized by someone else who does the same. First and foremost, it is a fight against oneself to save one's own skin. Both can die; it is a failure of recognition. This failure demonstrates the necessity of staying alive. Then, the relationship between master and servant is established.
One consciousness goes all the way, while the other experiences the tremor of its singularity and fears death. The master first becomes master of life before becoming master of the servant—the latter is first enslaved to life. It is a three-way relationship. The first relationship of servitude is the one retained by Nietzsche and Deleuze in Nietzsche and Philosophy. The reversal of servitude by the servant's consciousness has three stages.
1. Fear of death. The master may not have valued life at its true worth. The servant is confronted by the absolute master of death, who calls his reality into question.
2. Service to others = discipline of desire through decentering: in civil society, this leads to an economic system in which people do not work solely to satisfy their own needs.
3. Work, which is the conversion of the tremor of singularity into an experience of self-transformation through a transformation of the world: this is another way in which service liberates us from animality. Rather than suppressing the natural things around me (through consumption), I make nature a reflection of spiritual activity.
These three experiences are the basis for liberation, for reconciling oneself with the world through the creation of a state based on universality. The master is trapped in his singularity.
"While the servant works for the master, his desire takes on a dimension that consists not only of the master's desire, but also contains within itself the desire of another. The servant thus rises above singularity, which is bound to the self of his natural will, and stands, in proportion to his value, higher than the master considered in his selfishness. [...] such submission of the servant's will forms the beginning of true human freedom. The trembling of the singularity of the will, the feeling of nothingness, of selfishness, the habit of obedience is a necessary moment in the formation of every human being."
Submission to the state (in accordance with Rousseauist thinking) is not coercion but obligation. Coercion is the law of the strongest. Obligation is a natural submission to an order whose legitimacy is recognized.
(Olivier Tinland in the program Les Chemins de la Philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on May 1, 2018)

Listen to the podcast in French: 
https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/quoi-hegel-quest-ce-quil-a-hegel-24-la-dialectique-du-maitre-et-de-lesclave

E3.8.2_REF4_LIT "CIRCUMSTANCES COMPEL UNITY; FOR CONVENIENCE' SAKE A MAN MUST BE A WHOLE."
"The evening hour, too, gives us the irresponsibility which darkness and lamplight bestow," she writes. "We are no longer quite ourselves. As we step out of the house on a fine evening between four and six, we shed the self our friends know us by and become part of that vast republican army of anonymous trampers, whose society is so agreeable after the solitude of one's own room.[...]
 
Or is the true self neither this nor that, neither here nor there, but something so varied and wandering that it is only when we give the rein to its wishes and let it take its way unimpeded that we are indeed ourselves? Circumstances compel unity; for convenience' sake a man must be a whole. The good citizen when he opens his door in the evening must be banker, golfer, husband, father; not a nomad wandering the desert, a mystic staring at the sky, a debauchee in the slums of San Francisco, a soldier heading a revolution, a pariah howling with scepticism and solitude. [...]"
(Virginia Woolf, Street Haunting: A London Adventure, 1930)

E3.8.2_REF5_PH DELEUZE STATES THAT THE "PROBLEM OF THE SELF... FINDS ONLY IN CULTURE A MORAL AND POLITICAL RESOLUTION." The "I" is not a natural entity found in the brain or soul; it is a construct designed for social life.
"Can we solve the problem of the self by fulfilling Hume's hope? Now, we can define the concept of subjectivity. The subject is not a quality, but rather, the qualification of a collection of ideas. Saying that the imagination is affected by principles means that any set is qualified as a partial, actual subject. Therefore, the idea of subjectivity is the reflection of affection in the imagination; it is the general rule itself. In this case, the idea is not the object of thought or the quality of a thing; it is not representative. Rather, it is a rule, a schema, or a rule of construction. Transcending the partiality of the subject, the idea of subjectivity includes the principle and rule of possible agreement between subjects in each collection considered. Thus, the problem of the self, which has no solution at the level of understanding, finds a moral and political resolution only in culture. As we have seen, origin and affection cannot unite in a self since, at this level, the difference between principles and fantasy persists. The self is constituted by the synthesis of affection itself and its reflection: an affection that fixes the imagination and an imagination that reflects affection."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.8.2_REF5.1_PH THE REACTION OF THE MIND TO CIRCUMSTANCES "IS CALLED INVENTION." IN HUME'S PHILOSOPHY, THIS "INVENTION" IS THE CREATION OF ARTIFICE (JUSTICE, SOCIETY, CULTURE) TO REGULATE THE PASSIONS. The self is not a natural given; it is a social/institutional invention.
“What does the mind do once a subject has been chosen? It "favors certain ideas over others." Going beyond does not mean anything else. If the mind can "revive," it is undoubtedly because principles establish relationships between ideas and activate the mind by giving the impression's liveliness the laws of communication, distribution, and apportionment. A relationship between two ideas is the quality by which an impression communicates its liveliness to an idea. However, this liveliness is not a product of principles itself. It is a characteristic of the impression, the good and the given of the imagination. It is the mind's origin and its most fundamental aspect. [...]

Not all relationships are equal from the point of view of human nature. We know they do not all have the same effect of "reinforcing and enlivening our ideas," and legitimate belief necessarily passes through causality. Any relationship between two ideas is the quality by which an impression enlivens the connected idea, but the connection must be firm, constant, and unchanging. Furthermore, impressions not only force relationships; they also feign and fabricate them at will. Thus, the subject is pressured and tormented by mirages and fantasies. His passions and current dispositions lead him to believe fictions. In short, we are not only subjects; we are also something else. We are still egos, always slaves to our origins. There are illegitimate beliefs and absurd general ideas. Principles establish relationships between ideas, which, for the impression, are also laws of communication and liveliness. Yet, liveliness must conform to these laws without exception. This is why, in the schematism of knowledge, there are always excessive rules that must be corrected by other rules: the schematism of cause must conform to experience, and the schematism of the general idea must conform to space, under the dual aspect that defines it, geometric structure, arithmetic unity. Thus, a whole polemic continues in the self between the subject and fantasy, or rather in the subject itself, between the principles of human nature and the liveliness of the imagination, between principles and fictions. We know how, for each object of knowledge, fiction can be effectively corrected, even if it means being reborn with the next object. But we also know how, for the world in general in which every object is known, fiction takes hold of principles and radically puts them at its service. [...]
This is because she and he are reflected in the imagination, in fantasy. The principles of passion do not fix the mind without the passions resounding, spreading, and reflecting in the mind. The mind's reaction to the totality of circumstances is one with this reflection of passion in the mind; such a reaction is productive, such a reflection is called invention."
(Gilles Deleuze, Empiricism and Subjectivity, 1977)

E3.8.2_REF6_PH  I_What is sympathy according to Hume

"PERSONAL IDENTITY IS A SOCIAL CONSTRUCT GENERATED BY CERTAIN PASSIONS... THERE IS NO SELF INDEPENDENT OF OTHERS."
"Hume does not oppose reason to passions. Rather, he subordinates understanding to the impulsive movements that passions give to human action. In fact, he says that "reason is, and ought only to be, the slave of the passions." Passion is the primary force that motivates action; all actions stem from emotion. Understanding merely indicates the means to satisfy desire.
 
Passions also play a role in uniting people. They socialize us, including passions that, at first glance, seem to be antagonistic to social bonds, such as envy and malice. Hume conceives of social bonds not in terms of contracts but in terms of the circulation of emotions. According to him, personal identity is fictitious; there is no self independent of others. Personal identity is a social construct generated by certain passions thanks to the principle of sympathy, which we now call empathy, or the ability to feel the emotions of others. Thus, our subjectivities are open to one another; the mind is merely a theater in which emotions pass without a substantial unifying force. Passions such as pride, humility, love, and hate produce the idea of the self.

The following is an excerpt from A Treatise of Human Nature, Volume 2, which presents a portrait of a man who is inherently social and offers a critical response to Hobbes's position that man is a wolf to man:
"In all creatures, that prey not upon others, and are not agitated with violent passions, there appears a remarkable desire of company, which associates them together, without any
advantages they can ever propose to reap from their union. This is still more conspicuous in man, as being the creature of the universe, who has the most ardent desire of society, and is fitted for it by the most advantages. We can form no wish, which has not a reference to society. A perfect solitude is, perhaps, the greatest punishment we can suffer. Every pleasure languishes when enjoyed a-part from company, and every pain becomes more cruel and intolerable. Whatever other passions we may be actuated by; pride, ambition, avarice, curiosity, revenge or lust; the soul or animating principle of them all is sympathy; nor would they have any force, were we to abstract entirely from the thoughts and sentiments of others. Let all the powers and elements of nature conspire to serve and obey one man: Let the sun rise and set at his command: The sea and rivers roll as he pleases, and the earth furnish spontaneously
whatever may be useful or agreeable to him: He will still be miserable, till you give him some one person at least, with whom he may share his happiness, and whose esteem and friendship he may enjoy."

Since understanding is merely theater, the most vivid impressions enliven the mind. These impressions are most vivid in interaction with others because it is through interaction that we constitute ourselves.
On a political level, this leads him to believe that we can think of a natural genesis, a natural history, of the institution based on the principles of human nature without causing a revolution that would bring about a rupture. The break between a state of nature and a state of law makes no sense to an empiricist philosophy. How could one conceive of the institution of a state in a state of nature, where one lives in isolation? For H., it is the principle of sympathy that will be refined, starting from the family unit and evolving toward the formation of the state in stages through conventions and inventions. Sympathy, restricted to the family, broadens in the context of war between clans. At this point, we must invent a new artifice to overcome the impasse. We see a self-correction of sympathy based on experience within the family. Conventions can be established without legal institutions. People broaden their perspective based on the satisfaction of their family. We overcome partiality by conversing with those who are most different from us through the contagion of passions."
(Céline Bonicco-Donato in the program Les Chemins de la Philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on October, 17, 2019)

Listen to the podcast in French: https://www.radiofrance.fr/franceculture/podcasts/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/faut-il-preferer-la-destruction-du-monde-a-l-egratignure-de-son-doigt-9500672

E3.8.2_REF7_PH  A_Gustave Courbet_Charles Baudelaire_1848-1849

THE DANDY DOES NOT EXIST IN A VACUUM; HE EXISTS ONLY "BY FINDING [HIS EXISTENCE] IN THE EXPRESSION OF OTHERS' FACES."
"Much more than the cult of the individual, romanticism inaugurates the cult of living are still capable in a world dedicated to death, romantic rebellion looked for a solution in the attitude that it itself assumed. The attitude assembled, in aesthetic unity, all mankind who were in the hands of fate and about to be destroyed by divine violence. The human being who is condemned to death is, at least, magnificent before he disappears, and his magnificence is his justification. It is an established fact, the only one that can be thrown in the petrified face of the God of hate. The impassive rebel does not flinch before the eyes of God. "Nothing," says Milton, "will change this determined mind, this high disdain born of an offended conscience." Everything is drawn or rushes toward the void, but even though man is humiliated, he is obstinate and at least preserves his pride. A baroque romantic, discovered by Raymond Queneau, claims that the aim of all intellectual life is to become God. This romantic is really a little ahead of his time. The aim, at that time, was only to equal God and remain on His level. He is not destroyed, but by incessant effort He is refused any act of submission. Dandyism is a degraded form of asceticism.
The dandy creates his own unity by aesthetic means. But it is an aesthetic of singularity and of negation. "To live and die before a mirror": that, according to Baudelaire, was the dandy's slogan. It is indeed a coherent slogan. The dandy is, by occupation, always in opposition. He can only exist by defiance. Up to now man derived his coherence from his Creator. But from the moment that he consecrates his rupture with Him, he finds himself delivered over to the fleeting moment, to the passing days, and to wasted sensibility. Therefore he must take himself in hand. The dandy rallies his forces and creates a unity for himself by the very violence of his refusal. Profligate, like all people without a rule of life, he is coherent as an actor. But an actor implies a public; the dandy can only play a part by setting himself up in opposition. He can only be sure of his own existence by finding it in the expression of others' faces. Other people are his mirror. A mirror that
quickly becomes clouded, it is true, since human capacity for attention is limited. It must be ceaselessly stimulated, spurred on by provocation. The dandy, therefore, is always compelled to astonish. Singularity is his vocation, excess his way to perfection. Perpetually incomplete, always on the fringe of things, he compels others to create him, while denying their values. He plays at life because he is unable to live it. He plays at it until he dies, except for the moments when he is alone and without a mirror. For the dandy, to be alone is not to exist. The romantics talked so grandly about solitude only because it was their real horror, the one thing they could not bear."
(Albert Camus, The Rebel, An Essay on Man in Revolt, 1951)

E2.2.A.1.2.a.iv.wE3.8.2. "[Persons]
A person’s roles and his place in the narrative devolve from the choices that place him in a structural system related to others. The person thus comes to stand behind his roles, to select them and to be judged by his choices and his capacities to act out his personae in a total structure that is the unfolding of his drama.

The idea of a person is the idea of a unified center of choice and action, the unit of legal and theological responsibility. Having chosen, a person acts, and so is actionable, liable. It is in the idea of action that the legal and the theatrical sources of the concept of person come together. [...]

If judgment summarizes a life … then that life must have a unified location. Since they choose from their natures or are chosen by their stories, neither characters nor figures need be equipped with a will, not to mention a free will… The actions of characters and figures do no emerge from the exercise of a single faculty of power: there is no need for a single source of responsibility… Persons are required to unify the capacity for choice with the capacities for action. [...]

It is the intentions, the capacities for choice rather than the total configuration of traits which defines the person. Here the stage is set for identity crises, for wondering who one really is, behind the multifold variety of actions and roles. And the search for that core person is not a matter of curiosity; it is a search for the principles by which choices are to be made."
(Amélie Rorty, The Identities of Persons, 1976)

E2.2.A.1.2.a.v.wE3.8.2. "[Selves]
The two strands that were fused in the concept of person diverge again: When we focus on persons as sources of decisions, the ultimate locus of responsibility, the unity of thought and action, we must come to think of them as souls and minds. When we think of them as possessors of rights and powers, we come to think of them as selves. It is not until each of these has been transformed into the concept of individuality that the two strands are woven together again. […]

When a society has changed so that individuals acquire their rights by virtue of their powers, rather than having their powers defined by their rights, the concept of person has been transformed to a concept of self… The quality of an individual self is determined by his qualities: they are his capital, to invest well or foolishly. [...]

Because persons are primary agents of principle, their integrity requires freedom; because they are judged liable, their powers must be autonomous. But when this criterion for personhood is carried to its logical extreme, the scope of agency moves inward, away from social dramas, to the choices of the soul, or to the operations of the mind. […]

From character as structured dispositions, we come to soul as pure agency, unfathomable, inexpressible. [...]

Judgments of persons are moral; judgments of souls are theological; judgments of selves are economic and political. Societies of persons are constructed to assure the rights of choice and action; they emerge from a contract of agents; societies of selves are also formed to protect and guarantee the rights of their members. But when the members of a society achieve their rights by virtue of their possessions, the protection of rights requires the protection of property, even though in principle everyone is equally entitled to the fruits of his labors and protection under law. […]

The concerns of selves are their interests; their obligations are the duties with which they are taxed or charged. The grammar and the semantics of selfhood reveal the possessive forms. Whatever will come to be regarded as crucial property, or the means to it, will be regarded as the focus of rights; the alienation of property becomes an attack on the integrity if not actually the preservation of the self. [...]

The conscious possession of experiences [is] the final criterion of identity. The continuity of the self is established by memory; disputes about the validity of memory reports will hang on whether the claimant had as hers the original experience. Puzzles about identity will be described as puzzles about whether it is possible to transfer, or to alienate memory (that is, the retention of one’s own experience) without destroying the self. [...]

There is difficulty in describing the core possessor, the owner of experiences who is not herself any set of them. One can speak of characters as sets of traits without looking for a center; but it is more difficult to think of bundles of properties without an owner, especially when the older idea of the person as an agent and decision-maker is still implicit. It is presumed that the self as an owner is also endowed with capabilities to choose and to act."
(Amélie Rorty, The Identities of Persons, 1976)

E3.8.3_REF1_CUR A_Mame-Diarra Niang_Morphologie du rêve n6_2021

THE SELF IS “A TERRITORY MADE UP OF WELL-PRESERVED MEMORIES AND ERASURES”
“In her recent photographs, French artist and photographer Mame-Diarra Niang explores the identity of black bodies, rejecting any attempt at definition or narration based on centuries of Western representation. She seeks to abstract this identity through what she describes as forms of non-portraiture.

In this series, begun during lockdown, the artist photographs and re-photographs her screen, with each new shot blurring the outlines further, to the point of transforming these bodies into patches of color. Little by little, subjects disappear, the self dissolves in this territory made up of well-preserved memories that blur, "like a territory made up of well-preserved memories and erasures".
(Curatorship: Musée de l’Orangerie, Paris)
