E3.8.3_REF4_PH  I_Confirmation bias

"Jean-Paul Sartre's Being and Nothingness is sometimes described as the bible of existentialism. At its core is the notion of Bad Faith. For this episode of the Philosophy Bites podcast Sebastian Gardner, author of a recent book about Being and Nothingness, explains what Sartre meant by Bad Faith: mostly a condition of self-deception.
In the example of the café waiter, he identifies him as immersing himself in his role and his function in a way that brings him an illicit satisfaction - experiencing himself as a thing, an object with determinate properties, denying that he is free. He is playing the game of automation of consciousness.
In the vignette of the man trying to seduce a woman in a café, she is treating her hand as an object when she lets the man touch it without withdrawing - this serves as a self-deception strategy since she has not yet decided if she wants to go further in the relationship - she puts off to later that decision by becoming mindless of her body.
In the vignette of the homosexual debating with himself if he should acknowledge his past an call himself an homosexual or should disavow the facts and believe he can change - he illustrates that our past in some sense does determine what we are and also that we are free to reinvent ourselves. Sartre expresses this paradox saying the human subject's consciousness is non self-identical. How is it possible to exhibit yourself a certain way up to a certain point and yet to retain the capacity to change course? This capacity is grounded on the 2 dimensions of facticity and transcendence. Facticity includes the heritage of culture but also of on'es previous actions. Transcendence is the capacity to go beyond facticity.
Bad faith as the term is used by Sartre is not a moral term, but it is an ethical concept to convince us to operate a practical reorientation: it is not a descriptive metaphysics, it is a revisionary one (he is not describing the way the world is empirically, his philosophy has a near religious dimension to it). He is opposed to common sense in his system."
(Sebastian Gardner on Jean-Paul Sartre on Bad Faith;, Philosophy Bites)

Listen to the episode: http://philosophybites.com/2009/02/sebastian-gardner-on-jeanpaul-sartre-on-bad-faith.html

E3.8.3_REF5_CUR A_Maria Lassnig_Self-Portrait Under Plastic_1972 ; A_Maria Lassnig_Lady with brain_circa 1990-1999 /Maria Lassnig/

"The sensational « Self-Portrait Under Plastic » by the revolutionary Austrian-born artist Maria Lassnig, offers a great look into the artist's major investigation of the self. Together with her close observation of the physical presence of the body, Lassnig described this introspective process and state as « body awareness ». This aspect of her practice remained key for the development of her work, along with constant explorations of painting in relation to her internal world, whilst prioritizing how this might feel rather than look. She considered her inner world full of endless possibilities especially when compared to expressing external realities which she perceived as holding less potential.
In a way, she considered the external eventually becoming part of an introspective and predominant process. With her psychologically-charged « Self-Portrait Under Plastic » she precisely puts into question this illusion of boundary. She does this not only in regards to the relationship between interior and exterior but by using the plastic on her face as a way of reflecting upon the medium of painting itself: its limitations, illusionistic properties and how these may relate to the way we might at times perceive ourselves and the world around us."
(Ambar Quijano)

E3.8.3_REF6_PH MONTAIGNE TREATS HIS SOCIAL IDENTITY AS A STRANGER OR A GARMENT TO BE WORN AND REMOVED, THEREBY PROTECTING HIS INNER "CITADEL."
"All that seems relevant now and occupies my thoughts on Montaigne today is this: how, in a time so reminiscent of our own, did he liberate himself inwardly and how, in reading him, can we fortify ourselves by his example? In him I see the ancestor, the protector and the friend of each “homme libre” on earth, the most adept master at this new yet eternal science, the preserving of oneself above all other concerns. Few men on earth have ever fought with such faithfulness and tenacity to preserve their most intimate selves, their “essences”, from all impurities, from all toxins left by the rank spume of an epoch’s storm waves, and fewer still have managed to rescue from the time in which they lived, and for all time, their deepest selves.

This struggle that led Montaigne to safeguard his interior freedom, a struggle that in his case was surely more conscious and more determined than that of any other human being, had nothing heroic or sententious about it. It would be an injustice to include Montaigne with that group of poets and thinkers who, armed only with the word, fought for the “liberté de l’homme”. He has nothing of the forceful tirades or lofty outbursts of a Schiller or a Byron, nothing of the pugnacity of a Voltaire. He may have permitted himself a rueful smile at the idea of wanting to impose something as deeply personal as inner freedom on individuals, let alone on the masses, and he profoundly detested professional reformers, theoreticians and peddlers of ideology. He knew only too well the colossal task represented by this simple idea: to safeguard one’s independence at the core of oneself. His fight limits itself to the defensive, to defend this most intimate bastion, which Goethe named the “citadel”, where entry is forbidden to all other men. His tactic was to be as inconspicuous as possible, to attract the minimum of attention through outward appearance, to travel the world as if wearing a mask, to seek out only the path that would lead to himself.

Thus, Montaigne does not provide what one might term a biography. He never offended anyone, because he never pushed himself forward, never sought an audience or endorsement. Seen from the outside, he appeared to be a model citizen, a functionary, a married man, a Catholic, a man who discreetly paid his dues to the world. For his entourage, he assumed the mask of self-effacement so as to bloom inside himself, wondering at the nuances, the play of different colours over his soul. He was at every moment disposed to lend, never to give. Always, whatever his mode of living, he kept for himself alone the most authentic and most subtle element of his being. He left the rest to prattle on, to move with the herd, to get borne aloft, to preach and parade; he left the world to follow its chaotic crazed paths and only concerned himself with one thing: to be rational within himself, to remain human in an inhuman time, to remain free in the vortex of pandemonium. He let them have their say, those who mockingly accused him of indifference, indecision and cowardice; he let others relish their surprise at seeing him relinquish his duties and honours. His nearest and dearest, who knew him best, never doubted the perseverance, the clearsightedness and the subtlety with which, in the shadow of public affairs, he applied himself to the sole aim to which he was committed: to live his own life, and not simply to live."
(Stefan Zweig, Montaigne, 1942)

E3.8.3_REF7_LIT "THE OTHER ONE, THE ONE CALLED BORGES, IS THE ONE THINGS HAPPEN TO. I walk through the streets of Buenos Aires and stop for a moment, perhaps mechanically now, to look at the arch of an entrance hall and the grillwork on the gate; I know of Borges from the mail and see his name on a list of professors or in a biographical dictionary. I like hourglasses, maps, eighteenth-century typography, the taste of coffee and the prose of Stevenson; he shares these preferences, but in a vain way that turns them into the attributes of an actor. It would be an exaggeration to say that ours is a hostile relationship; I live, let myself go on living, so that Borges may contrive his literature, and this literature justifies me. It is no effort for me to confess that he has achieved some valid pages, but those pages cannot save me, perhaps because what is good belongs to no one, not even to him, but rather to the language and to tradition. Besides, I am destined to perish, definitively, and only some instant of myself can survive in him. Little by little, I am giving over everything to him, though I am quite aware of his perverse custom of falsifying and magnifying things. Spinoza knew that all things long to persist in their being; the stone eternally wants to be a stone and the tiger a tiger. I shall remain in Borges, not in myself (if it is true that I am someone), but I recognize myself less in his books than in many others or in the laborious strumming of a guitar. Years ago I tried to free myself from him and went from the mythologies of the suburbs to the games with time and infinity, but those games belong to Borges now and I shall have to imagine other things. Thus my life is a flight and I lose everything and everything belongs to oblivion, or to him.
I do not know which of us has written this page."
(Jorge Luis Borges, Borges and I, Labyrinths)

E3.8.3_REF8_LIT TO ENDURE LIFE, POWYS ARGUES WE MUST SPLIT: WE MUST PLAY THE PART WHILE SIMULTANEOUSLY WATCHING OURSELVES PLAY IT. We become the audience to our own social fiction, finding "consolation" in the distance between the actor (the social self) and the awareness watching it.

“I think that down at the bottom of my being has been concealed from pretty early days a deep distrust and a shrewd suspicion of that solidity of the objective world that most people take for granted. I was always superstitious—at one time I even believed in Hell—but I am pretty sure that the more I became true to my subtler instincts the more I regarded the whole astronomical universe with a certain detachment, regarded it in fact as the mere material stage for playing whatever romantic, picturesque, or fantastical rôle a person’s life-illusion might arbitrarily select. It has always appealed to me — that legendary last word of Cesar Augustus: 

“Have I not played the Farce well? Put out the lights: ring down the curtain! Plaudite et vale! “ 

Deep, deep, deep must have sunken into my soul that grandiose acting in the presence of the gods and of Fate, of those classic Greeks and Romans! My mania for the sceptical Shakespeare, who like his own more sceptical master Montaigne, had a passion for these consummate actors, encouraged this predilection in me. Oh, how I revelled in every line of his Julius Cesar; and how startled I was when, in reading Dante, I found Brutus and Cassius thrust down to the bottom of Hell! 

At a very early age somebody read to me Church’s Stories  from Homer, and I found out that those more primeval and more elemental heroes were just as addicted to this vision of themselves as acting a spectacular part before gods and men, whether in exultation or desperation, as were the Greeks and Romans of the historical times. Thus I learned at a very early age what a consolation in life it is to enjoy things and to endure things as an actor; that is to say, not hugger-mugger, and in brute unconsciousness, but as though in default of any god or even of any fellow mortal, you could play out your part before your own awareness, and be to the end both performer and audience! “
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934, Faber and Faber, p. 121-122)

E3.8.3_REF9_CUR A_Sasha Gordon_Garden Troll_2021

EUPHORIC NARRATIVES THAT RANGE FROM THE FAMILIAR TO THE UNCANNY.
"Born in The Bronx, Sasha has described herself as ‘biracial,’ given her Korean and Polish descent. She shares that a sense of belonging didn’t come easily for her whilst « feeling out of touch with her Asian identity until recently », and that growing up around a predominantly white and heteronormative neighborhood hindered her confusion further with being both queer and biracial.
« After years of being uncomfortable of my appearance and culture, I want to make up for lost time » later adding that she feels like she has to be « a certain type of Asian woman, a certain type of queer person, a certain type of body size ». Describing herself as being « hyper-aware » of her experiences, it is through this hyper-awareness that she closely examines compositional details and revisits her memories to develop intimate and at times euphoric narratives that range from the familiar to the uncanny."

E3.8.3_REF10_PH WE BECOME STRANGERS TO OUR IMMEDIATE REALITY BECAUSE WE ARE TOO BUSY MANAGING THE "BURDEN OF SUBJECTIVITY" AND THE PERFORMANCE OF OUR SOCIAL ROLES.
"As I have suggested, the diagnosis presupposed in Metatheatre that modern man lives with an increasing burden of subjectivity, at the expense of his sense of the reality of the world - is not new. Nor do works for the theater constitute the main texts which disclose this attitude and its correlative idea, reason as self-manipulation and role-playing. The two greatest documents of this attitude are Montaigne's Essays and Machiavelli's The Prince - both manuals of strategy which assume a gulf between the "public self" (the role) and the "private self" (the true self). The value of Abel's book lies in the forthright application of this diagnosis to the drama. He is quite right, for example, in arguing that most of the plays of Shakespeare which their author, and everyone else since, have called tragedies are not, strictly speaking, tragedies at all. In fact, Abel could have gone even further. Not only are most of the putative tragedies really "metaplays"; so are most of the histories and comedies. The principal plays of Shakespeare are plays about self-consciousness, about characters not acting so much as dramatizing themselves in roles. Prince Hal is the man of perfected self-consciousness and self-control, triumphing over the man of rash, unself-conscious integrity, Hotspur, and over the sentimental, cowardly, self-conscious man of pleasure, Falstaff. Achilles and Oedipus do not see themselves as, but are, hero and king. But Hamlet and Henry V see themselves as acting parts - the part of the avenger, the part of the heroic and confident king leading his troops to battle. Shakespeare's fondness for the play-within-a-play and for putting his characters into disguise for long stretches of the story clearly partakes of the style of metatheater. From Prospero to the Police Chief in Genet's The Balcony, the personages of metatheater are characters in search of an action."
(Susan Sontag, The death of tragedy, 1963)

E3.8.3_REF11_CUR A_Lynn Hershman Leeson_Roberta Construction Chart number 1_1975

"Lynn Hershman Leeson created, performed and documented an alter ego, Roberta Breitmore from 1973 to 1978 to 'focus on the blurriness between fiction and reality'. During her four-year existence, Roberta Breitmore had a bank account and library card, rented an apartment and went on dates. She did not plan for the character to be so developed but 'when you start to define how you make somebody real, she needs to be fleshed out and have a history. Roberta Construction Chart #1 gives detailed instructions on how to create Roberta's appearance. It doubles as a visualisation of 'femininity as masquerade, highlighting the tropes and traits that tend to be associated with femininity."
(Curatorship: Tate Modern, London)

E3.8.3_REF12_CUR  V_Mathilde ter Heijne_Mathilde, Mathilde_2000 ; V_Mathilde ter Heijne_Number one_2002 /Mathilde ter Heijne/

MATHILDE TER HEIJNE USES A "DOUBLE" (OFTEN A MANNEQUIN IN HER WORK) TO INHABIT SPECIFIC ROLES, SUCH AS THE "VICTIM." SHE EXPRESSES A "SIMULTANEOUS IDENTIFICATION WITH AND REJECTION OF THE ROLE."
"The tension inherent in ter Heijne’s prominent use of the double, as already employed in earlier installations, such as ‘Mathilde, Mathilde…’ (2000) clearly reaches its climax in 'Number One' (2002). E2.2.A.1.2.a.ii.wE3.8.3. "[Characters]
Since the elements out of which characters are composed are repeatable and their configurations can be reproduced, a society of characters is in principle a society of repeatable and indeed replaceable individuals.  [...]
To know what sort of character a person is, is to know what sort of life is best suited to bring out his potentialities and functions… Not all characters are suited to the same sorts of lives: there is no ideal type for them all… If one tries to force the life of a bargainer on the character of a philosopher, one is likely to encounter trouble, sorrow, and the sort of evil that comes from mismatching life and temperament. Characters formed within one society and living in circumstances where their dispositions are no longer needed — characters in time of great social change — are likely to be tragic. Their virtues lie useless or even foiled; they are no longer recognized for what they are; their motives and actions are misunderstood. The magnanimous man in a petty bourgeois society is seen as a vain fool; the energetic and industrious man in a society that prizes elegance above energy is seen as a bustling boor; the meditative person in an expansive society is seen as melancholic… Two individuals of the same character will fare differently in different polities, not because their characters will change through their experiences (though different aspects will become dominant or recessive) but simply because a good fit of character and society can conduce to well-being and happiness, while a bad fit produces misery and rejection. [...]


To be a character” is to maintain a few qualities, nourish them to excess until they dominate and dictate all others. A character is delineated and thus generally delimited. To “have character” is to have reliable qualities, to hold tightly to them through the temptations to swerve and change. A person of character is neither bribed nor corrupted; he stands fast, is steadfast. […]

Because characters are public persons, even their private lives can have universal form, general significance. The dramatic character, writ large, can represent for everyman what only later came to be thought of as the inner life of some; it can portray the myth, the conflicts, reversals and discoveries of each person, each polis."
(Amélie Rorty, The Identities of Persons, 1976)

E2.2.A.1.2.a.iii.wE3.8.3. "[Figures]
A figure is neither formed by nor owns experiences: his figurative identity shapes the significances of the events in his life. […]

Individuals who regard themselves as figures watch the unfolding of their lives following the patterns of their archetypes… They form the narratives of their lives and make their choices according to the pattern…

In contrast with the wholly external perspective on characters, the concept of a figure introduces the germ of what will become a distinction between the inner and the outer person. An individual’s perspective on his model, his idealized real figure, is originally externally presented, but it becomes internalized, becomes the internal model of self-representation."
(Amélie Rorty, The Identities of Persons, 1976)

E2.2.A.1.2.a.vi.wE3.8.3. "[Individuals]
From the tensions in the definition of the alienable properties of selves, and from the corruptions in societies of selves — the divergence of practice from ideological commitments — comes the invention of individuality. It begins with conscience and ends with consciousness.

Unlike characters and figures, individuals actively resist typing: they represent the universal mind of rational beings, or the unique private voice. Individuals are indivisible entities… Invented as a preserve of integrity, an autonomous ens, an individual transcends and resists what is binding and oppressive in society and does so from an original natural position. Although in its inception, individuality revives the idea of person, the rights of persons are formulated in society, while the rights of individuals are demanded of society. The contrast between the inner and outer person becomes the contrast between the individual and the social mask, between nature and culture.

A society of individuals is quite different from one composed of selves. Individuals contract to assure the basic rights to the development of moral and intellectual gifts, as well as legal protection of self and property. Because a society of individuals is composed of indivisible autonomous units, from whose natures — their minds and conscience — come the principles of justice, their rights are not property; they cannot be exchanged, bartered. Their rights and their qualities are their very essence, inalienable. [...]

Being an individual requires having a room of one’s own, not because it is one’s possession, but because only there, in solitude, away from the pressure of others, can one develop the features and styles that differentiate one’s own being from others. Integrity comes to be associated with difference; this idea, always implicit in individuality, of preserving one’s right against the encroachment of others within one’s own society, emerges as dominant… Conscientious consciousness is then the transparent eye that illuminates the substance of social life."
(Amélie Rorty, The Identities of Persons, 1976)

E3.9. "De-coincidence": The Liberation of "Standing Outside". True self-awareness is the act of letting go of our fixed "story lines."

E3.9.1. The Trap of the "Story Line": Coincidence as Stagnation. How the Western/Greek obsession with "essence," "definition," and the construction of a fixed "Ego" or "story line" creates a sclerosis of the self.

E3.9.1_REF1_PH “WE CANNOT BE IN THE PRESENT AND RUN OUR STORY LINES AT THE SAME TIME, […] In fact, anyone who stands on the edge of the unknown, fully in the present without reference point, experiences groundlessness”. We should cultivate intimacy with fear, as this courageous acceptance ultimately dissolves our dramas and opens us to truth.
“Intimacy with Fear
Fear is a natural reaction to moving closer to the truth.
Embarking on the spiritual journey is like getting into a very small boat and setting out on the ocean to search for unknown lands. With wholehearted practice comes inspiration, but sooner or later we will also encounter fear. For all we know, when we get to the horizon, we are going to drop off the edge of the world. Like all explorers, we are drawn to discover what's waiting out there without knowing yet if we have the courage to face it.
If we become interested in Buddhism and decide to find out what it has to offer, we'll soon discover that there are different slants on how we can proceed. With insight meditation we begin practicing mindfulness, being fully present with all our activities and thoughts. With Zen practice we hear teachings on emptiness and are challenged to connect with the open, unbounded clarity of mind. The vajrayana teachings introduce us to the notion of working with the energy of all situations, seeing whatever arises as inseparable from the awakened state. Any of these approaches might hook us and fuel our enthusiasm to explore further, but if we want to go beneath the surface and practice without hesitation, it is inevitable that at some point we will experience fear.
Fear is a universal experience. Even the smallest insect feels it. We wade in the tidal pools and put our finger near the soft, open bodies of sea anemones and they close up. Everything spontaneously does that. It's not a terrible thing that we feel fear when faced with the unknown. It is part of being alive, something we all share. We react against the possibility of loneliness, of death, of .not having anything to hold on to. Fear is a natural reaction to moving closer to the truth. 
If we commit ourselves to staying right where we are, then our experience becomes very vivid. Things become very clear when there is nowhere to escape. During a long retreat, I had what seemed to me the earthshaking revelation that we cannot be in the present and run our story lines at the same time! It sounds pretty obvious, I know, but when you discover something like this for yourself, it changes you. Impermanence becomes vivid in the present moment; so do compassion and wonder and courage. And so does fear. In fact, anyone who stands on the edge of the unknown, fully in the present without reference point, experiences groundlessness. That's when our understanding goes deeper, when we find that the present moment is a pretty vulnerable place and that this can be completely unnerving and completely tender at the same time.
When we begin our exploration, we have all kinds of ideals and expectations. We are looking for answers that will satisfy a hunger we've felt for a very long time. But the last thing we want is a further introduction to the boogeyman. Of course, people do try to warn us. I remember when I first received meditation instruction, the woman told me the technique and guidelines on how to practice and then said, "But please don't go away from here thinking that meditation is a vacation from irritation." Somehow all the warnings in the world don't quite convince us. In fact they draw us closer.
What we're talking about is getting to know fear, becoming familiar with fear, looking it right in the eye-not as a way to solve problems, but as a complete undoing of old ways of seeing, hearing, smelling, tasting, and thinking. The truth is that when we really begin to do this, we're going to be continually humbled. There's not going to be much room for the arrogance that holding on to ideals can bring. The arrogance that inevitably does arise is going to be continually shot down by our own courage to step forward a little further.
The kinds of discoveries that are made through practice have nothing to do with believing in anything. They have much more to do with having the courage to die, the courage to die continually. Instructions on mindfulness or emptiness or working with energy all point to the same thing: being right on the spot nails us. It nails us right to the point of time and space that we are in. When we stop there and don't act out, don't repress, don't blame it on anyone else, and also don't blame it on ourselves, then we meet with an open-ended question that has no conceptual answer. We also encounter our heart. As one student so eloquently put it, "Buddha nature, cleverly disguised as fear, kicks our ass into being receptive. [...]
To stay with that shakiness-to stay with a broken heart, with a rumbling stomach, with the feeling of hopelessness and wanting to get revenge-that is the path of true awakening. Sticking with that uncertainty, getting the knack of relaxing in the midst of chaos, learning not to panic-this is the spiritual path. Getting the knack of catching ourselves, of gently and compassionately catching ourselves, is the path of the warnor. We catch ourselves one zillion times as once again, whether we like it or not, we harden into resentment, bitterness, righteous indignation-harden in any way, even into a sense of relief, a sense of inspiration."
(Pema Chodron, When Things Fall Apart Heart Advice for Difficult Times, 1996)

E3.9.1_REF2_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN CONTRASTS THE GREEK PHILOSOPHICAL FIXATION ON THE ASSIGNABLE—WHERE "BEING" AND KNOWLEDGE DEPEND ON ESTABLISHING FIXED LIMITS AND IDENTITIES—with the evasive, an alternative mode of effectiveness that operates productively precisely by escaping definition, remaining unconfined, and dissolving the rigid consistency of the "self."

"Evasive (vs. assignable)"
1. If there is one Greek concept rooted in ontology or, as I would call it, a 'common ground' within which the Greeks thought, it is the idea that the more something is determined, the more it 'is'; determination creates 'being.' This 'term,' forming a limit by marking boundaries, saves us from the inconsistency of the unlimited apeiron (in Philebus). Thus, the indefinite is simultaneously that which has no limit or end and that which is vague, undecided, and uncertain, resulting in a diluted sense of being. It is between marked beginnings and ends, arche-telos, cut out as singular beings, that everything takes on consistency and constitutes itself as essence-presence, ousia. Consequently, Greek thought's defining feature is assigning and conferring on each thing its theoretical place, "in itself," which establishes its relevance and forms its "property." This means it can be a "self" in relation to itself, "in itself as itself" (hauto kath'hauto), detached from the rest of being, and stable and circumscribed by its quality. Even if it is volatile or subtle, such as an exhalation, a draft of air, or foam, the phenomenon possesses a "nature" and therefore a definite specificity in the eyes of the Greeks. However difficult it may be to identify or define, there remains a "that" with its own unity, which gives it a foundation as an entity, no matter the relationship, atmosphere, or movement.
Let us say that the Greeks had this fixation, in the various senses of the word. Rigorous knowledge, as knowledge of identity, is only possible to the extent that it is both limited and attributed, thus allowing for predication. Logos, or discourse-reason, is only legitimate as a segment determined by its beginning and end. It is the segment between what must be posited first as a "principle," even if it is axiomatic and cannot be questioned, and what must be posited as its end. This end is both its goal and limit, which cannot be exceeded. Without this limit, the discourse is "vain." According to Aristotle, it is necessary to "stand" between the two and "stop" there. The formula, by its insistence, is symptomatic of Greek anxiety in the face of the boundless. Thus, knowing is first determining, in which "thinking" and "being" are considered logically coextensive and overlapping, according to Parmenides's great statement. If there is something unassignable within Greek thought, should it still be assigned? This place is not a place but a universal receptacle, a "third kind" situated between the intelligible model and its copy in the sensible. In this "first this," a formless kind because it is indeterminate, each thing is cut out to be this or that by determining itself and affirming its quality (khôra in the Timaeus). Otherwise, this unassignable refers us to delirium, mania, divine pneuma, or a Good "beyond" essence itself, transcending all apprehension. Between these two possibilities lies the zone of relevance based on the definition of identity and "Being" as knowledge. As much as there is 'being,' there is possible assignment: the 'Ideas' themselves have their 'proper place,' the 'topos ton eidon,' that 'there,' standing out in the celestial distance from the plain of truth.
However, contrary to our intellectual habits, we must think backwards. There is another mode of "reality," or rather, of effectiveness. It is no longer "being," but evanescent—escaping a limiting grasp, yet operating indefinitely. In other words, it is a way of softening one's sentence (one's thought): dissipating and influencing, vanishing and invading, ambient and pregnant at the same time. We will have to make gradual shifts in our language to accommodate it and open up a register of the "evasive," which is the opposite of the assignable. Although "evasive" is commonly negative in French, as it is confronted with the prevailing requirement for determination — an evasive answer, as we know, is one that shies away, evades, and does not take responsibility — we must learn to reverse this choice to see what we miss in our experience. This choice is not only theoretical, but also, as Nietzsche would say, of our moral code of knowledge. The evasive is unconfined and capable of not being limited; it remains in expansion. It is the emptying out of a "self" and is therefore free from the consistency of a delimitable, appropriable "self" ("self as self"). If the characteristic of thought about Being is assigning, then moving beyond thought about Being is thinking about what can be productive in the unassignability of the elusive.
(François Jullien, Chinese thought in relation to philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.1_REF3_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN ARGUES THAT THE CONCEPT OF "COHERENCE" ALLOWS US TO BREAK FREE FROM THE WESTERN, ARISTOTELIAN RELIANCE ON FIXED "ESSENCE" AND "MEANING" by establishing a rationality based solely on "consistency"—the internal relationships that make things hold together—rather than on an underlying metaphysical substance.

"Coherence vs. Meaning"
2. [...] By establishing, from the point of view of logos, that 'a word means something and means only one thing,' and therefore meaning has unity, Aristotle establishes the purpose of speech as expressing the stable unity of things and speaking in terms of 'being.' He also institutes 'onto' logically. This unity of 'being' establishes the identity of the meaning of words, which Aristotle will call 'essence' or 'ousia.' Europe will base the condition of possibility of its knowledge on this essence, fixing it by 'definition.' Given how ingrained and global this concept is, the point of view of coherence seems crucial to me, precisely because of the bifurcation it opens up. It finally takes us out of the essentialist perspective of knowledge and introduces another way of describing things.
We cannot forget how much effort philosophy has made to break out of the essentialist perspective, recognizing its limitations but unable to make a new start. Phenomenology itself struggled with this, often being condemned to endless adjustments that still did not lift it out of this initial perspective established by language and grammar. However, the interest in thinking about a principle of coherence is that the unity is no longer the supposed unity of meaning and essence. Rather, it is a "holding together" that operates solely on the basis of internal relationships without the support of being and "substance." This ensures what I will call "consistency" — sticking to this purely associative cum ("with").  The connection alone makes things "hold together" by virtue of its power of correlation. This connection is comprehensible and establishes a rationality that rivals meaning. There is no need to imagine or invoke the base, the "under" (of "sub"-stance), the "behind," or the "beyond" (of metaphysics), the support, or the foundation. Relationality possesses sufficient effectiveness within itself to ensure the intelligibility of what exists. Therefore, consistency opposes essence as coherence opposes meaning or understanding opposes disjunction.
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.1_REF4_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN ARGUES THAT TRUE EXISTENCE IS NOT FOUND IN THE STATIC "COINCIDENCE" OF BEING AND THINKING ESTABLISHED BY GREEK PHILOSOPHY—WHICH TURNS THE SELF INTO A SCLEROTIC, SUBSTANTIALIST "BLOCK"—but rather in the continuous, vital process of "de-coincidence," where life sustains itself only by constantly detaching from fixed essences and present states to open up new, undefinable possibilities.
"III. To live is to de-coincide.
One might think that living is coinciding. In fact, one could argue that this is the definition of living: being in uninterrupted coincidence with the world and oneself. This coincidence constitutes my perception, both external and internal, through which I feel […]. In fact, we could say that this same coincidence, bringing together its two meanings as simultaneity and congruence, defines my present. It is this present of coincidence that makes me alive. However, this overlooks two main things. First, this present moment constantly slips away; therefore, such a coincidence is suspect in principle. Above all, however, the capacity that makes life possible is the opposite. It is precisely because we never cease to de-coincide—to continually undo the coincidence we have acquired—that life proceeds. In other words, life is alive because we continually undo the coincidence we have acquired. We must understand living as renewal. Living is not prolonging the previous state with the intention of perpetuating it because the latter becomes sclerotic, unravels, and tends toward death by enduring. Rather, it means detaching from the previous state, disassociating from its coherence, and not continuing it, but rather constantly bringing about a new one. In other words, it means continually detaching from the adequacy that led to the so-called "present" state. As a result of its exhaustion, life begins anew. "To live is to continuously de-coincide from the previous state in order to continue living"

VI. How Negativity Promotes Existence
The simplest thing would be to keep negativity at bay. If anything threatens or even destroys the self, it would always come from outside because I am intrinsically united with myself in my effort to subsist within myself. Otherwise, the self would contradict itself. I would call this negative force assailing me from outside evil without hesitation. Such forces include illness, death, suffering, and anything that jeopardizes my survival. This classic conception of confining and containing the negative in adversity is still that of Spinoza. Before introducing his central concept, "everything, as far as it is in itself, strives to persevere in its being" (the conatus), he presents the preliminary logical idea that "nothing can be destroyed except by an external cause" (Ethics, III, Prop. IV). However, he presents it without further argument as a self-evident proposition, even though this is the weakest point of his thinking and thus its limit. It stops there, settling on what it no longer questions or suspects might escape it. Spinoza remains dependent on a substantialist conception of the self, confining it to its compactness as a "thing," and positing it as an unbreakable block that cannot detach or decouple from itself. It can only affirm its essence as defined and not "deny" it. [...]

VIII. Tomb of Coincidence:
If consciousness emerges from decoincidence, then the mind strives for coincidence. Any aim,
can only aim at coincidence (the target), and the mind is essentially 'aim.' Greek thought established the condition of possibility for such coincidence from the outset. According to Parmenides, "it is the same to think and to be." In other words, being and thinking completely overlap, and neither overflows the other. Consequently, thought cannot stray outside Being (if it does, then it is no longer truly thinking), nor can Being offer thought anything unthinkable. The two coincide not as two separate terms but because they belong to the same conformity, folding one into the other. This coincidence was then fixed and labeled under the notion of "essence," which divides Being into identifiable objects that are conveniently manipulable by thought. However, by focusing on "essence" and making it the target of the mind, thought abandoned "existence." Existence cannot be contained in an essence. Through the possibilities it opens up, existence escapes all definition. It can only be observed in experience and cannot be entered into any reasoning or deduction of the mind. Therefore, existence cannot be manipulated in any way by thought. [...]"
(François Jullien, De-coincidence: Where Art and Existence Come From, 2017).

E3.9.1_REF5_PH David Altmejd, Untitled, Bronze

"Before Homer, the narrative was mythological; he innovtes giving constancy to the identity of his characters - the character becomes an essence instead of being permeable and the siege of all kinds of metamorphoses imposed by external forces. From then on, all facts are subordinated to the principle of causality. Homer formulates for the first time the awareness of a conflict between the relative powers of our being and those of nature - a desperate battle on our end - at least as long as we are fixed on preserving our being. The observation of the irremediable conflict between our mind and the malignancy of the world marks the emergence of an existential attitude. The Odyssey celebrates the virtues of intelligence while inevitably encountering its limits."
(Pierre Bergounioux in the program Les chemins de al philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on 08/27/2019)

Listen to the podcast in French: https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/lodyssee-dhomere-24-ulysse-le-raisonnable

E3.9.1_REF6_PH DEATH IS THE END OF "ALL POSSIBLE DISTANCE FROM SELF TO SELF.
"This fantasy of being someone else naturally ceases with death because it is I who dies, not my double. Pascal's famous words ('We die alone') aptly describe this unique existence in the face of death, even if that is not his main point. Death signifies the end of any possible spatial or temporal distance between oneself and oneself, and the urgency of coinciding with oneself. It is here that Rank's thesis finds profound meaning. Even more so does André Ruellan's proverb in his Manuel du savoir-mourir (Ed. P. Horay, p. 37): "Death is a rendezvous with oneself: one must be punctual at least once."
(Clément Rosset, The Real and Its Double)

E1.7.D.6.5.f_REF1_LIT.wE3.9.1.

"ULRICH REPLIED SHARPLY: « WHEN I THINK OF MY VERY YOUNG AGE, I WOULD SAY THAT THE INSIDE AND THE OUTSIDE WERE THEN HARDLY DISTINCT. When I crawled towards an object, the object flew towards me; when an event looked important to my eyes, I was not the only one to be moved by it: things themselves began to bubble. [...] I did not yet own myself; deep down, "I" was not yet, my personal states were not yet clearly separated from those of the world. It may seem strange, yet it is true to say that my feelings, my inclinations, that I myself was not yet entirely within myself. Even stranger, I could also say that I wasn't quite far from myself yet. Today indeed, as you think you are in full possession of yourself, if you wonder who you are, in the end, you will discover that you always see yourself from the outside, as an object. You will perceive that one occasion makes you sad and another furious, as your coat is sometimes wet, sometimes warm. By observing yourself, with all the attention possible, you will succeed, at most, in ending up behind yourself, never inside. Whatever you do, you stay outside of yourself, except precisely for the rare moments when the others would say about you that you are outside yourself. To compensate, we adults have been able to say "I am" on any occasion, if it pleases us. You see a car and in a way you see at the same time, like a shadow, the sentence: I see a car. You love, or you are sad, and you see that you are. Strictly speaking, however, neither the car, nor your sadness, nor your love, nor you are entirely there. Nothing is there entirely like it was in childhood. Everything you touch, as soon as you have succeeded in being a personality, freezes even within your deep intimacy. All that remains is a thin thread of self-awareness and troubled self-esteem, enveloped in an entirely exterior life. So what is wrong? We have the feeling that something could still be fixed but we don't know what. In fact how can we say that a child has absolutely different experiences from those of an adult? I don't have a definitive answer to these questions, although one or two thoughts come to mind. But it's been a long time since I've responded in my own way: by losing all love for this way of being "someone" and for this kind of world. »"
(Robert Musil, The Man Without Qualities, Volume 1, 1930)

E3.9.1_REF7_TH A_Christo_Packaging on a pram_1962

"The adult´s feeling of Ego cannot have existed from the start as it is. It must have undergone an evolution, which cannot be conceptually proven but can be reconstituted with a certain probability. The infant does not yet separate his Ego from the outside world as the source of sensations which flow over him. He learns this little by little, being exposed to various incentives. He can only be most strongly impressed by the fact that some of the sources of arousal, in which he will later recognize parts of his body, may communicate sensations to him at any time, while others withdraw from him sometimes - which is the case for what he desires the most: the maternal breast - and can only be brought back by cries of protest. Thus, the Ego is first opposed to an « object », namely something which is « outside » and can only be forced to appear again by performing a particular action and not before. Another impulse which pushes the Ego to detach itself from the mass of sensations, therefore to recognize an « outside », an external world, is given by the frequent, multiple and inevitable sensations of pain and displeasure which the pleasure principle - in its absolute mode of ruling - enjoins to suppress and avoid. Thus arises the tendency to separate from the Ego all that can become a source of such unpleasantness, to reject it outwards, to constitute a pure Ego of pleasure, facing a foreign and threatening exterior."
(Sigmund Freud, Civilization and Its Discontents, 1930)

E3.9.1_REF8_LIT A_Hasui Kawase, Spring Moon at Ninomiya Beach_1931

WE TRY TO "TIE" THE "EVER-FRAYING TAPESTRY OF STORY-LINES" INTO A "PERMANENTLY VALID CONCLUSION."
"In literature, when a storyline involves victim and a persecutor, we call it a drama. In life, most acts of aggression or complaint (which are two sides of the same coin: the emotional currency of existential malcontentment), most tantrums thrown by otherwise reasonable adults, most blamethirsty fingers pointed at some impartial reality, involve the self-victimization of drama. People prone to drama have not only cast themselves as victims of a perpetrator in a plot, but have tacitly conceded that there is a plot, which presupposes a playwright — some external entity scripting the story in which they feel done unto. The person self-cast into a drama is resigned to being a character, insentient to Joan Didion’s fundamental law of having character: “Character — the willingness to accept responsibility for one’s own life — is the source from which self-respect springs.” Wherever there is drama, there is a deficiency of self-respect and too shallow a well of self-knowledge.

The ways in which we are all susceptible to drowning ourselves into drama, and what it takes to float free, is what Iris Murdoch (July 15, 1919–February 8, 1999) explores in her subtle, splendid 1978 novel The Sea, the Sea (public library) — the story of a talented but complacent playwright approaching the overlook of life, who is ultimately overcome by his tragic flaw: Despite his obsessive self-reflection (or perhaps precisely because of it), his egotism ultimately eclipses his creative spirit — that brightest and most generous part of us, the part rightly called our gift, the part that extends the outstretched hand of sympathy and wonder we call art and invites, in Iris Murdoch’s lovely phrase, “an occasion for unselfing.”

"We are such inward secret creatures, that inwardness the most amazing thing about us, even more amazing than our reason. But we cannot just walk into the cavern and look around. Most of what we think we know about our minds is pseudo-knowledge. We are all such shocking poseurs, so good at inflating the importance of what we think we value. The heroes at Troy fought for a phantom Helen… Vain wars for phantom goods… People lie so… though in a way, if there is art enough it doesn’t matter, since there is another kind of truth in the art."
(Iris Murdoch, The sea, The sea, 1978)

A subset of the illusion of choice is the illusion of closure — the alluring but ultimately vain idea that, as life lives itself through us in ways far beyond our control, in a complex and by definition ever-fraying tapestry of story-lines, we can tease out any one narrative thread neatly enough to tie it into a complete and permanently valid conclusion. Murdoch dispels the vanity"
(Maria Popova, The Marginalian)

"Loose ends can never be properly tied, one is always producing new ones. Time, like the sea, unties all knots. Judgements on people are never final, they emerge from summings up which at once suggest the need of a reconsideration. Human arrangements are nothing but loose ends and hazy reckoning, whatever art may otherwise pretend in order to console us."
(Iris Murdoch, The sea, The sea, 1978)

"But here is where we do have choice: In accepting a hazy and uncertain reality beyond our control, we can also refuse to resign ourselves to being victims of it — the sort of adaptation Octavia Butler held up as the highest measure of intelligence and integrity. We can recognize that life is much more interesting as a process of continual presence than as an acted drama; that the world is much more interesting as a shoreline than as a stage — for it is at the living shore that we witness, as Richard Feynman did, “ages upon ages” unfolding into the wonder of life; at the shore that we are humbled, as Rachel Carson was, by “our place in the stream of time and in the long rhythms of the sea… in which there is no finality, no ultimate and fixed reality”; at the shore that we finally accept the most elemental fact of our lives: There is no final act — only shoreless seeds and stardust."
(Maria Popova, The Marginalian)

Read it in The Marginalian: https://www.themarginalian.org/2022/06/23/iris-murdoch-the-sea-the-sea/

E3.9.1_REF8.1_LIT TO BE SELF-AWARE IS TO STEP OFF THE STAGE (STAND OUTSIDE THE STORY) AND ACCEPT THE "SHORELESS" FLUIDITY OF LIFE.
"Loose ends can never be properly tied, one is always producing new ones. Time, like the sea, unties all knots. Judgements on people are never final, they emerge from summings up which at once suggest the need of a reconsideration. Human arrangements are nothing but loose ends and hazy reckoning, whatever art may otherwise pretend in order to console us."
(Iris Murdoch, The Sea, the Sea, 1978)

"But here is where we do have choice: In accepting a hazy and uncertain reality beyond our control, we can also refuse to resign ourselves to being victims of it — the sort of adaptation Octavia Butler held up as the highest measure of intelligence and integrity. We can recognize that life is much more interesting as a process of continual presence than as an acted drama; that the world is much more interesting as a shoreline than as a stage — for it is at the living shore that we witness, as Richard Feynman did, “ages upon ages” unfolding into the wonder of life; at the shore that we are humbled, as Rachel Carson was, by “our place in the stream of time and in the long rhythms of the sea… in which there is no finality, no ultimate and fixed reality”; at the shore that we finally accept the most elemental fact of our lives: There is no final act — only shoreless seeds and stardust."
(Maria Popova's review of Iris Murdoch's The Sea, the Sea, 1978)

E3.9.2. The Ontology of "Standing Outside": It is not just a choice but a structural necessity of reality. Nothing exists "in itself"; everything exists as a relation, a difference, or a perspective. To exist is to stand outside (ex-sistere).

E3.9.2_REF1_PH  "A being who was truly, exclusively saturated in a present moment wouldn’t be able to observe anything at all. Observation, any observation, installs a minimal distance from what it observes, for the simple reason that for any observation to take place, one here and now must be related to another here and now, and that relation needs to be registered by some trace or connector between the two. [...] 

The blur of the instant of change that is a logical prerequisite for stitching together any two moments in space-time inextricably inheres in the very reality being observed. […]

In a deep sense, then, the laws of physics, the laws that describe how things behave, are really the laws of our observations of how things behave."
(William Egginton takes up in The Rigor of Angels: Borges, Heisenberg, Kant, and the Ultimate Nature of Reality, 2023)

E3.9.2_REF2_SOC A_Roelandt Savery_Paysage montagneux avec un dessinateur

"In a famous essay, Panofsky showed how the invention of linear perspective in the first half of the 15th century brought about a new relationship between the subject and the world, between the viewer's point of view and a space made systematic, where objects and the intervals between them are only proportional variations of a seamless continuum. [...]
Where Bruegel, by removing humans from the landscape, simply makes manifest the exteriority of the subject which gives meaning and coherence to an objective nature, Savery reintroduces the subject into the pictorial representation by representing the very act by which man creates a distinct space from the one in which he is located, which is itself distinct from the space offered to the viewer because the perspective view presented to the latter is not the same as that the cartoonist is drawing on the paper, shifted to the left of the drawing but located in the very axis of the valley. In this landscape, therefore, we have a doubled objectification of reality, a somewhat reflexive representation of the operation by which nature and the world are produced as autonomous objects by the grace of the human gaze on them. "
(Philippe Descola, Beyond nature and culture, 2005)
