E3.9.2_REF8_PH "EXISTENCE
is often synonymous with being. However, the etymology suggests a difference. To exist is to be born or found outside of something else. In other words, there is no absolute outside. It is to be in the world, in the universe, in space, and in time. For instance, we hesitate to say that mathematical beings exist. Even if God existed, he could not be said to exist in this sense, as Lagneau pointed out. "To exist is to depend," wrote Alain. "It is to be beaten by the external flow." If God existed, he would not be God since he would be part of the universe. Existence always presupposes existence outside itself and others. Its essence is not to have one. "The nature, even the inner nature, of all things is outside of them... Relationship is the law of existence"
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2001)

E3.9.3. The Strategy of De-coincidence: From Subject to Situation. It involves abandoning the "Subject/Will" model in favor of the "Situation/Potential" model, and cultivating a distance to liberate the self from its own heaviness.

E3.9.3_REF1_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN ARGUES THAT THE CONCEPT OF "PROPENSITY," ROOTED IN THE CHINESE TERM SHI, CHALLENGES THE WESTERN PHILOSOPHICAL RELIANCE ON EXTERNAL "CAUSALITY" AND "EXPLANATION" by revealing the fundamental inseparability of "being" and "becoming," wherein a thing's evolution is understood as inherently implied within its present configuration.

"Propensity vs. Causality"
1. In order to think things through, we had to separate being and becoming. Is 'we' only the Greeks? [...]
We live in a black hole, not so much in the passage from one to the other, but in the inseparability of the two, in the idea that things are constituted by what they evolve into. This can also be read in reverse: things evolve from what constitutes them.
 In the terms of yesteryear, the event is in the structure. Propensity seems to me to be the most appropriate term to address this lack, to designate this inseparability, and to define it. It comes closest to the unthinkable idea of how things are driven by what they "are" and "are" by what drives them.
 How disposition implies inclination, and how inclination itself constitutes disposition. How evolution is not only contained in the configuration but is one with it and merges into it. I have found some support for this idea in the ancient Chinese term shi. Though not highly conceptualized, shi has been adopted in many fields, including strategy, power theory, aesthetics, and philosophy of history and first philosophy. As I read through the texts, I was surprised to find that, for the same occurrence, it can be translated equally well by one term or the other: "situation" or "evolution"; "condition" or "course of events."
At what point do the two come together to the point of discovering that they are communicative and, one might say, equivalent? This is a question that can never be fully illuminated by intelligence. [...]
The interesting thing about the concept of propensity is that it takes us out of the realm of causality and "explanation," which have reigned supreme over European knowledge, and introduces us to constant implication. The Greeks thought in terms of "cause" and "principle," with "cause" as the first and "principle" (arche and aitia, the two opening terms of Aristotle's vocabulary).
 We enter the "thing," in other words, through the "cause," causa, which derives its truth from the thing. We "give an account" (the famous logon didonai of the Greeks) of whatever being there may be from outside itself, which is made clear, at least symbolically, by the "ex" in "explanation." 
"To know" is "to know the causes of things," rerum cognoscere causas, says Latin sententiously, in a formula that would dispel the mystery of the world by placing it under this explanatory regime. [...]"
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.3_REF2_LIT "AESTHETICS OF INDIFFERENCE [...]
The greatest mastery of oneself is indifference to oneself, considering one's soul and body to be the house and farm where Destiny wanted us to spend our lives.
Treat your own dreams and intimate desires haughtily, en grand seigneur, taking intimate care not to notice them. To have modesty towards oneself; to realize that in our presence we are not alone, that we are witnesses to ourselves, and that it is therefore important to act before ourselves as before a stranger, with a studied and serene exterior, indifferent because noble, and cold because indifferent." 
(Fernando Pessoa, The Book of Disquiet, 1914)

E3.9.3_REF3_LIT A_Maja Vukoje_Untitled & patata_2013

TURNING MY 'I' INTO THIN AIR
 “What I am going to confess to you now is really very curious; but I get the same pleasure from yielding up my egotism with people as I get from the malice with which I play the zany! Everybody I meet seems to want to assert their ego. « I! I! I! » they cry. No one seems to get the depraved pleasure I get from turning my « I » into thin air and helping my friend’s « I » to swell and swell till it’s a regular balloon.
My progress and track through Life can be easily followed by all the wonderful balloons that go sailing up to the sky; balloons that formerly were little miserable soap-bubbles! No one—not even my intimate friends—can possibly realize the shifts, the subterfuges, the evasions, the devices, the serpentine coilings, the mole-like burrowings, to which I am always resorting, purely and solely to ward off some hurt to their pride, some blow to their life-illusion, some loss to their self-esteem - and this with practically every person I meet! It is interesting that I should have thus been inspired to discover a way of « acquiring merit » unknown to any Christian cult. [...]
It really is curious why I should have come to have such a deep superstitious mania for trying to make every living entity I encounter think more highly of itself."
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934)

E3.9.3_REF4_PH SEE QUE POUVONS-NOUS SAVOIR DES AUTRES

”A DEFECT OF STANDPOINT, NOT OF VISION.—We always stand a few paces too near ourselves and a few paces too far from our neighbour. Hence we judge him too much in the lump, and ourselves too much by individual, occasional, insignificant features and circumstances.”
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, All Too Human II, Aphorism 387, 1878)

E3.9.3_REF5_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN ARGUES THAT EUROPEAN PHILOSOPHY, BY ESTABLISHING THE AUTONOMOUS CARTESIAN SUBJECT AS ITS ABSOLUTE STARTING POINT, EFFECTIVELY RENDERED THE CONCEPT OF "SITUATION" UNTHINKABLE, reducing the lived world to a passive "landscape" cut out by an observer's gaze rather than the immersive, relational reality found in Chinese thought.

"POTENTIAL OF SITUATION (VS SUBJECT INITIATIVE)
In retrospect, what ultimately shaped the destiny of European philosophy was its establishment of a subject-self as the starting point. This observation is unfortunately too banal to be reflected upon sufficiently: when I think, I begin by thinking not about the world or "things," as the Greeks did, but about the "I" that thinks. The subject posits itself as the primary and sufficient object, with the "world" coming only afterwards as a consequence. From this "I think," this insular cogito, whose first outlines were found in Augustine, Descartes was able to draw the thread from which the "land of truth" is in sight, as Hegel would say. The fact that the philosopher discovers his uncertain and unassured thinking by beginning with "doubt" is obviously secondary to that initial "I" on whose rock he was able to stand in order to begin. Or is this doubt itself essential since it is from this single "I" of "I doubt" that I discover I cannot doubt, however relentless my doubt may be? Through this hyperbolic doubt, which believed from the outset to embrace everything and from which we inevitably find the world in its place, what did Descartes unwittingly cut himself off from at the outset? He could later recover it, but only in dependence on this primary ego. He could no longer think of it as a whole, but only within the confines of the subject. What did Descartes definitively relegate to the realm of the unthinkable with this masterstroke?
The common response was to condemn him, arguing that he had cut himself off from the Other from the outset. This meant he missed the co-originality of the "You" and the "I," the "Other" and the "subject," and he isolated himself in his solipsism. While it's true that Descartes turned away from the Hebrew gaze from the beginning, is that all he lost by starting out that way? [...]
The subject or situation is where European thought diverged without realizing it. Returning somewhat repentantly to the decisive place of cogito, modern philosophy has been so attached to recalling and magnifying the great Other to atone for its fault. However, it has perhaps not paid sufficient attention to what was left out in a less obvious way because it did not know how to approach it. We have continued to think of the Subject to the detriment of the Other, which in Europe has remained nameless and faceless. We have only been able to conceive of what we call, at best, "situation" through an unsatisfactory and makeshift approach.
This contrast is illuminated when viewed through the lens of Chinese thought. Consider, for example, what we call "landscape," which is merely the natural and primary mode of the "situation." The concept emerged in Europe during the Renaissance when the concept of the subject began to take shape. Descartes then exploited this concept masterfully. The dictionary still defines "landscape" as "the part of the country that nature presents to an observer," who cuts it out from his perspective. The horizon changes according to his position. In other words, the subject stands before the landscape, outside of it, and remains autonomous. It does not compromise itself. In China, however, instead of "landscape," we say "mountain(s)-water(s)" or shan-shui. [...]"
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.3_REF5.1_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN CONTRASTS THE WESTERN AUTONOMOUS SUBJECT, WHO IMPOSES A STATIC PLAN UPON AN OBJECTIFIED WORLD, WITH THE CHINESE CONCEPT OF "SITUATION" (SHI), WHERE THE STRATEGIST IS IMMERSED IN A DYNAMIC FIELD OF TENSIONS and achieves victory not through willful forcing, but by discerning and exploiting inherent propensities to ensure the enemy is defeated before the battle even begins.
“Or, as they say in Chinese, "feng-jing": on the one hand, "wind," which constantly passes and animates but cannot be seen; and on the other hand, "light," which brings visibility and promotes vitality. By saying this, the Chinese language-thought always names a correlation of interacting factors forming a polarity. The "subject" is not absent but immersed in this correlation of factors, which forms a polarity. It is immediately part of this field of tension from which it cannot detach itself. In other words, the subject does not emerge in an autonomous posture, as exemplified by the self-sufficient "I think," nor does it project its point of view onto the world by unfolding it like a panorama from its vantage point. The subject does not position itself as an object ("thrown" in front of it, "ob" — "jet"), on which its gaze comes to rest, which it can then sovereignly "observe." The landscape is no longer approached by a subject, as established by the famous Cartesian beginning. Rather, it is conceived as an investment of capacities that are reciprocally at work because they are both opposed and complementary. Within this, the subject is involved. The situation will thus refer preliminarily to this network of unlimited implications within which each person originally grasps themselves. This configuration is shaped by various tensions, and one can only free themselves from it through abstraction.
We can develop this idea of the situation further by returning to the Chinese term I started with. This term, shi, is translated as both "condition" and "evolution" and undoes the opposition between the static and the dynamic.
Once the situation reveals itself not just as a framework or context but as an active potential, its relationship to a "subject" is reconfigured. The strategist will no longer be the one who draws up a plan according to his objectives and projects it onto the situation. He will no longer use his understanding to conceive of what ought to be, nor will he use his will to bring it into being. This "bringing into being" implies forcing "understanding" and "will," the two master faculties of the subject of classical GE in Europe. The true strategist discerns the favorable and "promising" factors within the situation, not as they would ideally reconfigure it in their mind. They continuously gain momentum without others even noticing and turn the situation to their advantage. [...]
"So, instead of making a plan, I sketch a diagram of the factors and vectors at play. The lesson is that before engaging in combat, I must tip the potential of the situation massively in my favor. That way, when I finally engage, I have already won and the enemy is already defeated" (François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015).
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.3_REF5.2_PH IN THE CHINESE CONCEPT OF SHI (SITUATION AS POTENTIAL), THE SUBJECT IS NOT AN AUTONOMOUS AGENT OF WILL BUT AS AN ADAPTIVE PARTICIPANT IN A TRANSFORMATIVE FIELD OF INTERACTION.
"Now, 'situation,' if I refer back to the European lexicon, refers to 'the set of circumstances in which one finds oneself,' as if that were sufficient. However, the plural form of 'circumstance' indefinitely fragments the situation, contrasting and juxtaposing it in spatial, temporal, and aspectual ways. Circumstance is what "stands" "around." But around what, if not the perspective of a sovereign subject that predominates from the outset? Far from being neutral, "circumstance" is a term that wrongly stabilizes and marginalizes. The subject represents itself as an island against which the shifting tide of circumstances crashes. Circumstances are therefore inevitably a source of "friction," as Clausewitz observed, since they arise unexpectedly in relation to the plan drawn up in advance, causing the latter to gradually fall short and stumble. However, viewing the situation as potential takes the opposite view of this negativity, turning it around so that, instead of adventitious circumstances standing as obstacles causing the model to slip, the evolution and dynamic renewal of the situation (what the term "shi" most strongly expresses) are what I can draw on to tilt the situation to my advantage. With my mind unencumbered by projections, I can gradually take advantage of the situation.
The consequences of this on an operational level and in terms of management are easy to deduce. From then on, there will be no need to worry about "chance" or "luck," the gods, destiny, or the fates. There will be no need to rely on a "stroke of genius" either. This is the other big hole in our rationality when we abandon all our pre-established plans and react instinctively to whatever circumstances arise. Victory is always the result of the potential of the situation at hand. As Sunzi succinctly puts it, in war, it "does not deviate." 
This means that the battle is won before it even begins, while the defeated troops are those who "seek victory only at the moment of combat," hoping for last-minute success. From this, we can deduce that a truly good strategist is one whose strategy goes unnoticed and therefore does not receive praise. They are so adept at identifying favorable factors in advance and developing the situation's potential to their advantage that when they achieve victory, everyone considers their success "easy" and undeserved because it seems to flow naturally from the situation and was a foregone conclusion. This "without merit" is the great merit, enough to leave the subject of glory disappointed. Understandably, China has not conceived of an epic. [...]
I am always involved in a field of tension between various factors and vectors, which, because of their correlation, are necessarily in a state of transformation. Within this field, I am continually interacting with these factors, and this interaction constitutes "me." The autonomy or independence of the subject is no longer absolute, but rather, it has scope as a margin of maneuver according to which one adaptively manages one's conduct. Thus, one does not radically detach a "pure" "intention" that abruptly transcends everything given; rather, one's "good" or "bad" will is in play. [...].
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.3_REF5.3_PH “SUBJECT/SITUATION: A FORK IN THE ROAD OF THOUGHT [...]
Chinese language and thought provide no similar history of the advent of the subject. [...]
Hence, the ethical aspiration is not "autonomy," "being one's own law," as in Stoicism, nor is it freedom, conceived in classical European thought as the primary property of a subject. Rather, it is the opposite: not strictly its contrary, but its contradiction, expressed by availability. The essence of freedom is transcending any situation, whereas availability is openness to a situation without projecting anything onto it that would hinder it, to the point that the consistency of a "subject" is undone. The key word in Confucius's teachings is, "When it is appropriate to take on a responsibility, he takes it on; when it is appropriate to leave it, he leaves it." Everything depends on the situation encountered. It is by conforming to the situation—that is, by departing from any position of principle toward it—that he promotes morality on every occasion. Wisdom is knowing how to be in tune. His ethics therefore do not proceed from rules but from continuous regulation. When it is said of the Master that he has no "idea" to put forward, no "necessity" to which he would hold in advance, no "position" to which he would adhere, or finally, no "self" in which he would become bogged down, this negative formulation suffices to indicate what an obstacle the establishment of a subject would constitute in the face of a changing situation. However, when all these markers or expected reference points underpinning an identity are removed, morality consists of responding to all the possibilities of the situation without missing any. In other words, it means embracing its entirety so as not to sink into "error" (the obsession of philosophy) or partiality.
I will translate these phrases precisely because of their resistance to translation, as is said about some ancient thinkers who "got wind of the Tao." They are at the limit of what can be translated, not because their ideas are obscure, nor because their meaning is unclear, but because they simply do not fit. Rather, they systematically undermine the possibility of doing so, as seen in Zhuangzi, chapter 33, about Shen Dao and his associates.

Impartial and non-partisan, 
easygoing and without anything special, 
decisive without [anything] directing, 
tend toward things without duality, 
do not attach yourself to cogitating, 
do not strive to know, 
select nothing from things, 
always go with them...

Without a stated grammatical subject and therefore without a recognized attributive function, such formulaic expressions undo the anchoring in the self. They dissipate its consistency and, initially, its relevance. Through continuous slippage, they say everything: the non-rupture with the world, the non-retrenchment in thought, the non-choice among possibilities, and the non-withdrawal from the compossible. [...]
The principle is to "hold all things equally." One cannot do what the other can, and vice versa: "Heaven can cover, but cannot carry; Earth can carry, but cannot cover; the vast Way can embrace, but cannot distinguish." By realizing that all things and beings have their intrinsic possibilities and impossibilities, we understand that "choosing" is flawed because it cannot "hold everything," and that "instructing does not ensure success," and the way is to "neglect nothing." This is why Shen Dao is said to "free himself from knowledge" and "renounce the self" (qu ji). He shows himself to be "fresh," "light," and "disposed" (available), like water or wind in relation to things (ling tai). In this way, he conforms to the regulatory coherence of the Way. Always "changing," he assumes no responsibility. He mocks the so-called Sages and lets himself go with the flow, unencumbered and without imposing morality on himself. He embraces diverse situations "in their twists and turns," eliminating disjunctions. In this way, he can survive and avoid embarrassment. "Pushed, it moves forward," "pulled, it follows"; "like the wind, it twirls"; "like a feather, it spins." Holding itself in "completeness," it is "without rejection" or regret. It knows no harm in "establishing a self." In short, it floats without anchoring itself in the situation. Could we go further in terms of non-construction, non-imposition, and the unraveling of the image and sentence in the non-constitution of a subject?"
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015)


E3.9.3_REF5.4_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN ARGUES THAT EUROPEAN PHILOSOPHY HAS HISTORICALLY SUBORDINATED THE CONCEPT OF "SITUATION" TO THE DOMINANCE OF THE "SUBJECT"—linguistically evidenced by the term "circumstance" (what stands around a center).
“If we consider "subject" and "situation" as two competing concepts, as I am inclined to do here, then we must understand why the concept of "situation" has remained underdeveloped in Europe under the ascendant reign of "subject": why it has not become autonomous but remains dependent on "subject," which keeps it under its tutelage or within its sphere of influence. Let us pause on the term "circumstance," as the dictionary defines "situation" as a "set of circumstances": something that "stands around" or "surrounds," according to the composition of this word. This is a European term: peri-stasis in Greek and Um-stand in German. But what does it stand around if not the perspective that a subject projects onto the world, around which the situation is envisaged? [...]
It must rigorously 'circumscribe' itself in order to possess itself, and it measures and tests itself against it. Has Stoicism not simply exploited this image, which is much more than an image since it is the way language has constructed its morphology? Now, imagine a language such as Chinese, which has no morphology and therefore no declension. Its system of prepositions has remained rudimentary. What remains of this device, in which the subject imposes itself on the situation and dominates it on its own initiative, even when it must tragically succumb to it?
Has European thought ever emerged from this submission of the situation to the subject? Sartre was the first—or at least the best known—to propose a concept of situation. Far from undermining the cultural biases involved in this representation, he confirms and endorses them. [...]
In the short version of Existentialism Is a Humanism, Sartre considers the concept of situation immediately after reaffirming the "absolute truth" of the cogito as the only possible foundation of thought. This makes it clear that the logic is seamless and the thought continuous.”
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.3_REF6_PH NIETZSCHE WARNS THAT WITHOUT ARTISTIC DISTANCE, WE WOULD BE "NOTHING BUT FOREGROUND", TRAPPED IN THE "SPELL OF THAT PERSPECTIVE WHICH MAKES WHAT IS CLOSEST... APPEAR AS IF IT WERE VAST."
"What should win our gratitude.- Only artists. and especially those of the theater, have given men eyes and ears to see and hear with some pleasure what each man is himself, experiences himself, desires himself; only they have taught us to esteem the hero that is concealed in everyday characters; only they have taught us the art of viewing ourselves as heroes-from a distance and. as it were. simplified and transfigured-the art of staging and watching ourselves. Only in this way can we deal
with some base details in ourselves. Without this art we would be nothing but foreground and live entirely in the spell of that
perspective which makes what is closest at hand and most vulgar appear as if it were vast. and reality itself.
Perhaps one should concede a similar merit to the religion that made men see the sinfulness of every single individual
through a magnifying glass, turning the sinner into a great, immortal criminal. By surrounding him with eternal perspectives.
it taught man to see himself from a distance and as something past and whole."
(Friedrich Nietzsche, The Gay Science, Aphorism 78, 1882)

E3.9.3_REF7_PH A_Pieter Schoolwerth_Diamond Residence (Rigged n3)_2021_(1) ; A_Pieter Schoolwerth_Diamond Residence (Rigged n3)_2021_(2) ; A_Pieter Schoolwerth_No Body Get a Head_1991–2020 /Pieter Schoolwerth/

TO EXIST IS TO STAND OUTSIDE THE "ADEQUACY-ADAPTATION" THAT CLOGS LIFE.
"De-coincidence" is a concept that is both genetic and logical. It tells us what kind of break in order (in adequacy) is at stake in the mode of self-deployment. It takes us back to the beginnings of separation while helping us understand the internal reason for this deviation from the self. From this deviation, a new possible "self" emerges. It is from within adequacy itself that a disengagement is introduced. By stabilizing and sterilizing itself, or by adapting and deactivating itself, a normality is undone, reopening the possibility of a future.

In this sense, de-coincidence is a concept that not only expresses the vocation of art, but also—and above all—of existence. To de-coincide is to step outside the adequacy of a 'self,' its adaptation to a world, and to do so alone. That is what it means to truly exist. If "to exist" literally means "to stand outside" (from the theological Latin exsistere), then it means standing outside the adequacy-adaptation, which, when filled, becomes obstructed and no longer allows things to happen or be invented. While the thought of Being aims at immobilization in the stability of essence and the identical and the thought of life risks limiting itself to metabolic renewal alone, the thought of existence highlights the de-coincidence that fractures the self's adequacy with itself and its adaptation to itself. This brings the self out of the paralysis of such a "self," reopens an inner disengagement rather than a dissociation, and liberates initiative once again. Life promotes itself to existence, and becomes alive and qualifies itself by de-coinciding with itself, opening a breach in its acquired normality (its accepted functionality), and daring to "deviate." In short, it undoes any possibility of essence. [...]

Practicing decoincidence (of life, customs, and thought) is not aimed at demonstrating independence or shocking others. Rather, it is the only way to achieve existence. If we do not separate ourselves from the "environment," the group, and the herd—including the solidarity and conviviality that are theirs, such as Sunday dinner—and if we do not disassociate from the implicit agreement secreted there, then we will have renounced this requirement. [...]
Therefore, freedom unfolds through decoincidence. If we no longer believe that freedom is given to us from the outset by a metaphysical condition—that is, if we no longer want to remain stuck in the classic, disappointing contradiction of natural necessity (observed) versus the freedom of the spirit (postulated)—we will understand that effective freedom can only be achieved by breaking free from our adaptation to the world. In other words, effective freedom is achieved by opening up a margin of play and choice by shifting away from the world's normality and functionality and thus taking the initiative as the promoted "I" of the subject. In other words, only "man" can de-coincide from himself and his world. Only man can disadapt and introduce distance and perspective into the totalization and integration that make up the world. According to this formula, only man "exists."
(François Jullien, De-coincidence: Where Art and Existence Come From, 2017)

E3.9.3_REF7.1_PH TO LIVE, WE MUST BREAK THE “COINCIDENCE THAT WOULD BE A DEAD END," WHERE "NOTHING MORE CAN HAPPEN". 
"The fortuitous or the adapted?

We could stop there, at this fierce refusal—this refusal to coincide in terms of both existence and art, the two finding themselves united. This is in the name of a necessary fissure in the famous 'adequacy,' which no longer allows itself to be deceived by it—death. Contrary to what logic teaches, coincidence is actually a dead end or constitutes the 'aporia.'" [...]

Moreover, we can see that these two logics are rooted in two rival traditions. The first is the rational (Greek) tradition of knowledge based on the principle of the concordance of Being and thought. The second is the paradoxical (Christian) logic of existence, the "folly of the Cross" opposed to the sophia of the Greeks. John says, "Whoever wants to keep his life—whoever wants to coincide with it and adhere to it—by that very fact 'loses it.'" According to the first tradition, which is oriented towards Being, the ambition to establish the coincidence of adequacy is indeed to touch the absolute of Being. However, in the second tradition, which is oriented towards the Other, it is the preserved distance that, by letting go and remaining open to infinity, saves it from the appropriation of adequacy. The relevance of adequacy is simultaneously its limitation and what renders it sterile. [...]

In other words, that coincidence is not coincidental; it is evident in the fact that it has two divergent meanings. On the other hand, coincidence means a chance encounter, or "falling with the other at the same time" (co-in-cidere), which is impossible to justify. This "falling" is "pure coincidence," and the relationship is only one of simultaneity. As such, it cannot hide its contingency, which is indeed a matter of chance or "incident." Yet, it is through such coincidences that everything seems to have begun. One must be present at the same time as the other and be able to cross paths with the other for something to happen. Pollen grains must fall on stigmas; a sperm must meet an egg. Otherwise, nothing would have been created. A coincidence had to occur between them, one that could have easily not happened. In the infinite possibilities, this chance encounter had to occur for such an event to happen. "Strange coincidence!" we say, unable to add anything else. [...]
However, this is the opposite of the original historical meaning of "coincidence," which implies control. It is a geometric term that signifies when one line or surface is perfectly superimposed on another, just as paint completely covers a canvas. In other words, when coincidence means that one is perfectly adequate and adapted to the other. [...]
Coincidence no longer means the random intersection of two things, but rather, the complete conformity of one thing to another, such that nothing exceeds or is lacking, and nothing more can happen.

Therefore, we should pause to consider this internal dissidence within coincidence and its inability to align its two meanings, rather than glossing over it. I am always surprised by how easily dictionaries juxtapose the various meanings of the same word, which can diverge greatly, without further explanation. If these two meanings of coincidence—chance encounter or assured adequacy—do not coincide, and if they conceal a separation that is disturbing because it remains unspoken and unacknowledged, then shouldn't we see this as an inherent, irreconcilable flaw that cannot be bridged by thought? Perhaps this is the flaw that discourse constantly covers up without reducing it: the fundamental ambiguity that reason cannot dispel. In this ambiguity, our questioning about what constitutes "reality" is lost. Between chance encounters and justified adequacy, between randomness and adaptation, what bridge can we hope to build? Can we simply assume that if a chance encounter occurs, an adaptation must have preceded and prepared it? That in what appears to be chance, hidden adequacy was at work? However, the fortuitous nature of the encounter, "pure coincidence," cannot be eliminated.
Perhaps it is that, in the internal play of this concept, between chance encounters and perfect matches, we can identify the internal play of reality itself. But it did not allow itself to be approached any further. It remained unapproachable. This fundamental interplay, which thought cannot grasp, is not so much that of "chance" and "necessity," tuché and ananké, as the Greeks represented it, nor is it the inherent contradiction of life as we have made it. While chance and necessity are diametrically opposed, they still understand each other, leaving us with the hope that they will one day be reconciled. What resists thought is not so much the opposition, even if it appears to be a contradiction, since the negative still serves as a structure and can tip one into the other. Rather, as in this case, it is the discreet hiatus—the lack of agreement, or rather, the unattainable agreement, between these two meanings that are casually grouped under the same word, which can mean either one or the other—the non-connection, or non-relationship, between the two; the gap and discrepancy that remains between them, which cannot be resolved. Such a game (of reality) cannot be sufficiently understood or integrated, and the two divergent planes under the same word would only meet by pure coincidence—it is impossible to make them coincide any further. For convenience, we can call these two planes, on the one hand, the plane of the event (which happens by pure coincidence), and, on the other hand, the plane of the structural (where one can coincide completely with and be in line with the other). The gap between these two planes is the gap specific to reality. It is both that which causes reality to elude us and that which confronts us with the resistant effect of reality.
(François Jullien, De-coincidence, Where Art and Existence Come From, 2017)

E3.9.3_REF7.2_PH THE VOCATION OF THE SUBJECT IS TO "LEARN TO LEAVE ONESELF".
"Toward an Ethics of Decoincidence [...]
Detachment and disengagement are ethical strategies that call for decoincidence to free life from its confinement within its own world. These strategies wash away the positivity secreted by normality, shake life out of its inertia, and restore its momentum, however uncertain it may be.
Because the logic of decoincidence is at the root of ethics, it applies to our relationships with others and with ourselves. In one's relationship with oneself, decoupling from oneself means decoupling from the relationship of adequacy and adaptation that leads to the formation of a self that stabilizes into what is known as one's 'character.' However, the existing subject's vocation is not to be reduced to this "self," this "ethical" self (which is no longer ethical), or this ego that makes up the self. Rather, it is to regain its initiative vis-à-vis this self that is locked in its own world and no longer emerges from the rut of a self that has become "equal" to itself. We now know and can predict how this self will behave every time—alas!—as it is no longer capable of the slightest "protrusion" outside itself but is condemned to rumination. What we call moral "evil" or "vice" comes from the fact that the self no longer breaks away from what it has become through a fixed agreement, but instead locks itself in, becoming stuck to the point of being unable to attempt anything else. According to this "normalized" inner coincidence, which becomes blocked and can no longer be cracked, we can understand even the ordinary monstrosity of camp guards. On the other hand, learning to "leave" and dissociate from oneself, chipping away at the coincidence one maintains with oneself—in which one finds comfort and complacency—and maintaining oneself in continuous transformation is the condition—and I believe the only one—for promoting the subject. And this applies just as much to the life of thought: seeking to decouple oneself from the way one thinks, from the way one's thinking has become attuned to itself and remains assured of itself, is the only way to keep one's thinking active, alert, and not inert. [...]"
(François Jullien, De-coincidence, Where Art and Existence Come From, 2017)

E3.9.3_REF8_PH BARTHES DEFINES THE NEUTRAL AS THE "SUSPENSION OF ORDERS, LAWS... AND DOGMATISMS." It is the refusal to take a fixed position in the "story line" of conflict or contestation. It stands outside the binary of "for or against."

"THE DESIRE FOR NEUTRALITY [...]

Therefore, I say that the desire for neutrality is the desire for the following:

First, it is the suspension of orders, laws, threats, arrogance, terrorism, formal notices, demands, and attempts to seize.

Secondly, by extension, it is the rejection of pure discourse of protest. It is the suspension of narcissism, no longer being afraid of images, and dissolving one's own image, which borders on negative mystical discourse or Zen or Tao.

Neutrality continually presents a paradox as a desire: as an object, it is the suspension of violence; as a desire, it is violence. Throughout this course, it will be necessary to understand that there is a violence of the neutral, but this violence is inexpressible. There is also a passion of the neutral, but this passion is not a desire to grasp. I sometimes recognize this passion in myself in the calm with which I welcome the spectacle of "desires to grasp" and dogmatisms. However, this is discontinuous and erratic, as desire always is. It is not a matter of wisdom but of desire."
(Roland Barthes, The Neutral, Lectures at the Collège de France, 1977-1978)

E3.9.3_REF9_PH "REFLECTION
In the broad sense, it is a particular effort of thought. In the narrow sense, it is a return of thought to itself, which then takes itself as its object. Along with sensation, this latter movement is one of the two sources of our ideas, according to Locke. Without it, we would have no idea of "what we call perceiving, thinking, doubting, believing, or the idea of 'what we call perceiving, thinking, doubting, believing, our soul'" (Essay, II, 1, S 4). Reflection is therefore an intellectual and deliberate kind of inner sense: it is the knowledge the soul has of its various operations through which the understanding forms ideas" (ibid.). This movement is necessary but cannot exhaust the field of thought on its own. Marcel Conche advises in Présence de la nature, II, that it is better to philosophize in the manner of the Greeks than to shut oneself up in reflexivity, as Descartes and Husserl did. It is better to think about reality (to reflect, in the broad sense) than to watch oneself think (to shut oneself up in reflexivity, in the narrow sense). The self is certainly part of reality, so thinking about the world also means thinking about oneself. However, it is only a tiny part of it. Thinking about oneself has never been sufficient for thinking about what is or what it means to be alive. Logic and neurobiology teach us more about thought than reflection does. As Alain says in Cahiers de Lorient, I, p. 72, it is better to think about thought than to think about oneself. It is better to reflect (in the broad sense) on what we know or believe we know than to settle into reflection (in the narrow sense). "Thought," Alain said, "must have no other home than the entire universe; only there is it free and true. Outside oneself! Outward!" (Ibid.). Reflection leads to everything, provided one emerges from it."
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2001)

E3.9.4. Once the "story line" is dropped and the "self" de-coincides, what remains? A fluid, "conniving" relationship with the world in the flow of time.

E1.6.D.3.7.a.iii.1_REF1_PH.wE3.9.4.

"CONNIVANCE (VS. KNOWLEDGE) 1. In the face of knowledge and its hegemony over European culture, how can we name its other, which it has overshadowed and not taken into account? I will choose to call this other relationship to the world that knowledge has tended to bury, but has not been able to abolish, and whose "coherence" I must recall in the face of this established domination of knowledge. For the fact that knowledge, in its development into speculative knowledge, that is, knowledge for knowledge's sake, has detached itself, through science, from the need to adapt itself to the world from which it emerged, and has taken itself as an end in itself, has detached itself from the vital, now gives us this strictly correlative task: that of returning to that relationship which has been buried by reason and which, operating therefore in the shadows, nevertheless keeps us in a tacit understanding with things - but an "understanding" which we do not reflect upon. This "understanding" therefore remains implicit, beneath the relational work that reason itself performs, and which we can only illuminate by making reason retrace its steps, go back in its history, explore what it has strayed from: not what it heroically fought against (obscurantism versus enlightenment), but what it had to leave aside in its legitimate and powerful struggle to assert itself - and which now lies dormant, thus escaping our understanding. If, as we know, the opposite of knowledge is ignorance, its opposite is the collusion that is established with it on an equal footing, while at the same time turning their backs on each other. Connivere, as the Latin says: to get along "by winking".
For knowledge isolates a "nature" and turns it into an "object," methodically organizes its course, develops tools of abstraction and constructs mediations, renders spaces indifferent and projects an equal and planned time; it develops an argued discourse that promotes the conditions of science as well as politics. But is that all there is to knowledge? Doesn't life presuppose a mode of intelligence or, better, of "understanding" that, woven over the course of days, and even without us thinking about it, without us thinking about thinking about it, is held in adherence, instead of being distanced and edified in thought? For is knowledge not only the illuminated side, which is only made possible by its insidious reverse, to which it refers, but also by forgetting itself? Knowledge that is shadowy, that remains integrated in an environment, that does not abstract itself from a landscape, that does not extract itself from a conditioning, that does not separate theory from practice, that does not detach an "I" from the world: it remains below any possible exposure, above any explicitness, and above all, it does not question. 
Complicity is that knowledge that has not broken its attachment. [...]"
(François Jullien, Chinese thought vs philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.13.a.


E1.6.D.3.7.a.iii.1_REF2_PH 

FRANÇOIS JULLIEN EXPLORES THE NOTION OF LANDSCAPE AS A RELATIONSHIP OF CONNIVANCE WITH THE WORLD, WHERE OBJECTIVE OBSERVATION IS TRANSFORMED INTO A TACTILE UNDERSTANDING. Criticizing European Romanticism for its tendency to project subjectivity onto landscape, he proposes an approach in which landscape becomes a partner that awakens an affective and spiritual dimension. 
"There is 'landscape' when my cognitive capacity switches and is reversed into collusion; when the relationship of objectification, and above all of observation, that I maintain with the world is transformed into tacit understanding and communication: from knowing that I was vis-à-vis the 'country' (the knowledge of geography), I become collusive again in a landscape. Not, strictly speaking, that I "personify" elements of the landscape, or that I "project" myself into them, or that I lend my subjectivity to things, or that I animate the inanimate, as has been said and rehashed, as if to compensate for the rationalism of knowledge, the ordinary romanticism, the chatty romanticism, in Europe - all operations in which the subject still wants to be the master and which are only devices. But this transformation does take place: when the land becomes landscape, what I perceive in it is no longer alien to me, but beckons to me, "speaks to me," "touches me," as we say colloquially (but can we ever escape this colloquialism?). That is to say, as the Chinese literati painters so beautifully expressed it, that the perceptive becomes affective at the same time, or that I perceive then from within as from outside of myself; through this, that a dimension of infinity or of spirit then emanates and is released from the physicality of things, with which my mind "understands" (softly, as the Chinese strategically put it). The land becomes a landscape - do we know, or rather "do we really know", but the Chinese language of thought has proved particularly gifted in expressing this implicit, when this "corner of the land" establishes itself as a partner, the tension that it promotes integrates me and is shared: there is "landscape", in other words, when a relationship is established (re-established) with this "country" that makes me go back upstream - finds its source on this side of that which is established by knowing reason; when the place suddenly becomes a "link"; and when this "corner of the country" thus "becomes the world": when I connect with it as with that which makes the world. [...]
At the same time, there is no fusional shift, no compensatory slide into the ecstatic or the mystical, as so much bad lyricism in Europe has advocated, so I do not indulge in any irrationalism (this is an upstream of reason and not its polemical denial), where the famous "communion" or symbiosis with nature, which comes as reparation for knowing reason, at least leaves ambiguity hanging in the air. [...Let it not be a subject self that projects itself into the landscape, making it the confidant of its emotion (which I have just stigmatized as the banal attitude of bad romanticism), but let it be the landscape that, by setting itself apart, draws me into its field of tension, in resonance with its variation (the landscape is like a bow "that has played on my soul," said Stendhal), brings back - from beneath the mind that asserts itself as autonomous (the insular subject that knows and wants) - my more original belonging to the world: a world that has emerged from its indifference and to which my vitality is bound. [...]

"To tell the truth, we live in the alternation of these two registers, or rather of these two regimes [...] I press more on one key or the other of my inner keyboard; I constantly move the cursor between one pole and the other. Because we live by moving discreetly from one to the other, and in this agility, by advancing (asserting) the knowing, or by retreating, conniving, regressing from our autarkic self. Now, the ideal for the knowing subject is modeling. But how can we go one step further and name the other side of this, the side that has remained in the shadows, which means that the capacity is acquired and grows, but silently, without even paying attention to this continuous process, until we finally see the result?"
(François Jullien, Chinese thought vs philosophy, 2015)



E3.9.4_REF1_SOC A_Jennifer Tee_Subtle planes~Spirit Matter_2013_(1) ; A_Jennifer Tee_Subtle planes~Spirit Matter_2013_(2) /Jennifer Tee/

"Dualism is not bad in itself and it is ingenuous to stigmatize it for purely moral reasons, in the manner of the ecocentric philosophies of the environment, or to make it bear the responsibility for all the evils of the modern era, from colonial expansion to the destruction of non-renewable resources, or the reification of sexual identities or class distinctions. We owe at least to dualism, with the bet that nature is subject to its own laws, a formidable stimulation for the development of science. We also owe to dualism, and its belief that humanity is gradually becoming more civilized by disciplining its instincts and by controlling nature, some of the improvements, particularly political ones, which the aspiration to progress were able to generate. Anthropology is the daughter of this movement, of scientific thought and of the faith in evolution, and there is no need to be ashamed of the circumstances of its birth nor to condemn it to disappearance in order to atone for its youthful sins. But its role does not accomodate well with this heritage; we need to understand how peoples who do not share our cosmology have been able to invent for themselves realities distinct from ours, thereby testifying to a creativity that cannot be gauged against our own achievements. However, this is what anthropology cannot do when it takes for granted as a universal matter of fact of experience our own reality, our ways of establishing discontinuities in the world and of detecting relationships we believe are constant, our ways of distributing entities and phenomena, processes and modes of action, in categories that we believe are predetermined by the texture and structure of things."
(Philippe Descola, Beyond nature and culture, 2005)

E3.9.4_REF1.1_SOC A_Luca Izzo_Desformes 06

"The desocialization induced by the colonial process engendered dramatic upheavals, which the missionaries aimed to correct by dispensing education. It is by this means, in fact, that a consciousness of individuality was born and inscribed in an autonomous body. Old Boesoou leaves little doubt about this in his response to Leenhardt, who inquired about the effects produced by schooling:
- In short, is it the notion of spirit that we have brought to your thinking?
And he objected:
- The spirit? Bah! You did not bring us the spirit. We already knew of the existence of the spirit. We were proceeding according to the spirit. What you have given us is the body."
(Philippe Descola, Beyond nature and culture, 2005)

E2.2.A.3.wE3.9.4.

“TIME IS NOT A RIVER BUT A SEA

Time is not a river but a sea,
Holding all that was and is to be.
It is the mind that moves across the story,
Racing with the wind upon its glory,
Tracing through the will one's destiny.
Years shimmer in their drowned eternity.

The problem is, of course, identity,
Having the impression we are free.
Remember that the Earth does not seem round.
Each eve the sun sinks slowly towards the ground,
Even as we choose to take our tea.”
(Nicholas Gordon, Philosophical poems, 2002)

E3.9.4_REF2_LIT P_Shomei Tomatsu_Sumie, a multiracial girl_1958

SEGALEN STRIPS "EXOTICISM" OF ITS COLONIAL/TOURISTIC CLICHÉS (PALM TREES, CAMELS) TO DEFINE IT PURELY AS "THE NOTION OF THE DIFFERENT; THE PERCEPTION OF THE DIVERSE; THE KNOWLEDGE THAT SOMETHING IS NOT ONESELF."
"First and foremost, clear the ground. Throw overboard everything that is misused and rancid in the word ‘exoticism’. Strip it of all its trappings: palm trees and camels; colonial helmets; black skin and yellow sun; and at the same time get rid of all those who used them with silly loquacity. [...]
Then strip the word exoticism of its solely tropical, solely geographical meaning. Exoticism is not only given in space, but also in time.
And very quickly arrive at a definition, at establishing the sensation of exoticism, which is nothing other than the notion of difference; the perception of diversity; the knowledge that something is not oneself; and the power of exoticism, which is nothing other than the power to conceive of the other.
Having arrived at this gradual narrowing of a notion so vast in appearance that it seemed, at first, to encompass the World and the Worlds; having stripped it of the countless dross, smudges, stains, ferments, and molds that such long use—so many mouths, so many prostitute and tourist hands—had left on it; finally possessing this concept in the form of a clear and vivid idea, let us allow it to take shape again, and like a seed, this time pure, to develop freely, joyfully, without hindrance but without overload; to seize all the sensory and intelligible riches it encounters in its expansion and, swelling with everything, in turn to embellish and revive everything.
This mode of thinking is none other than the infinitely free mode of Hindu thought. Hindus think, and such a particular principle immediately tends to become universal (see Oldenberg, Le Bouddha).
However, there are born travelers in this world—exotics. They recognize the unforgettable jolts given by moments such as I have described beneath the cold or dry betrayal of phrases and words. They will acknowledge that, without contravening the two formidable laws mentioned, this highlights the essence of the interplay of these laws: the subject's intoxication when conceiving of its object, knowing itself to be different, and feeling the diverse. No doubt, nothing more will be invented. But I hope that for them, the flavor will be greater and more tenacious, and the freedom of its play will be excessive. It is for them that I write."
(Victor Segalen, Essay on Exoticism, an Aesthetic of Diversity, 1908)

E3.9.4_REF2.1_LIT "LET US NOT FLATTER OURSELVES FOR ASSIMILATING CUSTOMS, RACES, NATIONS... BUT ON THE CONTRARY REJOICE IN NEVER BEING ABLE TO."
"Individualism

Only those with a strong sense of self can perceive differences. According to the law that every thinking subject presupposes an object, the notion of difference implies an individual starting point. Those who know themselves and what they are not will fully enjoy this wonderful sensation.

Exoticism is not the kaleidoscopic state of the tourist or the mediocre spectator but rather the lively and curious reaction to the clash of a strong individuality against an objectivity that it perceives and savors. The sensations of exoticism and individualism are complementary.

Exoticism is not an adaptation. It is not the perfect understanding of something outside of oneself that one embraces within oneself. Rather, it is the acute and immediate perception of eternal incomprehensibility.

Therefore, let us start from this admission of impenetrability. Rather than flattering ourselves by thinking we can assimilate customs, races, and nations, let us rejoice in the fact that we can never do so. This allows us to enjoy the enduring pleasure of feeling the diverse. This raises the question: Does increasing our ability to perceive diversity diminish or enrich our personality? Does it steal something from us, or does it make us more abundant? There is no doubt that it enriches it abundantly with the whole universe [...]".
(Victor Segalen, Essay on Exoticism, an Aesthetic of Diversity, 1908)

E3.9.4_REF2.2_LIT SEGALEN DEFINES THE "POWER OF DIVERSITY" AS A DYNAMIC PROCESS WHERE THE SUBJECT MUST ACTIVELY ENGAGE ("EMBRACE AND MERGE") TO CREATE THE SENSATION OF DIFFERENCE. It is not a static binary (Black vs. White), but a lived relation or gap.
"Essential Exoticism"

[...] Verbally, if we are to believe the words, there is an exacerbation of exoticism in the antinomies. Not only do these notions differ, they are also directly opposed to each other! If flavor grows with difference, then what could be more delicious than the clash of irreconcilable contrasts?
Yet, when you consider it, there is such harshness and dryness: White and black. Black and white.
Analysis: The power of diversity contains two phases, one of which is reducible. One of the divergent elements is us. In the other phase, we observe a difference between two parts of the object. This second phase must be reduced to the first if we want to turn it into a sensation of exoticism. Then, the subject embraces and merges with one part of the object for a time, and diversity emerges between it and the other part. Otherwise, there is no exoticism. Realizing that the object is not an object does not cause the emergence of the diverse. Rather, it is simply an intellectual operation of a mathematical nature—the simplest one, in fact: addition. In library science, it is cataloging. This is the beginning of the exact sciences. It is not exoticism.
However, in the presence of antinomies and categorical oppositions, the mind must take sides. Can it do so? Good or evil? Continuous or discontinuous? And so many others. Sympathy is difficult. Drawing lots is unconvincing. The spectator will have no inclination toward either. Exoticism will not arise. [...]

L'Expertise et la collection. Réunir des objets qui parfois n'ont qu'une qualité qui est de différer légèrement entre eux est encore un hommage, - grossier parfois - rendu à la Différence. Le collectionneur croit vulgairement, ou est cru, réunir un lot de « semblables » ou analogues pièces... Quelle erreur ! C'est dans la Différence que gît tout l'intérêt. Plus la Différence est fine, indiscernable, plus s'éveille et s'aiguise le sens du Divers. Rouge et vert? Que non pas ! Rouge et rougeâtre, puis rouge et rouge avec un divisionnisme sans limite. L'agglomération des objets facilite le jugement qui les « discerne », le « discernement ». Toute série, toute gradation, toute comparaison engendre la variété, la diversité. Séparés, les objets semblaient vaguement semblables, homogènes; réunis, ils s'opposent ou du moins « existent » avec d'autant plus de force que la matière, plus riche et plus souple, a davantage de moyens et de modalités nuancées. [...]"
(Victor Segalen, Essay on Exoticism, an Aesthetic of Diversity, 1908)

E3.9.4_REF3_PH TO LIVE IS TO REFUSE THE "SLACK TIDE" OF A FIXED IDENTITY.
"SOARING (VS STASIS) [...]
This is why it is legitimate to consider the rigor of non-contradiction and the depth of ambiguity. This allows us to establish the vocation of science and determine being. It also allows us to probe what logically escapes science in living.
In other words, we must separate the logic of living from the logic of 'being' or ontology. I see no way to grasp the concept of life without first entertaining this suspicion and taking this requirement into account. One excludes the other, even if I think between the two. The thought of living and the question of being—that is, the consideration of growth, essence, or even non-coincidence and determination—are competitive with each other. The Greeks, when they thought under the hegemony of the "question of being"—which they did not always do, as Heraclitus and the Stoics show us—made us miss the other. If "being" is known by coincidence because it is stable, and its determinative character delivers it to the definition of science, then living can only be apprehended by de-coincidence. In order to think it, we must develop a new logic from scratch. [...] 

Living, in its momentum, is nothing other than de-coinciding with oneself, dispossessing oneself, or leaving oneself in order to become oneself. The essence of living in its intensity is that it cannot be confined to any property, or in other words, it is always transcending the "self." Coinciding with oneself is death. More bluntly, when we coincide completely with ourselves, we are dead. [...]"
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought in Relation to Philosophy, 2015)

E3.9.4_REF4_PO I_The mediating void

LAO-TZU HELPS US RESOLVE THE PARADOX OF A LIFE THAT RECONCILES BOTH ADHERENCE TO THE PARTICULARITY OF THE PRESENT MOMENT AND OPENNESS TO THE UNIVERSE.
"Those who observed the Way refused to be filled. Thus, never being filled, they could remain preserved and escape premature completion"
(Lao-tzu, chap. XV).

Through these quotations, we see that Lao-tzu was concerned with duration. For him, duration implies adherence to the Way. It means remaining in order to live, living without dying, and dying without decaying. If human life is a journey through time, it is important to incorporate what he calls the Return within that journey itself. The Return is not seen as a stage that comes only 'after'; it is simultaneous with the journey, a constituent element of time. How can one engage in the process of becoming and growth, which necessarily involves distancing and particularization, while simultaneously effecting the Return (to the Origin)? Through the Void," replies the Taoist. In the linear development of time, the void introduces the circular movement that connects the subject to the original space each time it intervenes. Thus, the Void, which resides within the Origin and at the heart of all things, once again proves to be the guarantor of life's proper functioning within the Time-Space framework. Since living time is an actualization of vital space, the void is a kind of regulator that transforms each stage of life into a space animated by vital breaths. This is an indispensable condition for achieving true fulfillment."
(François Cheng, Void and Fullness: The Pictorial Language of China, 1991)

“Lost within immensity
all presence is a pivot
Around which the universe
revolves, suddenly close and intimate”
(François Cheng, The Book of the Mediating Void)
